About This Course
The Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC), managed by The Cyber AB
(CyberAB.org; formerly the CMMC Accreditation Body or the CMMC-AB), is a program
through which an organization's cybersecurity program maturity is measured by their initial
and ongoing compliance with applicable cybersecurity practices, as well as their integration
of corresponding policies and plans into their overall business operations. Once rule-making
has concluded and CMMC 2.0 has been implemented, all organizations providing products
or services to the United States Department of Defense (DoD) must comply with the
requirements of their applicable CMMC Level.
This course prepares students for the Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) certification,
which authorizes the holder to use The Cyber AB Certified CMMC Professional logo, to
participate as an Assessment Team Member under the supervision of a Lead Assessor, and
to be listed in the CMMC Marketplace. The CCP certification is also prerequisite for the
Certified CMMC Assessor (CCA) certification.
IMPORTANT NOTE TO STUDENTS AND INSTRUCTORS REGARDING DELTA
MATERIALS
Logical Operations' CCP and CCA courseware is accredited as CMMC-AB Approved
Training Materials (CATM). The CATM curriculum aligns with the official CCP and CCA
exam blueprints and examinations currently available from the Cyber AB and its subsidiary,
the CMMC Assessors and Instructors Certification Organization (CAICO). Therefore, the
curriculum also aligns with official documents as they existed at the time the CAICO
developed the blueprints and exams.
Since the release of the blueprints, examinations, and accredited curriculum, the DoD has
issued the final CMMC program rule (32 CFR Part 170 – see https://
www.federalregister.gov/documents/2024/10/15/2024-22905/cybersecurity-
maturity-model-certification-cmmc-program). There are several differences, or deltas,
between the content of the final rule (and other associated documents) and the content of
the current examinations. As just one example, in the final rule, CCP now stands for
"CMMC Certified Professional." There are many more substantive changes.
The primary responsibility of approved CMMC publishing and training partners is to
prepare candidates for success on the CCP and CCA exams. Because the CATM curriculum
prepares candidates for the current versions of the examinations, the curriculum will remain
aligned with the official blueprints and examinations until such time as the CAICO updates
them, or provides additional guidance.
To support the ecosystem during the Delta period between publication of the final rule and
the CAICO's release of updated blueprints and examinations, the DoD and CAICO are
providing Delta training and an online Delta test for individuals who have already
successfully completed CCP or CCA certification exams. This material is available by login
at The Cyber AB website.
Important Delta Note

--- PAGE 10 ---
During the Delta period, a candidate's process for completing the certification exam requirements is:
1.
Take official CCP or CCA training from Approved Training Partners (ATPs) using official
CATM content.
2.
Pass the official examination.
3.
Review the Delta training materials by logging in to CyberAB.org and accessing your dashboard.
4.
Complete the online Delta test while logged in to CyberAB.org. This only needs to be completed
once.
Course Description
Target Student
This course is a prerequisite for the Certified CMMC Professional program, and it prepares students
for the Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) certification exam. Students might consider taking this
course to learn how to perform CMMC certification readiness checks within their own organization,
or as a consultant to other Organizations Seeking Certification (OSC). The CCP certification is also
a required step toward becoming a Certified CMMC Assessor (CCA), so students might take this
course to begin down the path toward CCA certification.
Course Prerequisites
To ensure your success in this course, you should have some foundational education or experience
in cybersecurity, such as:
•
College degree in a cyber or information technical field with 2+ years of experience; or
•
2+ years of equivalent experience (including military) in a cyber, information technology, or
assessment field.
The Cyber AB and DoD have established specific requirements for those who wish to pursue CCP
or CCA certification. Refer to The Cyber AB website (https://cyberab.org/CMMC-Ecosystem/
Ecosystem-roles/Assessors) for official requirements and process information.
Course Objectives
In this course, you will learn about the CMMC Model, framework, context, and application within
the DoD, as well as the expectations and requirements imposed upon organizations that do business
with the DoD. It will also help students to identify threats to cybersecurity and privacy within an
IoT ecosystem and implement appropriate countermeasures.
You will:
•
Identify the threats to the Defense Supply Chain and the established regulations and standards
for managing the risk.
•
Identify the sensitive information that needs to be protected within the Defense Supply Chain
and how to manage it.
•
Describe how the CMMC Model ensures compliance with federal acquisitions regulations.
•
Identify responsibilities of the Certified CMMC Professional, including appropriate ethical
behavior.
•
Establish the Certification and Assessment scope boundaries for evaluating the systems that
protect regulated information.
•
Prepare the OSC for an Assessment by evaluating readiness.
•
Use the CMMC Assessment Guides to determine and assess the Evidence for practices.
•
Implement and evaluate practices required to meet CMMC Level 1.
•
Identify the practices required to meet CMMC Level 2.
•
As a CCP, work through the CMMC Assessment process.

How To Use This Book
As You Learn
This book is divided into lessons and topics, covering a subject or a set of related subjects. In most
cases, lessons are arranged in order of increasing proficiency.
The results-oriented topics include relevant and supporting information you need to master the
content. Each topic has various types of activities designed to enable you to solidify your
understanding of the informational material presented in the course. Information is provided for
reference and reflection to facilitate understanding and practice.
Data files for various activities as well as other supporting files for the course are available by
download from the CHOICE Course screen. In addition to sample data for the course exercises, the
course files may contain media components to enhance your learning and additional reference
materials for use both during and after the course.
Checklists of procedures and guidelines can be used during class and as after-class references when
you're back on the job and need to refresh your understanding.
At the back of the book, you will find a glossary of the definitions of the terms and concepts used
throughout the course. You will also find an index to assist in locating information within the
instructional components of the book. In many electronic versions of the book, you can click links
on key words in the content to move to the associated glossary definition, and on page references in
the index to move to that term in the content. To return to the previous location in the document
after clicking a link, use the appropriate functionality in your PDF viewing software.

--- PAGE 13 ---
Managing Risk within the
Defense Supply Chain
Lesson Time: 3 hours
Lesson Introduction
As someone interested in becoming a Certified CMMC Professional (CCP), you are aware
that the threats to the Defense Supply Chain continue to increase, both in numbers and
complexity. This risk involves sensitive information not only in government systems, but
also in systems operated by companies that develop and deliver products, services, and
solutions to the federal government and throughout every link of the global supply chain. It
is essential for the Certified CMMC Professional to know how to identify and manage these
risks to sensitive information following the pertinent regulations and the documented
standards and best practices.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Identify threats to the Defense Supply Chain.
•
Identify regulations and standards that guide risk management for the Defense Supply
Chain.
1

--- PAGE 14 ---
TOPIC A
Identify Threats to the Defense Supply Chain
It is critical to identify and mitigate against potential threats to the Defense Supply Chain. In this
topic, you will identify why the federal government is changing its expectations on managing those
risks.
The Defense Industrial Base (DIB)
•
Includes:
•
Department of Defense (DoD) components.
•
More than 100,000 companies and subcontractors.
•
Companies providing incidental materials and services.
•
Government-owned facilities operated by the government or contractors.
•
Part of the Defense Supply Chain (DSC).
•
DSC extends beyond the DIB.
•
Examples: office equipment, food, janitor services
•
Vast and complex.
•
Spans the globe.
•
Down all the way to “Mom & Pop” small businesses.
•
Includes supporting IT companies.
•
Modern weapon systems require:
•
An army of defense contractors.
•
Years of effort to manage and field these systems.
Defense Industrial Base (DIB): The worldwide industrial complex that enables research and
development, as well as design, production, delivery, and maintenance of military weapons
systems, subsystems, and components or parts, to meet U.S. military requirements.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms, DIB Sector Specific Plan, DHS CISA
Figure 1-1: Thousands of companies throughout the world provide goods and services within the
vast and complex Defense Supply Chain.

Risks to the Defense Supply Chain
•
Adversaries’ motivations to obtain our technology:
•
Learn our capabilities to figure out how to defeat us.
•
Steal our technology to avoid costs of their own R&D efforts.
•
Minimize the time that the U.S. has military technological superiority.
•
Potentially easier to hack a defense contractor than the military itself.
•
Not all defense contractors have military grade cybersecurity technologies and processes.
•
F-35 blueprints exist in the DoD and the DIB.
•
Any given contractor might have only a portion, but that can still help an enemy if stolen.

Information Flow between Prime Contractors and
Subcontractors
•
Prime contractors receive contracts from the government.
•
Take specific portions of these contracts and award them to various
subcontractors via a teaming agreement.
•
Shared data might not be protected well and is therefore vulnerable.
•
For example, F-35 prime may share sensitive information to
subcontractor to build engines:
•
Performance characteristics.
•
How much thrust will the engine need to produce.
•
Integration details.
•
How will the engine connect to the airframe
and be controlled by the pilot.
•
Schedule information.
•
Highlights when the airplane will be operational.
•
All this data may be unclassified, but it:
•
Is sensitive in nature.
•
Is highly valuable to our adversaries.
•
Needs to be protected.

any information that

(as a government

Philosophical Change to Securing the Nation's Data
•
Increased expectations for accountability and integrity about cybersecurity.
•
Transparency between traditionally isolated entities.
•
This requires a shift:
•
From trusting contractor’s self-attestations and plans of action to fix gaps.
•
To requiring an independent, on-site validation of full compliance.
•
Changed expectations for IT teams.
•
Need to understand the information flow to know how to protect the information.
•
This also requires a shift:
•
From a “protect the system” mindset.
•
To a “protect the information” mindset.
•
DoD's philosophical changes led to the creation of the CMMC program.

of IT teams

The Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC)
Program
•
DoD initiative to verify defense contractors' cybersecurity preparedness and effectiveness.
•
Program to standardize cybersecurity implementation across the DIB.
•
Defines how a high performing, cybersecure organization acts.
•
Collects existing cybersecurity standards and best practices.
•
Requires a trained, independent assessment conducted on a defense contractor's cybersecurity posture.
•
Consists of levels designed to fit contractor's type of sensitive information.
•
Promotes cybersecurity maturity.
•
Provides assurance that the DIB can protect sensitive information within a complex supply chain:
•
Accounts for information flow down to subcontractors.

Lesson 1: Managing Risk within the Defense Supply Chain | Topic A
Cultural Change/Shift

--- PAGE 23 ---
ACTIVITY 1-1
Identifying Threats to the Defense Supply Chain
Scenario
You are working for an organization that has a contract with the DoD or desires to be a DoD
contractor. You need to better understand the threats to sensitive information and what happens
when a contractor fails to protect this information.
1.
Why is the Defense Supply Chain at risk?
A: Because the DIB is a complex system with Prime contractors and subcontractors dealing with
sensitive information. The more complex the system is, the more opportunities there are for an
adversary to hack into it to steal sensitive information.
2.
How does CMMC encourage a different way of thinking about cybersecurity
and protecting sensitive information within the Defense Supply Chain?
A: CMMC shifts the focus to protecting the information and not just the IT systems. Additionally,
CMMC uses third-party verification to formalize evaluation of how thoroughly the contractors
supplying the DoD make cybersecurity an essential part of their organizational culture.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 11
Lesson 1: Managing Risk within the Defense Supply Chain | Topic A
Activity

--- PAGE 24 ---
TOPIC B
Identify Regulatory Responses against Threats
Through the years, numerous regulations and standards have been established to safeguard
information in the Defense Supply Chain. In this topic, you will examine some of the regulations
and standards that are designed to secure the nation's defense data.
Intellectual Property
•
Federal contracting scenarios typically involve
various types and sources of IP.
•
Numerous regulations have been put into place
to protect this IP.
Intellectual Property (IP) : A work or invention, such as a design, process, computer program, or
trade secret developed through work of the mind or intellect, which may be protected through
legal mechanisms such as patent, copyright, and trademark, or by taking steps to keep the
information secret.
Figure 1-10: Many government contracts involve the handling of information that should be
protected, even though it is not considered classified information.
Notes

Intellectual Property

•
A company may employ different types of IP when
doing business with the government.
•
Internal IP
•
The company’s own methods, techniques, inventions.
•
External IP may come from:
•
Partners outside the government.
•
Generally protected by contracts between the
parties, such as license agreements, NDAs,
and other contract terms.
•
Federal government
•
Governed and protected through contract clauses
with the third-party agencies involved.
•
Commonly covers:
•
Federal Contract Information (FCI).
•
Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI).

Federal Acquisition Regulations (FAR)
•
Chapter 1 of Title 48 of the Code of Federal Regulations:
•
Also known as:
•
48 CFR.
•
Federal Acquisition Regulations (FAR).
•
Provides uniform policies and procedures that apply to all Executive Branch departments and agencies
regarding acquisitions.
•
As a Certified CMMC Professional, you must have some familiarity with FAR:
•
Documents rules that government contractors are subject to.
•
Takes priority over the DFARS and other supplements.
•
Provides a consistent set of baseline requirements that apply to all solicitations.
Acquisitions: The acquiring by contract with appropriated funds of supplies or services
(including construction) by and for the use of the Federal Government through purchase or
lease, whether the supplies or services are already in existence or must be created,
developed, demonstrated, and evaluated.
48 CFR
(A.K.A. the
“FAR”)
Source: 48 CFR § 2.101
Figure 1-13: FAR are regulations that apply to acquisitions involving any department of the
federal government, not just those for the Department of Defense.

Defense Federal Acquisition Regulation Supplement (DFARS)
•
A supplement of the FAR:
•
Called Defense Federal Acquisition Regulation Supplement or DFARS.
•
Includes policies and procedures that:
•
Apply only to acquisition activities by the Department of Defense.
•
Are administered by the Department of Defense.
•
As a Certified CMMC Professional, you must have some familiarity with DFARS:
•
Know that it covers DoD acquisitions.
•
Be familiar with clauses that apply directly to contractors.
•
For most contactors, it is not necessary to know everything documented in DFARS.
DFARS

Definitions Provided in FAR 52
•
FAR 52 (48 CFR § 52.204-21) covers:
•
Definitions you need to know to understand the regulations.
•
Requirements and procedures that a contractor must undertake to safeguard FCI.
•
Responsibilities when delegating contract work to a subcontractor.
•
Covered contractor information system
means an information system that is owned
or operated by a contractor that processes,
stores, or transmits Federal contract
information.
•
Federal contract information means
information, not intended for public release,
that is provided by or generated for the
Government under a contract to develop or
deliver a product or service to the
Government, but not including information
provided by the Government to the public
(such as on public Web sites) or simple
transactional information, such as necessary
to process payments.
•
Information means any communication or
representation of knowledge such as facts,
data, or opinions, in any medium or form,
including textual, numerical, graphic,
cartographic, narrative, or audiovisual
(Committee on National Security Systems
Instruction (CNSSI) 4009).
•
Information system means a discrete set of
information resources organized for the
collection, processing, maintenance, use,
sharing, dissemination, or disposition of
information (44 U.S.C. 3502).
•
Safeguarding means measures or controls that
are prescribed to protect information systems.
a)
Definitions. As used in this clause –
Figure 1-17: FAR 52 begins with definitions of terms used in the regulations, such as Federal
Contract Information (FCI).

Safeguarding FCI as Outlined in FAR 52
•
FAR 52 (48 CFR § 52.204-21) covers:
•
Definitions you need to know to understand the regulations.
•
Requirements and procedures that a contractor must undertake to safeguard FCI.
•
Responsibilities when delegating contract work to a subcontractor.
1)
The Contractor shall apply the following basic
safeguarding requirements and procedures to
protect covered contractor information
systems. Requirements and procedures for
basic safeguarding of covered contractor
information systems shall include, at a
minimum, the following security controls:
i.
Limit information system access to
authorized users, processes acting on
behalf of authorized users, or devices
(including other information systems).
ii.
Limit information system access to the
types of transactions and functions that
authorized users are permitted to execute.
iii. Verify and control/limit connections to and
use of external information systems.
iv.
Control information posted or processed on
publicly accessible information systems.
v.
Identify information system users, processes
acting on behalf of users, or devices.
vi.
Authenticate (or verify) the identities of
those users, processes, or devices, as a
prerequisite to allowing access to
organizational information systems.
b)
Safeguarding requirements and procedures.
Figure 1-18: Six of the 15 requirements and procedures that must be implemented to protect FCI
per FAR 52.

More FCI Safeguarding Requirements
•
FAR 52 (48 CFR § 52.204-21) covers:
•
Definitions you need to know to understand the regulations.
•
Requirements and procedures that a contractor must undertake to safeguard FCI.
•
Responsibilities when delegating contract work to a subcontractor.
vii.
Sanitize or destroy information system media
containing Federal Contract Information
before disposal or release for reuse.
viii.
Limit physical access to organizational
information systems, equipment, and the
respective operating environments to
authorized individuals.
ix.
Escort visitors and monitor visitor activity;
maintain audit logs of physical access; and
control and manage physical access devices.
x.
Monitor, control, and protect organizational
communications (i.e., information transmitted
or received by organizational information
systems) at the external boundaries and key
internal boundaries of the information
systems.
xi.
Implement subnetworks for publicly accessible
system components that are physically or
logically separated from internal networks.
xii.
Identify, report, and correct information and
information system flaws in a timely manner.
xiii.
Provide protection from malicious code at
appropriate locations within organizational
information systems.
xiv.
Update malicious code protection mechanisms
when new releases are available.
xv.
Perform periodic scans of the information
system and real-time scans of files from external
sources as files are downloaded, opened, or
executed.

FCI per FAR 52.

Other requirements.This clause does not

Federal agencies and departments relating to
covered contractor information systems
generally or other Federal safeguarding
requirements for controlled unclassified
information (CUI) as established by Executive

supersede any of the
requirements that
have been put into
effect by other
contract clauses or
laws.

Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI) LRP Drivers
Legal Authority
•
2002 Federal Information Security Management Act (FISMA)
Amended in 2014
•
Executive Order 13556, Controlled Unclassified Information
(4 November 2010)
Regulatory Authority
•
32 CFR Part 2002, Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI)
Policy
•
National Archive & Records Administration (NARA)
Information Security Oversight Office (ISOO) CUI Notices
•
These regulations:
•
Explain how to adhere to Executive Order 13556.
•
Stipulate and create overall requirements,
governance, and management of CUI.
•
Appointed NARA to oversee CUI policy.
•
Stood up ISOO, which publishes CUI notices.
•
Government must protect its sensitive
information (e.g., FCI and CUI).
•
Standardized handling of protected information
that is unclassified.
•
CUI is subject to laws governing FCI, as well as
those specifically addressing CUI.
•
CUI notices are considered federal policy.
•
Policies stipulate that CUI must be protected in
accordance with:
•
NIST SP 800-171
•
NIST SP 800-172
•
NIST SP 800-171A

requirements for CUI are shown here.

--- PAGE 37 ---
NARA ISOO
National Archives and Records Administration (NARA)
•
Information Security Oversight Office (ISOO)
•
Contained within NARA.
•
Responsible to the President for policy and oversight of:
•
The U.S. government’s security classification system.
•
The National Industrial Security Program.
•
Receives policy and program guidance from the National Security Council (NSC).
•
Has three components:
•
Classification Management Staff
Develop security classification policies for classifying, declassifying, and safeguarding
national security information generated in Government and industry.
•
Operations Staff
Evaluate the effectiveness of the security classification programs established by
Government and industry to protect information vital to our national security
interests.
•
Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI) Staff
Develop standardized CUI policies and procedures that appropriately protect sensitive
information through effective data access and control measures.
•
Serves as the authority on protection of CUI.
Figure 1-23: NARA's Information Security Oversight Office is the authority on the protection of
CUI.

NIST SP 800-171 Focus on Confidentiality
•
NIST SP 800-171:
•
Focuses primarily on protecting the confidentiality of CUI.
•
Does not emphasize other aspects of cybersecurity,
such as integrity or availability.
•
Additional frameworks can be used
to protect integrity and availability.
•
Examples:
•
NIST Cybersecurity Framework
•
NIST SP 800-53 rev 5
Focuses on
protecting the
confidentiality
of CUI.
Focuses on
protecting the
availability
of critical
systems.
Originally
developed to
protect availability
of critical
infrastructure,
such as power
plants.
Figure 1-26: With its focus on CUI, NIST SP 800-171 primarily focuses on protecting
confidentiality, so an additional framework such as the NIST Cybersecurity Framework might be
used to ensure that integrity and availability are also thoroughly protected.

NIST SP 800-172 Enhanced Security Requirements for CUI
•
NIST SP 800-172
•
“Enhanced Security Requirements for Protecting Controlled
Unclassified Information: A Supplement to NIST SP 800˗171”.
•
Provides federal agencies with enhanced security requirements.
•
Aims to protect the confidentiality, integrity, and availability of CUI:
•
When CUI resides in a nonfederal system and organization.
•
When the nonfederal organization is not collecting or maintaining
information on behalf of a federal agency or using or operating a
system on behalf of an agency.
•
Where there are no specific safeguarding requirements for
protecting the CUI prescribed by the authorizing law, regulation,
or government-wide policy for the CUI category listed in the
CUI Registry.
•
NIST SP 800-171B incorporated into higher levels during the
development of the CMMC Model.
•
NIST SP 800-172 made NIST SP 800-171B obsolete.
•
NIST SP 800-172 was formerly known as Draft NIST SP 800-171B.
Figure 1-28: NIST SP 800-172 provides protections for integrity and availability not covered in
NIST SP 800-171.

--- PAGE 44 ---
Philosophical Change of Direction to Secure the Nation’s Data
DFARS Clause 252.204-7012, Safeguarding Covered Defense Information and Cyber Incident Reporting
•
Global self-attestation by contract signature.
•
Imposes requirements on how contractors handle CUI.
•
Requires compliance with NIST SP 800-171.
•
Documented in System Security Plan (SSP) and Plan of Action and Milestones (POA&M).
•
Self-attest only.
DFARS Provision 252.204-7019, Notice of NIST SP 800-171 DoD Assessment Requirements
•
Detailed self-attestation.
•
DIB contractors must formally report to DoD a summary score of their NIST SP 800-171 compliance.
•
Subject to Defense Contract Management Agency (DCMA) audits.
DFARS Clause 252.204-7020, NIST SP 800-171 DoD Assessment Requirements
•
Defines how the DoD will conduct different types of assessments.
•
Government contractors must be assessed for compliance.
DFARS Clause 252.204-7021, Contractor Compliance with the CMMC Level Requirement
•
Tiered assessment requirements of cybersecurity based on the sensitivity of the information.
•
Enacts Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC).
•
Government-recognized assessments for contractors who handle critical national security
information; self-assessments for contractors who do not handle critical information.
•
CMMC Model 2.0 released in November 2021; will be implemented after the rulemaking process.
DEC 2017
NOV 2020
NOV 2020
NOV 2020

requirements for securing the nation's data.

--- PAGE 50 ---
Federal Information Security Modernization Act (FISMA)
Overview
•
Law that requires executive agencies to:
•
Plan for security based on risk impact levels (Low, Moderate, and High).
•
Assign security responsibility to appropriate officials.
•
Monitor security controls in their systems.
•
Authorize system processing prior to operations and, periodically, thereafter.
•
Applies to:
•
Federal agencies.
•
State agencies that administer federal programs (e.g., unemployment, student loans, Medicare/Medicaid).
Impact to FCI/CUI
•
NIST SP 800-53 defines the cybersecurity requirements for FISMA.
•
Based on DoD Information Technology Security Certification and Accreditation Process (DITSCAP) controls.
•
Drove the need for:
•
Risk-based policy for cost-effective security within the Federal Government Supply Chain.
•
Creation of the NIST Risk Management Framework (RMF).
•
Creation of 32 CFR Part 2002 ˗ CONTROLLED UNCLASSIFIED INFORMATION (CUI).
•
Implementing Directive for Executive Order 13556.
Figure 1-36: An overview of Federal Information Security Modernization Act (FISMA) and its
impact on protecting sensitive information.
Notes
38 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 1: Managing Risk within the Defense Supply Chain | Topic B
Federal Information
Security Modernization
Act (FISMA)

Risk Management Framework (RMF)
Overview
•
Designed to help Federal agencies meet FISMA requirements (plus other laws/regulations/policies).
•
Standardized process to follow to secure, authorize, and manage IT systems.
•
Two NIST Special Publications:
•
NIST SP 800˗37 Revision 2 ˗ Risk Management Framework for Information Systems and Organizations.
•
NIST SP 800˗53 Security and Privacy Controls for Information Systems and Organizations.
Impact to FCI/CUI
•
Used with NIST SP 800˗53 as the basis for the Government’s design of NIST SP 800˗171.
•
WARNING:
•
NIST RMF, like ISO 27000, is a framework used for organizations to tailor controls to meet their requirements.
•
However, NIST SP 800˗171 is prescribed by the Government.
•
Can't change controls.
Figure 1-37: An overview of Risk Management Framework (RMF) and its impact on protecting
sensitive information.

Risk Management

--- PAGE 52 ---
The RMF Process
•
Prepare cybersecurity strategy at the organization level.
•
Establish the context and priorities for managing security risk.
•
Categorize information systems.
•
Select security controls.
•
Tailor controls to reduce risk to an acceptable level based on risk assessment.
•
Implement security controls.
•
Assess security control implementation.
•
Implemented correctly and producing desired outcomes.
•
Authorize information systems.
•
Monitor security controls.
•
Ensure ongoing effectiveness.

--- PAGE 53 ---
Federal Risk and Authorization Management Program
(FedRAMP)
Overview
•
A certification program for Cloud Service Providers (CSPs) who provide the government cloud services.
•
Streamlines federal IT procurement of cloud services that meet federal cybersecurity regulatory standards.
•
"Do once, use many times" framework.
•
Standardized approach to security assessment, authorization, and continuous monitoring.
•
CSPs can work with multiple agencies without repeating the certification process.
•
Can be thought of as "cloud FISMA".
•
NIST SP 800˗53 defines the cybersecurity requirements.
•
Independent assessments completed by a third-party organization.
•
Promotes the usage of secure cloud services to reduce costs for executive agencies and departments.
Impact to FCI/CUI
•
DoD requires FedRAMP Moderate or Higher for CSPs processing, storing, or transmitting CUI for the DIB.
•
CMMC created its ecosystem using FedRAMP as a program model.
•
DFARS Clause 252.204˗7012 specifies:
•
Contractors must ensure:
•
CSPs meet security requirements equivalent to FedRAMP Moderate.
•
CSPs are complying with paragraphs (c) through (g) of 252/204˗7012.
•
Failure to comply with the latter half means OSC is not compliant with their Incident Response requirements.

Handling Sensitive
Information
Lesson Time: 4 hours, 20 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you better understand the threats to the Defense Supply Chain and the regulatory
responses that govern mitigation against such threats, you can delve more deeply into what
exactly is this sensitive information that requires protection and how does it need to be
managed.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Discriminate between sensitive information types.
•
Manage sensitive information.
2

--- PAGE 62 ---
TOPIC A
Identify Sensitive Information
It is a matter of national security to ensure that organizations who have contracts with the
Department of Defense (DoD) know what sensitive information is and how they should protect it.
As a Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) who may be advising or helping to assess such
organizations, you must be able to discriminate between different types of sensitive information so
you can help to identify how that information should be handled and protected.
Sensitive Information
Sensitive Information: Information where the loss,
misuse, or unauthorized access or modification could
adversely affect the national interest or the conduct of
federal programs, or the privacy to which individuals are
entitled under 5 U.S.C. Section 552a (the Privacy Act).
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-53 Rev 4 (adapted))
Figure 2-1: An organization's ability to identify sensitive information within their environment
and ensure it is properly protected is a matter of national security.

Federal Contract Information (FCI)
•
FCI is the broadest definition of government information requiring protection.
•
Characteristics include:
•
Private information.
•
Contract related.
•
Exemptions include:
•
Provided by government on public websites.
•
Transactional information.
•
Defined in section 4.1901 of the Federal Acquisition Regulation (FAR).
https://www.acquisition.gov/far/4.1901
FCI: Information, not intended for public release, that is provided by or generated for the
Government under a contract to develop or deliver a product or service to the Government, but
not including information provided by the Government to the public (such as on public websites)
or simple transactional information, such as necessary to process payments.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (48 CFR 52.204-21)
Figure 2-3: FCI is the broadest definition of government intellectual property requiring protection
by an OSC.

--- PAGE 65 ---
What Is FCI and What Is Not FCI
•
What is FCI:
•
A Defense Contractor has a contract that includes the schedule for delivery.
•
The project schedule is not intended for public release.
•
It is only useful to the Defense Contractor and DoD.
•
Therefore, it is FCI.
•
What is not FCI:
•
Within the same contract, the basic requirements for bidding on the project
are included.
•
The information has been publicly released on the centralized government
website for all to see.
•
Therefore, it is not FCI.

Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI)
•
CUI is:
•
Always considered FCI (but not all FCI is necessarily CUI).
•
Not classified information
(as defined under E.O. 13526 or Atomic Energy Act of 1954).
•
Information that:
•
The government creates or possesses.
•
An entity creates or possesses on behalf of the government.
•
Requires safeguarding.
•
Dissemination controlled by a law, regulation, or government policy.
•
Defined in Part 2002 of Title 32 CFR 2002.4(h).
CUI: Information that requires safeguarding or dissemination controls pursuant to and consistent
with law, regulations, and government-wide policies, excluding information that is classified under
Executive Order 13526, Classified National Security Information, December 29, 2009, or any
predecessor or successor order, or the Atomic Energy Act of 1954, as amended.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (E.O. 13556 (adapted))

Not more important than CUI Basic.

Controlled Technical Information (CTI)
•
CTI is a specified category of CUI.
•
CTI is a type of CUI that includes technical information.
•
Especially likely to occur in contracts with military or space applications .
•
Technical information is technical data or computer software, as defined in DFARS Clause 252.227-7013.
•
Marked or otherwise identified in the contract, task order, or delivery order and provided to
contractor by or on behalf of, DoD in support of the performance of a contract; or
•
Collected, developed, received, transmitted, used, or stored by, or on behalf of the government in
support of the performance of a contract.
CTI: Technical information with military or space application that is subject to controls on the
access, use, reproduction, modification, performance, display, release, disclosure, or
dissemination.
Source: DFARS Clause 252.204˗7012

Export-Controlled Information (ECI)
•
ECI is a specified category of CUI.
•
ECI includes:
•
Physical assets and encryption technologies.
•
Assets falling within the scope of ITAR/EAR.
•
International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) states that the
U.S. government has the authority to ban the sale of weapons
and related technologies to foreign companies.
•
Export Administration Regulations (EAR).
•
Administered by the Department of Commerce.
•
Includes technologies not on the ITAR list, which focuses mostly
on defense technologies.
•
ECI is subject to additional requirements above and beyond CUI handling.
•
ECI can't be released to anyone that is not a U.S. citizen.
•
CCPs might see ECI dissemination markings on CUI materials where export is controlled.
ECI: Any information or material that cannot be released to foreign nationals or representatives of
a foreign entity, without first obtaining approval or license from the Dept. of State for items
controlled by ITAR or the Dept. of Commerce for items controlled by the Export Administration
Regulations (EAR).

--- PAGE 77 ---
ACTIVITY 2-1
Identifying What Is and What Is Not Sensitive
Information
Scenario
The definitions for FCI and CUI are pretty broad. In this activity, to help you clarify the distinctions
between what is sensitive information and what is not sensitive information, you'll consider some
examples and determine whether they qualify as FCI, CUI, CTI, or ECI.
1.
Example: A Request for Proposal (RFP)
Is a RFP sensitive information?
A: It depends. Most RFPs are entirely public, so they are not FCI. However, some aspects of an RFP
may only be available to companies with credentials for certain systems. In such cases, those
portions of the RFP are likely to be at least FCI and may even rise to the level of CUI.
2.
Example: Teaming agreement.
When an RFP is released, in many cases a Prime contractor will be able to
perform most of the requirements themselves. However, they frequently need
assistance from one or more subcontractors to meet the RFP's requirements.
So, the Prime contractor and subcontractor(s) enter into teaming agreements
that describe their respective roles and responsibilities should they win a
contract. Is a teaming agreement sensitive information?
A: Typically, no. Teaming agreements are not created for the government, so although they are
(typically) nonpublic documents, they are not FCI. However, as discussed in the previous
example, some RFPs include FCI or CUI. If the teaming agreement includes portions of the RFP
with FCI or CUI, then the teaming agreement "inherits" the FCI or CUI designation.
3.
Example: A contract with the government.
Are government contracts sensitive information?
A: Many contract terms are publicly available. These include the contractor's name and address, the
amount of the contract, the contracting and funding agencies, the types of goods or services
purchased, and more. But specific contract terms are typically not public information. So, should
the contract be treated as FCI? Probably. Is the contract still FCI if it is made public because of a
Freedom of Information Act (FOIA) request? It depends on the information that is disclosed by the
FOIA request, but there is a good chance that the contract terms will now be public information.
Does that mean we must stop treating the contract information as FCI? No. There is no
requirement that you stop protecting information; the only difference is that you may not need to
protect it. As a general rule, it is better to assume that contract terms are FCI until you are
confident they aren’t.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 65
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic A
Activity

--- PAGE 78 ---
4.
Example: Manual written for a product created under a defense contract.
When a company creates product documentation when under contract with
the DoD, are those materials sensitive information?
A: Yes, a manual for a product created for the government is CUI. In fact, the manual is likely an
example of CTI, a specified defense subcategory of CUI.
5.
Example: A product specification for a defense contractor's commercial
product.
Are commercial product specification sheets produced by a defense
contractor sensitive information?
A: Product specifications for a government contractor's products are not likely to be information
created under a contract for the government. Therefore, they are not typically going to be FCI.
6.
Example: A design specification created by a defense contractor.
A contractor documents design specifications and submits them to the
government. Are design specifications created under contract sensitive
information?
A: Design specifications and other information created under a contract with the government is likely
to be CUI, unless the contract explicitly states that the information is public or to be made public.
For example, if the government hires your customer to create a new software application that will
be released as open-source software, that might cause the software's design specifications and
source code to not need to be treated as CUI. Short of outlier situations such as this, though, the
software, its documentation and design information, and its source code should be treated as CUI.
7.
Example: Draft marketing materials that a defense contractor is creating for
the government.
Under contract with the DoD, a contractor creates various drafts of marketing
materials. Are drafts of marketing materials sensitive information?
A: Marketing materials are public. But drafts of the materials are not typically public. Therefore, the
drafts should be treated as FCI.
8.
Example: Email communications from the government.
The defense contractor receives emails from the government communicating
information about the contract. Are emails communicating contract
information sensitive information?
A: If the defense contractor receives emails from specific individuals in the government (as opposed
to public announcements like the CISA weekly new cybersecurity vulnerability alerts), those
emails are likely to contain nonpublic information and should be treated as FCI or even CUI,
depending on whether the information conveyed in the email is subject to safeguarding or
dissemination controls.
9.
Example: Encryption technology created by a defense contractor.
The defense contractor creates encryption technologies as part of a drone
operating system. Are the software and encryption source code sensitive
information?
A: The drone operating system is CUI and probably CTI. In addition, the encryption technology
embedded in the operating system might also be ECI.
66 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic A

--- PAGE 79 ---
10.
Example: Military base road map.
Is a road map for a military base sensitive information?
A: No, the road map is public information. However, civil engineering maps for a military base would
be considered CUI because it details the infrastructure, which is not public information.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 67
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic A
These examples show
that sensitive information
encompasses a wide
range of information.
Therefore, point out that
most government
contractors will
encounter at least some
FCI or CUI as part of
their performance under
a government contract.

TOPIC B
Manage the Sensitive Information
In this topic, you will learn about the management and governance of Federal Contract Information
(FCI) and Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI). This includes the tasks that an Organization
Seeking Certification (OSC) is required to perform based on the regulations to properly protect
sensitive information. As a CCP, you might be providing consultative services or working as an
Assessment Team Member to validate an OSC's compliance to the regulations.

DoD Instruction 5200.48
•
DoD Instruction 5200.48 “Controlled Unclassified Information”:
•
Establishes policy.
•
Assigns responsibilities.
•
Prescribes procedures for the identification, handling, and storage of CUI throughout the DoD.
•
Establishes the official DoD CUI Registry.
•
Requires companies interacting with CUI for a lawful government purpose in support of a DoD
program to follow:
•
DoDI 5200.48.
•
DFARS Section 252.204-7008.
•
DFARS Section 252.204-7012.

DoD Instruction 5200.48

--- PAGE 82 ---
How CUI Is Determined
•
DoD Instruction 5200.48 specifies that CUI is determined by the authorized holder.
•
The determination is made at the time of creation.
•
The authorized holder is responsible for determining whether the information falls into a CUI category.
•
The authorized holder is then responsible for applying CUI markings and dissemination instructions
accordingly.
•
Per DoD Instruction 5200.48, DoD’s CUI responsibility includes:
•
Identifying whether any of the information being shared to their contractors is CUI via the contracting
vehicle.
•
Appropriately marking documents, material, or other media accordingly.
•
The Prime monitors and determines additional classification and/or handling requirements for:
•
Aggregated and/or consolidated information.
•
Data and/or other media.
•
All DoD personnel, contractors, and subcontractors will submit unclassified
data for review in accordance with DoDI 5230.09.

Legal Rationale for Sharing and/or Transmitting CUI
•
Lawful government purpose is a dynamic standard.
•
Use risk-based decision making when deciding if a person is authorized to receive the CUI.
•
DoDI 5200.48 states:
•
No individual may have access to CUI information unless it is determined he or she has an authorized, lawful
government purpose.
•
The person with authorized possession, knowledge, or control of CUI will determine whether an individual
has an authorized, lawful government purpose to access designated CUI.
Lawful government purpose: Any activity, mission, function, operation, or endeavor that the U.S.
Government authorizes or recognizes as within the scope of its legal authorities or the legal
authorities of non-executive branch entities (such as state and local law enforcement).
Source: 32 CFR 2002.4
Figure 2-20: Only people with a lawful government purpose should have access to CUI.

Legal Rationale for
Sharing and/or
Transmitting CUI

--- PAGE 85 ---
Sharing and/or Transmitting CUI
•
NARA permits access and dissemination if it:
•
Is not prohibited by law, federal regulation, or government-wide policy.
•
Furthers a lawful government purpose.
•
Is not subject to limited dissemination controls.
•
CUI should only be distributed when there is a reasonable expectation that all intended recipients are:
•
Authorized to receive the CUI.
•
Trained how to properly handle CUI.
Share CUI when access promotes a common project or
operation and furthers a lawful government purpose.
•
Between agencies.
•
Under a contract or agreement with the
designating agency.
Do NOT share CUI if access harms or inhibits a
common project or operation.
•
Between agencies.
•
Under a contract or agreement with the
designating agency.
Transmit CUI electronically (e.g., website or email) via:
•
Approved secure communications systems.
•
Systems utilizing other protective measures, such as:
•
Public Key Infrastructure (PKI) or
•
Transport Layer Security
•
CUI must be stored or handled in controlled
environments that prevent unauthorized access.
•
Sender.
•
Recipient.
Sharing CUI
Transmitting CUI
Figure 2-21: There is a difference between sharing and transmitting CUI.

Sharing and/or

--- PAGE 86 ---
Sharing Sensitive Data
•
Sharing sensitive data between external networks and the OSC’s network requires:
•
Protection of data integrity and confidentiality by implementing methods, such as:
•
Encryption of emails, folders, and network segments.
•
Security settings to protect data transmitted during teleconference sessions.
•
Correct handling of data that contains CUI Markings.
•
Compliance with U.S. Privacy Laws to protect PII.
•
U.S. Privacy Act (44 U.S. Code Sec 3542).
•
Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA).
•
Compliance with internal organizational policies and applicable federal agency policies that specify approved
methods of safe data transmission.
Data Integrity: Property that data has not been altered in an unauthorized manner.
Confidentiality: Preserving authorized restrictions on information access and disclosure, including
means for protecting personal privacy and proprietary information.
Personally Identifiable Information (PII): Information that can be used to distinguish or trace an
individual’s identity, either alone or when combined with other information that is linked or
linkable to a specific individual.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (44 U.S. Code Sec 3542)
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-33)
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-33)

DoD Marking Requirements
Must Do
•
At a minimum, CUI markings for unclassified documents will include:
•
The acronym “CUI” at the top and bottom of each page.
•
The CUI designation indicator.
•
For all documents containing CUI, add CUI designations on the first page and/or the cover page.
Do Not
•
Do not add “UNCLASSIFIED” before ”CUI.”
•
Do not add the CUI category to the top and bottom of the page.
•
Category is listed in the CUI designation indicator.
Optional
•
For unclassified documents, portion markings are optional.
•
If annotated, markings must be applied to all portions to include subjects, titles, paragraphs,
subparagraphs, bullet points, figures, charts, tables, etc.
•
Do not apply portion markings to the CUI designation indicator.
Source: https://www.dodcui.mil/Portals/109/Documents/Desktop%20Aid%20Docs/20-S-2093%20cleared%20training%20guide-13_oct-20.pdf
Figure 2-23: To be compliant with DoD marking requirements, all documents containing CUI
must be marked accordingly.

•
CUI Category or Subcategory markings are separated by a double forward slash (//).
•
All CUI Specified Category markings must be included in the CUI Banner Marking.
•
Each federal agency can modify these markings based on their needs.
•
Documents must indicate the designator’s agency and a branch or division with contact information.
•
Letterhead.
•
Signature block with agency.
•
Use of a “Controlled by” line.

--- PAGE 97 ---
Requirements for Mailing CUI Documents
•
A document containing CUI may be transmitted via:
•
First Class, Express, Certified, or Registered mail, as well as by any commercial carrier;
•
A person authorized access to the CUI so long as the person can control access to the document being
transported; or
•
Mail services internal to the facility.
•
To transmit CUI in a document, it must be packaged in a sealed, opaque envelope:
•
For external transmissions, the document must have the recipient’s address, a return address,
and the words “TO BE OPENED BY ADDRESSEE ONLY” on the front.
•
For internal transmissions, the document must have the words “TO BE OPENED BY ADDRESSEE
ONLY” on the front, but only the recipient’s address is required.
Figure 2-33: When mailing documents containing CUI, you must adhere to the following
requirements.
Notes

Fax and Package Marking Considerations
•
Faxes:
•
Use a transmittal coversheet (e.g., SF 901) with the CUI documents.
•
Annotate with:
•
“When enclosure is removed, this document is Uncontrolled Unclassified Information” or
•
“When enclosure is removed, this document is (CUI Category); upon removal, this document does not
contain CUI.”
•
Make sure authorized person is present to receive the fax.
•
DO NOT send to an unattended fax machine.
•
Packages:
•
CUI can be mailed or shipped.
•
Package in a non-transparent envelope or box.
•
Include authorized person in the address block.
•
Pay to have the carrier track the package.
•
Do not mark the outside envelope or box with CUI marking.
Outer shipping
container has no
CUI marking
Inner container
marked as CUI

CCP Responsibilities Related to CUI Marking
•
As a CCP, you need to know that CUI should be, and how it is to be, marked.
•
On the consulting side, you might be:
•
Educating your client on how to mark CUI.
•
Assisting your client, implementing templates to ensure processes are supported.
•
Labeling storage media, systems, rooms, etc.
•
During assessments, you will be:
•
Evaluating OSC training of their staff on CUI marking.
•
Assessing if they are marking all forms of CUI.
•
Evaluating CUI-related protections.
•
CMMC Model is only concerned with whether CUI is marked and handled correctly.
•
CCAs will not evaluate the specific details of the marking (e.g., is it really CUI//SP-CTI).

Safeguard Data Using Access Controls
•
To safeguard sensitive data, apply the proper access controls.
•
Physical and Logical access controls work collaboratively to ensure only those authorized are
granted access.
•
To be effective, organizations must use a combination of both.
Restrict Physical Access to:
•
Facilities
•
Rooms
•
Devices
•
Media
•
Other assets
Restrict Logical Access to:
•
Data
•
Digital resources
•
Networks
Figure 2-36: Applying the proper physical and logical access controls protects data from
unauthorized individuals.
Notes
88 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Safeguard Data Using
Access Controls

--- PAGE 101 ---
Authentication and Authorization
Authentication
•
The process by which a system verifies the identity of a user who wants access to the system.
•
Example: Passwords, tokens, thumbprints
Authorization
•
Determines what an identity can access within a system once it's been authenticated to it.
•
Example: OAuth
Access Control Mechanisms
•
Determine which operations a user may or may not engage with through comparing the user
identity to the access credentials (e.g., file, program, or data access).
•
Example: Role Based Access Control (RBAC)
Encryption
•
Provides additional protections in cases where a user or system may be sharing sensitive
protected information (e.g., design documents, financial statements, test results).
•
Example: Secure Shell (SSH) and Socket Layer Protocols (SSL)
Figure 2-37: Authentication, authorization, and access control mechanisms are applied to
safeguard sensitive information from unauthorized individuals.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 89
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Authentication and
Authorization

--- PAGE 102 ---
Multifactor Authentication (MFA)
•
Users are required to present at least two of the following:
•
Something you know: Response to Challenge Question
•
Something you have: Token (physical or electronic) or third-party authentication app
•
Something you are: Fingerprint or Retina scan
•
May be accomplished through:
•
Two Factor Authentication (2FA)
•
Adaptive Multifactor Authentication (AMFA)
•
Encryption Key Management
Multifactor Authentication (MFA): A mechanism that provides for added protection
of data through electronic methods.
Figure 2-38: MFA offers stronger protection of an organization's sensitive information.
Notes
90 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Multifactor
Authentication (MFA)

Company Policies and Processes for Safeguarding Standards
•
Policies should clearly articulate data handling expectations and objectives to establish
governance for the organization.
•
Processes should articulate how the policy objectives will be met.
•
Collectively, organizational policies and processes should serve to complement existing DoD policy
and instruction, including:
•
CUI LRP Drivers
•
Executive Order 13556, Controlled Unclassified Information
•
32 CFR Part 2002, Controlled Unclassified Information
•
DoD Instructions (DoDI)
•

•

•
DoD Manual 5200.01
•
Volume 1, DoD INFOSEC Program: Overview, Classification, and Declassification
•
Volume 2, DoD INFOSEC Program: Marking of Information
•

Cybersecurity Awareness Training
•
Identifies common risks and cybersecurity threats.
•
Reduces human error.
•
Provides best practices for cyber hygiene.
•
Helps employees understand the security risks of their actions.
•
Mitigates security risks to sensitive information.
•
Recognizes and responds to internal and external threats.
•
Enhances organizational resilience against cybersecurity
threats.
•
Embeds a culture of security compliance.
Awareness: A learning process that sets the stage for training by changing individual and
organizational attitudes to realize the importance of security and the adverse consequences of its
failure.
Awareness and Training Program: Explains proper rules of behavior for the use of agency information
systems and information. The program communicates information technology (IT) security policies
and procedures that need to be followed. (i.e., NSTISSD 501, NIST SP 800-50).

Configuration Management
•
Helps organizations establish, maintain, and control the organization’s systems, devices, and data.
•
These controls collectively help to reinforce our cyber resiliency while reducing potential threat.
•
Establishes and maintains baseline configurations and inventories of the organizational system
throughout the respective system development life cycles.
•
Includes hardware, software, firmware, and documentation.
•
Enforces the principle of least functionality by:
•
Configuring organizational systems to provide only essential processes.
•
Establishes and enforces security configuration settings.
•
Tracks, reviews, approves, and logs changes to systems analyzing the security impact of changes.
•
Defines, documents, approves, and enforces both physical and logical access restrictions
associated with changes to organizational systems.
•
Restricts, disables, or prevents the use of nonessential:
•
Programs, functions, ports, protocols, and services.
Configuration Management: A collection of activities focused on establishing and maintaining the
integrity of information technology products and systems, through control of processes for
initializing, changing, and monitoring the configurations of those products and systems
throughout the system development life cycle.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms, NIST SP 800-53 Rev 5

--- PAGE 107 ---
Physical Storage
•
Store CUI in controlled physical environments that prevent unauthorized access.
•
At least one physical barrier must be employed to limit access to authorized people.
•
Employ keyed/cipher locks, security check points, and/or automated access control systems.
•
Printing of CUI documents and data should be minimized.
•
Proper safeguarding of printed CUI needs to be applied where hard copies are unavoidable.
•
Reasonable steps should be taken to mitigate risk of inadvertent disclosure/unauthorized access:
•
During working hours, have a visitor escort policy.
•
Monitor your surroundings when unauthorized personnel are present in the office or during travel.
•
Do not view CUI in a public place to prevent observation.
•
Do not leave CUI unattended to prevent accidental exposure.
•
Do not discuss CUI to prevent overhearing.
•
After working hours or during travel, store CUI documents and authorized hard storage devices in:
•
Locked desks or file cabinets.
•
Locked rooms.
•
Locked data centers.
•
Storage room designed for data security.
•
Locked communication areas.
Figure 2-43: Compliance with the requirements for proper storage protects CUI from being
accessed by unauthorized individuals.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 95
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Physical Storage

--- PAGE 108 ---
Electronic Storage
•
Store CUI in controlled electronic environments that prevent unauthorized access.
•
Employ encryption for data maintained on storage media.
•
Full disk, volume/virtual, and file/folder encryption, where indicated.
•
Use only authorized workstations or mobile devices.
•
Don’t store CUI on personally owned systems or devices.
•
The OSC should utilize an agency sponsored/authorized virtual environment, where available.
•
Minimum security controls must be applied on systems and devices.
•
At least one electronic barrier must be employed to limit access.
•
For example, dedicated network drives, SharePoint sites, intranet sites.
•
A splash screen warning and notice of consent must alert users of CUI within the system.
Figure 2-44: Compliance with the requirements for proper storage of electronic media protects
CUI from unauthorized access.

Backup Systems
•
Data backups provide a secure archive of the organization’s most valuable information and data.
•
Allows organizations to quickly and seamlessly restore data.
•
Allows for continuity of operations.
•
Solutions may be tailored and scaled to support the organization’s business needs.
•

•
Encrypt backup files.
•

•

•
Removable media.

--- PAGE 110 ---
Required Encryption
•
When authorized employees interact with CUI, the files must be protected by encryption.
•
Encryption is required for CUI both:
•
At rest/in storage.
•
On all mobile devices and portable media.
•
On system media when outside a CUI-controlled environment/enclave.
•
In transit/during transmission.
•
Current commercial email and storage solutions are insufficient to comply with DoD requirements
for contractors with CUI.
•
At a minimum, organizations should employ FIPS-validated cryptography to protect the
confidentiality of CUI.
•
Cryptographic modules must be tested & validated to meet Federal Information Processing Standard
requirements, FIPS 140-1 or 140-2.
•
In essence, if cryptography is required, then it must be validated.
•
Should the cryptographic module be revoked, use of that module is no longer permitted.
FIPS
Encryption: The process of changing plaintext into cipher text.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NISTIR 7621 Rev 1, CNSSI 4009)
Figure 2-46: Encryption protects files containing CUI at rest and in transit.

Required Encryption

--- PAGE 111 ---
Network Segmentation
•
Benefits:
•
Monitor traffic more effectively.
•
Reduce lateral movement.
•
Create segment(s) based on data classification.
•
Establish controlled environments and restrict access through your role - or rule-based access control procedures.
•
Examples of network segments:
•
Separating public facing from internal systems (in which the public -facing side is referred to as a DMZ).
•
Keeping financial or management data separate from general company data.
•
Creating segments to isolate the storage and processing of data classified as FCI or CUI from the general network.
•
Document segments with a network diagram.
•
DMZ provides external, untrusted sources with restricted access to releasable information while
shielding the internal networks from outside attacks.
Network segmentation: The use of physical devices such as firewalls or logical separation such as
subnetting to create distinct segments in your internal network.
Demilitarized Zone (DMZ): A small section of a private network that is located between two
firewalls and made available for public access.
Figure 2-47: Network segmentation is a crucial tool to create a controlled environment for FCI
and CUI.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 99
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Network Segmentation

--- PAGE 112 ---
Role-Based and Rule-Based Access Control
Role-Based Access Control (RBAC)
•
Manages access to different assets or segments of your network through roles that align with
responsibilities, departments, etc. inside your company.
•
Each individual account can then be given roles based on responsibilities or required accesses.
•
Less possibility of human error compared with providing privileges to every account individually.
•
Allows you to give access to or remove access from a new segment or asset to an entire role group at once.
•
Instead of having to modify dozens or even hundreds of accounts to make the change.
Rule-Based Access Control
•
Can supplement a RBAC model by adding rules to only allow access to specified data classifications.
•
Rules might be applied to specific user account(s), certain IP addresses (whitelisting/blacklisting), certain ports,
or during certain times of day.
•
Provides a more granular level of control of who or what has access to CUI data.
•
Can be controlled at a high level of system access all the way down to the level of individual files or folders.
•
Allows for personnel who need access to FCI/CUI to be the only people allowed to view or modify the files.
Attribute-Based Encryption (ABE)
•
Integrates traditional RBAC with cryptographic encryption methods.
•
Used to supplement RBAC in cloud environments.
Figure 2-48: Role-based access control and rule-based access control are effective tools to limit
the access to FCI and CUI to only authorized individuals with a lawful government purpose.

Dissemination Controls
•
Regulations for properly disseminating CUI are noted in DoDI 5200.48.
•
Organizations must comply with the CUI dissemination controls that protect both:
•
Physical environment (e.g., facilities, IT assets, work areas), and
•
Data (e.g., email, storage locations).
•
Technical and non-technical mechanisms can be used to ensure that sensitive information is
protected during dissemination, such as:
•
Commercial Off-The-Shelf (COTS) products designed to securely manage information workflows.
•
Encryption to safeguard email.
•
Usage policies and procedures to protect portable devices containing sensitive information.
•
Policies and procedures for employees and visitors when they access sensitive information on premises.
•
Access controls to ensure only authorized users and systems are accessing and disseminating sensitive
information.
•
Protective measures and dissemination controls will be specified in the contract, grants, or other
formal agreements, as applicable.
Dissemination control: Method of managing sensitive information distribution so that it doesn't
spread more widely than allowed by law, regulation, or government -wide policy.
Figure 2-56: DoDI 5200.48 specifies the dissemination requirements for DoD CUI.

Dissemination Controls

Limited Dissemination Controls (NOFORN and REL TO)
•
No Foreign Nationals (NOFORN or NF):
•
For information meeting criteria of:
•
Intelligence Community 710.
•
Intelligence Community Policy Guidance 403.1.
•
May only be applied to unclassified intelligence information and certain nuclear and national disclosure
documents.
•
Information with this marking must not be provided to:
•
Foreign governments (including coalition partners).
•
International organizations.
•
Foreign nationals.
•
Non-U.S. citizens.
•
Releasable To (REL TO):
•
Only applied to CUI that:
•
Is predetermined by the originator to be releasable through established channels.
•
Has an export control.
•
Has a licensing requirement with a foreign disclosure agreement in place.
For more information on general dissemination principles and all the limited dissemination controls:
https://www.archives.gov/cui/registry/limited -dissemination

--- PAGE 123 ---
Decontrolling CUI
•
Decontrolling CUI relieves the authorized holder(s) from complying with CUI handling
requirements but is not equivalent to authorizing it for public release.
•
All DoD CUI must be controlled until authorized for public release in accordance with:
•
DoD Instructions (DoDIs) 5230.09, 5230.29, and 5400.04, or
•
DoD Manual (DoDM) 5400.07.
•
Official DoD information that is not classified or controlled as CUI will also be reviewed prior to
public release in accordance with DoDIs 5230.09 or 5230.29.
Decontrolling occurs when an authorized holder, consistent with CUI regulations and the CUI
Registry, removes safeguarding and dissemination controls from CUI that no longer requires such
controls.
Sources: 32 CFR 2002.18 and NARA CUI Registry
Figure 2-59: When CUI no longer requires safeguarding and dissemination controls, it can be
decontrolled and released to the public.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 111

Decontrolling CUI

Records
•
What qualifies as a record?
•
Reports.
•
Technical documentation.
•
Historical records.
•
What does not qualify as a record?
•
Blank forms.
•
Templates.
•
Materials available from public sources.
Record: Agency records and Presidential papers or Presidential records (or Vice -Presidential), as
those terms are defined in 44 U.S.C. 3301 and 44 U.S.C. 2201 and 2207.
Records are also items created or maintained by a Government contractor, licensee, certificate
holder, or grantee that are subject to the sponsoring agency’s control under the terms of the
entity's agreement with the agency.
Source: NARA CUI Glossary
https://www.archives.gov/cui/registry/cui-glossary.html

Records Management Directives for Retention and Disposal
Retention
•
44 U.S. Code Chapter 33 provides guidance related to the creation, maintenance, and disposition
of Federal records.
•
Government records may only be kept for authorized periods of time.
•
No government records may be destroyed, mutilated, or removed from government custody without
authorization.
•
Some records may be preserved permanently through NARA.
•
Records that are not in active use but cannot be destroyed for some time including both physical
and digital records:
•
Stored appropriately in agency-occupied/owned space.
•
Transferred to Federal Records Center (FRC).
•
Transferred to a NARA-approved commercial storage facility.
Disposal
•
After the retention period has passed, follow the policies and procedures for CUI disposal:
•
44 U.S.C. Chapter 33-Disposal of Records https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/44/chapter-33
•
DoD Mandatory CUI Training https://securityawareness.usalearning.gov/cui/index.html
•
DoD Records Management Program
https://www.esd.whs.mil/Portals/54/Documents/DD/issuances/dodi/501502p.pdf
Figure 2-61: 44 U.S.C. Chapter 33 provides guidance for the creation, maintenance, and
disposition of Federal records.

--- PAGE 126 ---
Media Sanitization
•
Categories of sanitization include:
•
Clear: applies logical techniques to sanitize data in all user -addressable storage locations for protection
against simple non-invasive data recovery techniques.
•
Typically applied through standard Read and Write commands to the storage device:
•
Rewriting with a new value.
•
Using a menu option to reset the device to the factory state (where rewriting is not supported).
•
Purge: applies physical or logical techniques that render target data recovery infeasible using state of the art
laboratory techniques.
•
Destroy: renders Target Data recovery infeasible using state of the art laboratory techniques and results in
the subsequent inability to use the media for storage of data.
•
Cryptographic Erase (CE) is a type of purging.
•
Leverages the encryption of target data by enabling sanitization of the target data’s encryption key.
•
Leaves only the ciphertext remaining on the media, effectively sanitizing the data by preventing read -access.
Media sanitization: The actions taken to render data written on media unrecoverable by both
ordinary and extraordinary means.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms, NIST SP 800-88 Rev 1
Figure 2-62: Sanitization methods can include clearing, purging, and destruction.
Notes
114 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 2: Handling Sensitive Information | Topic B
Media Sanitization

--- PAGE 127 ---
Best Practices for CUI Destruction
•
When destroying CUI, including both hard copy and electronic form, agencies must:
•
Do so in a manner that makes it unreadable, indecipherable, and unrecoverable.
•
Use the specific method prescribed by any relevant law, regulation, or government -wide policy.
•
Follow the guidelines in 32 CFR 2002.14 and NIST SP 800˗88, Revision 1.
•
Follow NARA CUI Notice 2019-03: Destroying Controlled Unclassified Information (CUI) in paper form.
•
Paper-based or shreddable hard copy CUI:
•
Use crosscut shredders which produce particles that are 1 mm x 5 mm (0.04 in. x 0.2 in.) in size (or smaller).
•
Pulverize/disintegrate paper using disintegrator devices equipped with a 3/32 in. (2.4 mm) security screen.
•
Burn to white ash.
•
Electronic media:
•
Clear, purge, or destroy following guidance from NIST SP 800˗88, Revision 1.
•
Use NSA˗evaluated products posted at Media Destruction Guidance site.
•
https://www.nsa.gov/Resources/Media-Destruction-Guidance/
•
Best Approach:
•
Securely store physical and electronic storage media until cleared for disposal.
•
Contract with a third-party company to provide NSA/DoD/NIST-validated destruction services.
•
Document the destruction (generally provided by the third -party company).
B
E

P
R
A

S
Figure 2-63: To properly dispose of or destroy CUI, follow the guidelines in 32 CFR 2002.14 and
NIST SP 800-88, Rev. 1.

ACTIVITY 2-2
Managing Sensitive Information
Scenario
As a CCP, you have been hired to consult with a couple of OSCs and provide guidance on how they
are managing sensitive information properly.
1.
You are providing consulting services for a small business that is planning to
obtain CMMC Certification. The OSC has previously only worked in the
private sector, but wants to start bidding on government contracts. How do
you even start to identify the sensitive information when they don't have any
yet?
A: They might identify RFPs that represent the kind of contracts they will seek. That will help them
consider requirements they must be able to meet and to plan the systems they would use in doing
so. If they are looking to subcontract, they might talk to the Prime contractor. They might also
confer with the contracting officer with the government. They might also confer with a PTAC
(Procurement Technical Assistance Center) for guidance. There are many of these around the
country, and they can provide free advice to organizations looking to do business with the
government.
2.
While you are doing some consulting work for Consolidated Flange Company,
in the Engineering office, you notice that one of the packages awaiting pickup
by the U.S. Postal Service is marked with a CUI cover sheet. You inquire
about this and are told that the package contains some technical drawings
that are being sent to one of the company's subcontractors. Are there any
potential problems with this scenario?
A: If the package is awaiting pickup by the U.S. Postal Service, then presumably what you have seen
is the actual package that will be used to ship the drawings. When the package is moving through
the postal system, no CUI markings should be visible from the outside, as that would provide a
clue to a bad actor that the package contains something they might be interested in intercepting.
An inner package with the proper CUI markings should be completely hidden by outer packaging,
so that no CUI marking is visible on the outer package.
3.
When the OSC asks for guidance on proper management of FCI and CUI,
what are the key things you would advise them to do?
A: Answers will vary, but might include: create a physically-controlled environment with locked doors
and file cabinets; segment the network and restrict access to data using role-based or rule-based
access control; implement encryption technologies in your email system; and sanitize media
before disposal.

Ensuring Compliance
through CMMC
Lesson Time: 3 hours, 55 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you know about the cybersecurity regulations and standards and how to manage
sensitive information, let's turn the focus on the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification
(CMMC) and its role in verifying compliance with those regulations. In this lesson, you will
describe the components of the CMMC Model architecture. You will also define the
CMMC implementation and assessment program and the ecosystem of organizations and
individuals that support it. In addition, you will define the role that self-assessment plays at
some levels of the CMMC program.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Describe the general architecture of the CMMC Model.
•
Describe the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification program and its ecosystem.
•
Identify the obligations of CMMC self-assessments and attestation to compliance with
CMMC.
3

--- PAGE 132 ---
TOPIC A
Describe the CMMC Model Architecture
The Department of Defense (DoD) has worked with partners to develop the Cybersecurity Maturity
Model Certification (CMMC) model and program. In this topic, you will be introduced to the
components of the CMMC Model. These components detail the cybersecurity requirements that
Defense Industrial Base (DIB) organizations must implement to protect sensitive information.
Maturity Model
Maturity model: A model that assesses how institutionalized critical practices and processes are in
an organization and helps determine what capabilities they need in order to continue to improve
their performance.
•
A maturity model measures different characteristics versus a compliance model:
•
Compliance is often seen as checklist driven, yes/no.
•
A maturity model measures how well you perform a checklist practice consistently.
•
Practices must be institutionalized, or ingrained, in the organization operations.
•
The more deeply embedded practices are in the operations, the more likely the:
•
Organization will continue to perform them, even under stress.
•
Outcome will be consistent and high quality.
Figure 3-1: A maturity model promotes practices becoming ingrained in a company's culture.
Notes
120 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic A
Maturity Model

--- PAGE 133 ---
The Development Process of the CMMC Model
•
Office of the Under Secretary of Defense for Acquisition and Sustainment (OUSD A&S) developed
the CMMC Model in collaboration with:
•
DoD stakeholders.
•
University of Affiliated Research Centers (UARCs).
•
Federally Funded Research and Development Centers (FFRDCs).
•
DIB sector entities.
•
Interim versions were released for public review.
•
Initial CMMC Model and interim rule published September 2020.
•
Interim rule became effective November 30, 2020.
•
In March 2021, the DoD started an internal review, informed by
850 public comments in response to the interim rule.
•
CMMC Model 2.0 was released in November 2021.
•
The rulemaking process is expected to take from 9-24 months.
Figure 3-2: The OUSD (A&S) collaborated with other entities and stakeholders to develop the
CMMC Model.

CMMC's Basis in Cybersecurity Standards and Best Practices
•
The CMMC Model is aligned with well-recognized and widely endorsed standards:
•
NIST SP 800˗171
•
NIST SP 800˗172
•
This alignment allows the DoD’s requirements to evolve as NIST updates their
requirements for protecting CUI.
•
These changes to Model 2.0 were made in order to place more emphasis on
FedRAMP and DFARS along with the NIST standards.
