Domain

--- PAGE 138 ---
The CMMC Model Domains
•
Access Control – Manage who accesses your networks and systems.
•
Audit and Accountability – Ensure you create logs and review them frequently.
•
Awareness and Training – Ensure your people are trained appropriately.
•
Configuration Management – Ensure your baselines and other configurations are kept up to date.
•
Identification and Authentication – Know who is requesting access and authenticate appropriately.
•
Incidence Response – Be able to recover once an incident occurs.
•
Maintenance – Keep your systems up to date and patched.
•
Media Protection – Ensure mobile media is protected against theft or loss.
•
Personnel Security – Manage risks to your environment by insiders.
•
Physical Protection – Employ physical protection mechanisms to prevent access to physical devices.
•
Risk Assessment – Have a process for identifying and managing enterprise risk.
•
Security Assessment – Independently verify your security posture.
•
System and Communications Protection – Manage security tools and processes related to system security.
•
System and Information Integrity – Monitor and protect the information system against malicious content.
Figure 3-7: These are the 14 domains of the CMMC Model.

Scenario
As a CCP, your job requires you to ensure that organizations adhere to requirements outlined in
various documents, including CMMC documentation and government regulations and policies.
While you certainly don't have to memorize all of the information in these documents, you need to
become familiar with their content, understand how they work together to serve as your guidelines
for organizations that handle sensitive government information, have them available for your

practice this skill by looking up information in the CMMC Model Overview and the CMMC

Why does the CMMC Model have multiple levels?

threats, from foundational to expert.

Note that RA.L2-3.11.1 uses the term "Periodically". Does that term mean some specific period in
CMMC, such as annually or quarterly?
c)
Look up the term in the CMMC Glossary and Acronyms document.
What is the definition of "periodically"?
A: The definition is adapted from the Oxford Dictionary. To paraphrase, periodically simply means
regular intervals. Each organization is allowed to define the period that is best for their business,
but for CMMC practices that period can't be longer than a year.

Return to the CMMC Model.

TOPIC B
Define the CMMC Program and Its Ecosystem
Now that you have learned the requirements for each Level in the CMMC Model, we can turn our
focus to the implementation and assessment program. In this topic, you will learn how those
cybersecurity requirements will be implemented in the DoD contracting process and how
organizations will be assessed. You will also be introduced to the people and organizations in the
CMMC ecosystem.
Rationale for the Introduction of the CMMC Program
•
Under FAR 52.204-21 and DFARS 252.204-7012, companies in the DIB are required to:
•
Self-attest to their compliance with cybersecurity requirements.
•
List gaps on a Plan of Actions and Milestones (POA&M).
•
DoD wanted a mechanism to ensure consistency with cybersecurity compliance that is
commensurate with the nature of the sensitive information.
•
CMMC is designed to provide increased assurance to the DoD that:
•
DIB contractors can adequately protect sensitive unclassified information.
•
The DoD wants to shift to security being viewed as foundational to acquisition.
•
Not traded along with cost, schedule, and performance.
Figure 3-16: The CMMC Model is designed to provide assurance of cybersecurity compliance to
the DoD.
Notes
138 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic B
Rationale for the
Introduction of the
CMMC Program

--- PAGE 151 ---
Increased Expectations with the CMMC Program
•
CMMC assesses contractors’ compliance in meeting their responsibilities in handling FCI/CUI.
•
Must protect the FCI/CUI received from, or created for, the government for a defense contract.
•
CMMC requires flow down of cybersecurity requirements from Prime contractors to subcontractors.
•
Need to clearly define:
•
The nature of the data to be exchanged.
•
How it will be handled in their respective environments.
•
Follow all regulations for FCI/CUI handling and records management.
•
In Levels 2 & 3, CMMC builds upon existing regulations by adding third-party verification of:
•
Compliance with cybersecurity requirements.
•
Institutionalization of cybersecurity practices in DIB company culture.
Figure 3-17: CMMC verifies implementation and institutionalization of cybersecurity practices.

CMMC Benefits for Contractors
•
Better overall cyber hygiene.
•
Increased ability to continue mature processes across lines of businesses.
•
Heightened awareness of where sensitive data resides on internal networks/systems.
•
And last, but certainly not least, the ability to bid on DoD contracts.

CMMC Benefits for

--- PAGE 153 ---
Key Aspects of the CMMC 2.0 Framework
•
Streamlined Model
•
Ensures that the most critical requirements are included by reducing the model from 5 levels to 3 levels.
•
Aligns to the widely accepted National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) cybersecurity standards.
•
Reliable Assessments
•
Reduces the assessment costs for Level 1 organizations.
•
Allows Level 1 and some Level 2 organizations to demonstrate compliance through self-assessments.
•
Allows a small subset of Level 2 organizations to demonstrate compliance through self -assessments.
•
Increases accountability of third-party assessors.
•
Increased oversight of adherence to professional and ethical standards.
•
Flexible Implementation
•
Fosters a spirit of collaboration between the DoD and the DIB in building a culture of cyber resilience.
•
Allows organizations to use time-bound Plan of Action and Milestones (POA&Ms) in limited circumstances.
•
Only possible after meeting a minimum score.
•
Not all requirements will be allowed on the POA&M list.
•
Increases the flexibility and speed of certification.
•
Allows waivers to entire CMMC requirement on a very limited basis in mission critical instances.
•
Only with senior DoD leadership approval.
Figure 3-19: The three key aspects of the CMMC 2.0 program requirements are a streamlined
model, reliable assessments, and flexible implementation.
Notes

Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic B
Key Aspects of the
CMMC 2.0 Framework

--- PAGE 154 ---
CMMC 2.0 Rulemaking and Timeline
Rulemaking Timeline
•
On November 4, 2021, CMMC 2.0 was released to the public.
•
The DoD is pursuing official rules in two federal regulations.
•
32 CFR will codify CMMC as a program.
•
48 CFR will codify CMMC requirements and allow DFARS 52.204
-
7021 to be the final rule.
•
Both rules will have a Congressional review and a public comment period for stakeholder input.
•
The rulemaking process is expected to take 9 to 24 months.
In the Interim
•
The CMMC pilot programs for assessments are suspended.
•
No CMMC requirement will be included in DoD solicitations.
•
Contracting organizations are encouraged to strengthen their cybersecurity practices.
•
Project Spectrum is a platform to help the DIB assess and build their cybersecurity capabilities.
•
Organizations should begin to work toward compliance to CMMC 2.0 practices.
•
The DoD is considering incentives for organizations who voluntarily obtain CMMC certification.
•
Specifics will be detailed during the rulemaking period.
•
More information can be found at
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/About/
.
Figure 3-20: The rulemaking and timeline for CMMC Model 2.0.

--- PAGE 155 ---
CMMC Level Determined by DoD Contracts
•
The CMMC Model is composed of three different,
yet cumulative, Levels.
•
Solicitations and Requests for Information (RFIs) will indicate
the required CMMC Level.
•
Based on the sensitivity of the information to be shared with the contractor.
•
FCI requires Level 1.
•
CUI requires Level 2 or Level 3.
•
A company may choose any of the Levels they feel are correct for their different lines of business.
•
NO requirement to achieve CMMC Level 1 prior to moving to any other CMMC Level.
•
CMMC Level 2 certification will need to be achieved before moving on to Level 3.
Figure 3-21: The contracts an organization desires to bid on will determine which CMMC Level
they seek.

CMMC Assessment Requirements
•
CMMC 2.0 adds a verification component with respect to cybersecurity requirements.
•
Tiered assessments based on the sensitivity of the information provided the contractor.
•
Foundational:
FCI not critical to national security – self-assessment.
•
Advanced:
CUI critical to national security – third-party assessment.
CUI not critical to national security – self-assessment.
•
Expert:
CUI in high priority/most critical defense programs – government-led assessments.

Self-Assessments vs. Third-Party Assessments
Self Assessments
•
Level 1 and a small subset of Level 2.
•
For OSCs who handle FCI only.
•
For OSCs who handle CUI that is not critical
national security information.
•
OSCs assess their compliance to
cybersecurity practices.
•
Conducted annually.
•
Senior company official required to sign off
to attest to the self-assessment.
•
Self-assessment scores and affirmations
posted to the Supplier Performance Risk
System (SPRS).
Third-Party Assessments
•
Level 2.
•
For OSCs who handle CUI critical to national
security.
•
C3PAOs assess the OSC’s compliance with
cybersecurity practices.
•
Conducted triennially.
•
C3PAO provides an assessment report to the
DoD.
*Pending final ruling: OSCs requiring a
government-led Level 3 assessment will need
a third-party Level 2 assessment first.
Figure 3-23: All organizations can self-assess their compliance to the 17 practices at Level 1;
however, organizations aiming for Level 2 and higher will need a third-party assessment.

CMMC Benefit – Independent Third-Party Assessment
•
Third-party assessments:
•
Are independent.
•
Are impartial.
•
Provide validation of the contractor's cybersecurity protections.
•
Allow potential issues to be identified and remediated before contract.
•
Critical mission of The Cyber AB:
•
Consistency among assessors.
•
Assessors experienced/trained in the CMMC Model and Assessment Process.
•
Certified CMMC Assessors will work for C3PAOs.
•
C3PAOs will contract with members of the DIB for a CMMC Assessment.
•
Assessors will NOT be allowed to assess an OSC they consulted with.
Figure 3-24: Independent and impartial third-party assessments validate the contractor's
cybersecurity protections.

--- PAGE 159 ---
CMMC Certification - Levels 2 and 3
•
Certification is awarded to OSCs who undergo and pass an assessment led by an external team.
•
OSCs who self-attest are only affirming their compliance and do not receive official certification.
•
Certification is awarded:
•
At the entity level, not based on project or contract.
•
On a 3-year, renewable basis.
•
For a company to receive their CMMC Certification at Level 2, they need to:
•
Contract with a C3PAO for the CMMC Assessment.
•
Coordinate with C3PAO for logistics and any preparatory artifacts the assessor requires.
•
Facilitate the on-site portion of the assessment.
•
Meetings.
•
Interviews.
•
Artifacts.
•
Successfully demonstrate all practices for their desired CMMC Level.
•
For a company to receive their CMMC Certification at Level 3, they will
need to work with the government-led team, once the Assessment Guide
and assessment processes are defined.
Figure 3-25: The basic requirements for a CMMC Level 2 Certification starts with contracting with
a C3PAO to conduct the CMMC Assessment.

The OSC's Path to CMMC Level 2 Certification
1.
Understand the CMMC requirements.
DoD CIO CMMC Model site :
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Model/
2.
Identify the scope (Enterprise, Organizational
Unit, or Program Enclave).
3.
Identify the desired CMMC Level.
4.
Conduct a gap analysis.
5.
Close any identified gaps.
6.
Find a C3PAO in the CMMC Marketplace to
perform a Level 2 assessment.
7.
Assessment is conducted by the C3PAO’s
certified Assessment Team.
8.
C3PAO submits Assessment Report to the DoD.
9.
When approved, CMMC Certification is issued
to the OSC.
•
Certification is valid for 3 years.
•
OSC expected to maintain compliance with
the CMMC Model throughout this time.

The CMMC Ecosystem

Authorities
•
Office of the Undersecretary of Defense for Acquisition and Sustainment (OUSD A&S)
•
DoD office that procures what is necessary for national defense.
•
Owner of the CMMC Model and Assessment Guides.

-
AB)
•
Non
-
profit organization that operationalizes CMMC assessments and training.
•
Must achieve and maintain ISO/IEC 17011 accreditation requirements.
•

Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic B
Authorities

--- PAGE 164 ---
Roles and Responsibilities – Authorities
•
The OUSD (A&S) will:
•
Maintain the CMMC Model and Assessment Guides.
•
Rely on CMMC certification to make decisions about contracts.
•
Build on existing regulations (i.e., DFARS 252.204-7012) to add
verification of cybersecurity compliance.
•
Oversee the CMMC Assessment ecosystem.
•
Approve all Cyber AB conflict of interest policies for the ecosystem.
•
The Cyber AB will:
•
Build an ecosystem to implement the CMMC Model and program.
•
Certify CMMC organizations (C3PAOs, RPOs, CAICO)
•
Certify individuals performing services (RPs)
•
Provide additional guidance and documentation, such as:
•
On The Cyber AB website https://cyberab.org/.
•
In the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) documentation.
•
The future CAICO will:
•
Certify CCPs, CCAs, and CCIs.
•
Oversee LPP and LTP organizations responsible for curricula and training.
•
Manage the creation and delivery of CMMC exams.

Provisional Assessors (PA)
Purpose
•
Provisionally trained to conduct Assessments at

•

about the Assessment process to:
•

and Pathfinders.
PA Minimum Qualifications
•
Must meet one of the following:
•
10+ years of experience conducting evidence-based
assessments in cyber or other technology field,
including ISO, FedRAMP, CMMI, RMM CERT, DIBCAC or
similar, or
•
20+ years experience as a consultant or leader in
cybersecurity and qualifying for DOD 8570 IAM Level III
certification.
Assessor Prerequisites
•
Complete the CCP application process.
•
Complete the formal AB-provided PA training.
•
Engage with clients through a C3PAO.

Satisfy all CCP and CCA requirements found at
https://cyberab.org/CMMC-Ecosystem/Ecosystem-
roles/Assessors
Transition from PA to CCA
•
Pass the CCP exam within 6 months of its
availability.
•
Pass the CCA exam within 6 months of its
availability.
Benefits
•
CMMC-AB trained with provisional training
materials.
•
As part of a C3PAO team, available to implement
mock CMMC Pilots and Pathfinders.
•
Available to engage directly with DIB contractors

A: Organizations must be in compliance with CMMC before they will be awarded a contract. With the
phased implementation, organizations already under contract will need to seek certification or risk
losing their contracts when due for renewal. Other legal liabilities, including the False Claims Act,
might also be a consequence of being out of compliance with CMMC.

Define Self-Assessments
With the introduction of CMMC 2.0, some organizations will perform self-assessments to affirm
their compliance with the CMMC Model. In this topic, you will define the obligations of self-
assessment.
Accountability
•
Companies have responsibilities.
•
Protect the environment.
•
Protect their employees.
•
Protect the interest of their shareholders or stakeholders.
•
Most people are not experts in all aspects of running a company so they:
•
Create and hire HR experts in local, state, and federal labor laws.
•
Hire OSHA experts in occupational safety.
•
Hire defense contracting experts.
•
Cybersecurity should not be any different.
•
Hire experts to keep your company aligned with cybersecurity rules.
•
Good for your company as a competitive differentiator.
•
Good for the country as you protect sensitive information.

History of DIB Cybersecurity Compliance Requirements
•
Under FAR 52.204-21 and DFARS 252.204-7012:
•
Contractors implicitly self-attested they met requirements by signing a federal contract.
•
No verification of compliance.
•
Under DFARS 252.204-7019:
•
Contractors must submit a NIST SP 800˗171 self-assessment score to the DoD.
•
Allowed a plan of action for any gaps.
•
No verification that plan to close gaps is implemented.

Interim Rule Self-Assessments and SPRS
•
September 29, 2020, DoD Interim Final Rule (DFARS 252.204˗7019˗D041):
•
Supplements CMMC program with a DoD Assessment Methodology.
•
Amends DFARS to achieve a two-step review process of contractors.
•
Mandates contractor certification through NIST SP 800˗171 DoD Assessment Methodology.
•
Until CMMC Certification program is fully implemented.
•
Contractors must have a current security assessment on file prior to contract award.
•
In effect, beginning November 30, 2020.
•
“Current” means not more than three years old, unless solicitation specifies an exception.
•
Conduct a self-assessment.
•
Submit the summary score to the Supplier Performance Risk System (SPRS).
•
URL: https://www.sprs.csd.disa.mil/
•
Exceptions:
•
Those exclusively dealing with COTS.
•
Those who do not store, process, generate, transmit or access CUI.
•
Those who believe they are exempt should be prepared to
demonstrate justification.

•

-
7012 that is based on trust.
•

-

Government

led assessment as verification of compliance.

Self-Assessment Under CMMC 2.0
•
Contractors are required to conduct a self-assessment for Level 1 practice requirements.
•
A limited number of Level 2 assessments might also be conducted as self -assessments.
•
Senior company official must sign to attest the accuracy of the self -assessment score.
•
Must be conducted annually.
•
Self-assessment scores must be reported to SPRS.
Self-assessment: Assessing your organization’s compliance to the practice requirements.
Self-attestation: Making an official declaration that something complies with regulations without
independent substantiating evidence.

--- PAGE 194 ---
The Christian Doctrine
•
Mandatory procurement statutes/regulations shall
be read into federal contracts.
•
By operation of law, even if not explicitly stated.
•
Standard for inclusion is "significantly or deeply ingrained
strand of public procurement."
•
Exception to:
•
General rule that government must put vendors on
notice of contract requirements.
•
Standard commercial contracting practices and contract
interpretation principles.
•
Rationale
•
Procurement policies set by higher authority cannot be
avoided or evaded (deliberately or negligently) by lower
government officials.
•
Result of court case
•
G.L. Christian & Associates v. United States, 312 F.2d 418
(Ct. Cl. 1963)
Figure 3-58: Based on a FAR-related court case, the Christian Doctrine states that mandatory
procurement clauses are inherent in all federal contracts.
Notes
182 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic C
The Christian Doctrine

--- PAGE 195 ---
The False Claims Act
•
Individuals/companies that knowingly defraud the government can be held liable.
•
3X the contract value plus a penalty of $11,000+/claim.
•
Sometimes called the Lincoln Law because implemented during the Civil War.
•
Qui tam provision:
•
Claims can be brought by non-government people on behalf
of the government.
•
Informally called whistleblowers.
•
Person filing gets 15-30% of the damages.
•
Vast majority of FCA actions initiated by whistleblowers.
•
Claims typically involve fraud in military spending and health care.
•
Includes claims that services are rendered in accordance with the contract.
Figure 3-59: The False Claims Act can be used to penalize contractors who are not actually in
compliance with cybersecurity regulations.

Christian Doctrine, False Claims Act, and Cybersecurity
•
DoD contracts
•
Include FAR 52.204˗21 or DFARS 252.204˗7012.
•
Even if not included explicitly, can still be read in under the Christian Doctrine.
•
If a contractor is not actually in compliance, they are at risk for a False Claims Act.
•
Civil Cyber-Fraud Initiative
•
Launched on October 6, 2021, by the U.S. Department of Justice to protect against increasing cyber threats.
•
Will utilize the False Claims Act to pursue cybersecurity-related fraud by government contractors.

https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/deputy-attorney-general-lisa-o-monaco-announces-new-civil-cyber-fraud-initiative

ACTIVITY 3-3
Identifying the Risks of Inaccurate Self-
Assessments
Data File
C:\093200Data\Ensuring Compliance through CMMC\Aerojet Rocketdyne Case Study.docx
Scenario
Self-assessment provides a way for an organization working under contract with the government to
attest to their cybersecurity compliance. As a CCP, it is critical to understand the importance of
accurately self-assessing and the risks of inaccurate reporting those results.
1.
Read a case study where self-assessment failed.
a)
Navigate to C:\093200Data\Ensuring Compliance through CMMC folder.
b)
Open Aerojet Rocketdyne Case Study.docx
2.
What is the charge against Aerojet Rocketdyne?
A: Brian Markus, the former senior director of Cybersecurity, Compliance, and Controls at Aerojet
Rocketdyne, charged the company with violating the False Claims Act. Markus claimed that the
company fraudulently entered into contracts with the federal government knowing that they were
not in compliance with the regulations in 48 CFR 252.204-7012 and 48 CFR 1852.204-76.
3.
Which of the arguments that Aerojet Rocketdyne attempted to use in an effort
to dismiss the case surprised you?
A: Answers may vary. The claim that cybersecurity regulations were not the core of the contract was
a surprise. And the claim that the government's constant changes to the regulations meant they
didn't really expect full compliance was a shock. Both indicate a wholesale rejection of the
importance of the contractor's responsibility to protect the sensitive government information that is
provided and created under a defense contract.
4.
Explain the court's ruling and the ramifications it has for self-attestation.
A: The Court has not issued a final ruling. They have rejected the motion to dismiss, which indicates
that organizations that claim self-attestation to cybersecurity regulations and requirements are
legally obligated to be in compliance and can be held accountable under the False Claims Act.
5.
What are the consequences with the court case still being unsettled six years
after the noncompliance was reported?
A: Unless Aerojet Rocketdyne has changed their business practices, the sensitive data is still
potentially unprotected.
Note: On July 8, 2022, the Department of Justice announced in a press release
that the Aerojet Rocketdyne lawsuit had been settled (https://
www.justice.gov/opa/pr/aerojet-rocketdyne-agrees-pay-9-million-resolve-false-
claims-act-allegations-cybersecurity).
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 187
Lesson 3: Ensuring Compliance through CMMC | Topic C

of the CMMC Model architecture, you defined the model hierarchy including the domains, practices,

organizations and individuals that supports the program's implementation. The last topic covers the
obligations of self-assessments and how they are used to affirm an OSC's compliance to the CMMC
Model.

--- PAGE 203 ---
Performing CCP
Responsibilities
Lesson Time: 1 hour, 25 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you are familiar with the CMMC Model, you can turn your focus to the role of a
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) in securing the nation's defense information.
Understanding the responsibilities and the guidelines for ethical behavior in this role will
help you become a valuable part of the CMMC ecosystem.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Identify a Certified CMMC Professional's responsibilities.
•
Demonstrate ethical behavior required of a CCP as outlined in the Code of Professional
Conduct.
4

--- PAGE 204 ---
TOPIC A
Identify Responsibilities of the CCP
For some organizations, obtaining CMMC Certification will mean having to adopt a new,
cybersecurity-oriented mindset. Cybersecurity cannot continue to be an afterthought; it needs to be
baked into the organization's DNA. This means it should be adopted across the entire organization
and considered from the early stages of every project. As a Certified CMMC Professional (CCP),
you will likely be influencing not only the technical aspects of the organization's network, but also
operational and business decisions. In this topic, you will learn about the responsibilities of a CCP.
Professional Conduct
•
CCPs must adhere to the CMMC Code of Professional Conduct (CoPC), which:
•
Outlines expectations of Certified CMMC Professionals acting on behalf of the DoD and The Cyber AB.
•
Identifies responsibilities governing the work performed.
•
Places responsibility to the CMMC process and your country ahead of personal objectives.
•
Specifies that a CCP must comply with the following guiding principles:
•
Professionalism.
•
Objectivity.
•
Confidentiality.
•
Proper Use of Methods.
•
Information Integrity.
•
As employees of C3PAOs, CCPs are also required to follow professional conduct
requirements from:
•
ISO/IEC 17020:2012, including ethical concepts such as:
•
Impartiality and independence.
•
Confidentiality.
•
DoD specific guidance, policy, and updates to existing regulations, addressing issues such as:
•
Non-disclosure of non-public information.
•
Personal conflicts of interest.
CCP
Figure 4-1: CCPs are bound by the terms of the CMMC Code of Professional Conduct (CoPC), as
well as ISO/IEC and DoD conduct requirements, which help to maintain expectations of ethical
conduct in the duties performed by the CCP on behalf of the DoD and The Cyber AB.

ACTIVITY 4-1
Identifying the Responsibilities of a CCP
Scenario
CCPs may be pulled in many different directions and must always strive to use good judgment in
matters of ethics. In this activity, you will consider some of the types of ethical questions a CCP
might have to consider.
1.
When you were preparing to become a CCP, you were working for Better
Industries Inc., helping them identify gaps in their effort to obtain CMMC Level
2 Certification. After obtaining your CCP certification, you left Better Industries
and took a position with the Best Group, a CMMC Third-Party Assessment
Organization (C3PAO). Now, Better Industries is undergoing the Assessment
process, and the Best Group that will be managing Better Industries' CMMC
Level 2 Assessment. Since you are no longer employed by Better Industries,
you reason that it is acceptable for you to serve on the Assessment Team. Is
your reasoning correct?
A: No, it isn't proper for you to participate on the Assessment Team. This would present a conflict of
interest.
2.
Narrow Valley Industries (NVI) obtains more than half of its business through
government contracts. Since compliance with government regulations and
policies is so important to their business, they have decided it would be
helpful to have someone in their organization who is thoroughly familiar with
CMMC. They have offered a position in their company to your colleague,
Dave Farragut, who is a CCP. Will Dave be able to retain his CCP certification
if he accepts the position at NVI?
A: Yes, he may continue to retain his certification and serve as a cybersecurity team member for NVI.
Of course, he would not be able to serve in any way that would cause a conflict of interest. For
example, he would not be able to serve on an Assessment Team evaluating NVI. Furthermore,
NVI might not want Dave acting as a consultant to other companies, but that's a matter between
NVI and Dave. Dave will still have to be mindful of any such requests, though, as any
arrangements that appear to be in violation of the Code of Professional Conduct could put his
CCP certification in jeopardy.
3.
As a CCP and external consultant, you have been helping the Capstan
Company perform a gap analysis as they prepare for CMMC Level 2
Certification. Because you are also knowledgeable in computer networking
and cybersecurity, they would like you to help them redesign their network to
create a "secure enclave" in one wing of their building where all government
projects will be worked on, isolated from people and systems in other parts of
the building. As a CCP functioning as an external consultant, are you
permitted to provide this type of technical consulting?
A: Yes. As a CCP working as a consultant, it is assumed that you may have considerable influence
over technical aspects of your clients' network, as well as on operation and business decisions.

In order for a cybersecurity cultural change to occur, you must have ethical practices to ensure that

Code of Professional Conduct.

Required agreement effected by and between The Cyber AB and all CMMC ecosystem members.

Defines the expectations, responsibilities, and guidelines for work performed under the terms of
the agreement.

Objectivity

CoPC Practices (2 of 2)

--- PAGE 219 ---
The CoPC and Ethical Violations
•
Ethical, or other violations, of the CoPC may result in administrative action.
•
Examples of unethical conduct may include:
•
Prior relationship with the OSC or key members of the OSC, which may impede objectivity.
•
Failure to consider presented Evidence in accordance with CMMC Assessment Guides and
CMMC Assessment Process (CAP).
•
Failure to comply with evidentiary rules for characterization of OSC Evidence.
•
Guiding, or providing leading questions, during interview sessions with the intent of “helping” the
organization.
•
Discussion and/or removal of OSC Assessment data, artifacts, and/or notes.
•
In the event of a reported violation:
•
Seek first to clarify the alleged unethical conduct:
•
Context.
•
Circumstances.
•
Extenuating factors.
•
If clarification is unsuccessful, submit CoPC violation report to The Cyber AB.
•
The Cyber AB will conduct investigation, as appropriate.
•
Investigation may result in a corrective action (e.g., warning, remediation, suspension, denial/termination of
CMMC credentials, etc.).

Professional Conduct
Best Practices

--- PAGE 221 ---
Conflicts of Interest
•
Occurs when an individual has multiple interests (financial, organizational, etc.) and acting in the
best interest of one could serve to work against the best interests of another.
•
Conflicts of interest (or the perception of them) undermine objectivity.
•
For example:
•
Family member worked/works at the OSC.
•
Prior work history with the OSC.
•
Previous CMMC work in a different CCP role.
•
If you consulted for an OSC, you can't be on their Assessment Team.
Conflict of interest: A situation where an individual has competing obligations that
might lead to compromises in decision-making and damage to the integrity of the
outcome.

Guidelines for Ethical Behavior
•
Act ethically and professionally:
•

Assessment Guides?

•

•
Is there an existing relationship with the OSC (business or personal) that may
introduce bias, or otherwise impede objectivity?
•
Can you demonstrate how objectivity is maintained to minimize the impact?
•
Follow ethical practices regarding legal obligations:
•
Are you meeting contract requirements?
•
Are you complying with all Non-Disclosure Agreements (NDAs)?

Looking Up Ethical Guidelines in the CoPC
Data File

Scenario
As a CCP, you will be responsible for reading, signing, and adhering to the CMMC Code of
Professional Conduct (CoPC). In this activity, you will examine the CoPC and refer to it to answer
questions about the behavior expected of a CCP (and others who the CoPC applies to).
1.
Open the CMMC Code of Professional Conduct, which has been provided in
your course data files, and get acquainted with the general types of
information it contains.
a)
In your course data files at C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources, open the PDF file named Code of
Professional Conduct_V2.4.
b)
Examine the table of contents.
The document contains sections named Introduction, Guiding Principles, Practices, and
Implementation. The appendix contains a list of terms, and the document concludes with a signature
page.
2.
Identify the purpose of the CoPC and who it applies to.
a)
Scroll to the Introduction, and briefly examine the purpose of the document and the lists of people it
applies to.
The CoPC:
•
Applies to people who are issued credentials by The Cyber AB, are registered practitioners or
providers, training publishers, industry working group members, and others.
•
Sets "performance standards by which the roles of the CMMC ecosystem will be held
accountable, and the procedures for addressing violations of those performance standards."
•
Is intended to help "members in situations where we may be asked to compromise our values
and integrity."
3.
Identify the guiding principles underlying the CoPC.
a)
Scroll to the Guiding Principles section, and briefly examine the principles that are outlined.
4.
Refer to Section 2 Guiding Principles as you answer the following question.
If you find yourself in an unavoidable situation that leads to the appearance of
a conflict of interest, how should you proceed?
A: You should always consider how any situation might lead to a real or perceived conflict of interest.
Section 2.2 Objectivity states that, "In the case where a perceived or management conflict may be
present, document and describe the conflict to all affected parties and secure agreement to
continue."
5.
Identify what is covered in the Practices section.
a)
Scroll to the Practices section.
b)
Briefly scan the text in this section to get a general idea of what it covers.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 211
Lesson 4: Performing CCP Responsibilities | Topic B
Activity

--- PAGE 224 ---
This section covers mandatory expectations for everyone who plays a role in the CMMC ecosystem
(which, as a CCP, will include you). Subsections cover "Professionalism", "Confidentiality",
"Adherence to Materials and Methods", "Information Integrity", "Respect for Intellectual Property",
and "Lawful and Ethical Practices".
6.
Refer to Section 3 Practices as you answer the following question.
In the past, you have performed services for Develetech Corporation. They
have requested the organization you work for (a C3PAO) to conduct their
CMMC Assessment. You are not clear on whether the work you have done
for them would constitute a conflict of interest. Identify where you can find any
statements in the Code of Professional Conduct that might help you
determine what would constitute a conflict of interest.
A: Section 3.1 Professionalism, Item 9 states, "Under no circumstances are credentialed or
registered individuals permitted to conduct a certified assessment, or participate on a certified
assessment team, if they have also served as a consultant to prepare the organization for that
assessment. Consulting is defined as 'providing direct assistance to the creation of processes,
training, and technology required to meet the intent of CMMC controls and processes.'" If the work
you performed for Develetech previously had nothing to do with the processes, training, and
technology that would now help them to meet CMMC requirements, there might not be a conflict
of interest. However, even when you know there isn't any conflict of interest, it is always wise to
consider when there might be an
appearance
of such. If you identify potential for so much as a
perceived
conflict of interest, you may opt to address it beforehand (see Step 4 of this activity) or
simply choose not to take on the assignment.
7.
Scan the remainder of the document.
a)
Scroll through the remainder of the document, briefly examining other sections that are covered.
b)
Examine the Signature Page section.
Each person covered by the CoPC is responsible for signing this page. Before you do so, make sure
you read the entire document carefully, and consider the responsibilities you are taking on.
Periodically review the CoPC so you are always clear on what is being expected of you.
8.
You suspect that one of your colleagues might be in violation of the CoPC.
How does the CoPC recommend that you deal with such a situation?
A: You should first take this matter up directly with your colleague. If your colleague does not resolve
the problem, then you should report the problem to The Cyber AB. Section 4.1 Response for
Potential Violations, Item 1 recommends, "When observing colleagues making choices that are in
violation of the CoPC, you should privately request clarification or offer help rectify the violation.
However, if clarification or help is not effective to resolve the issue, or if you believe corrective
action is required to resolve the situation, then it is expected that you will submit a report."
9.
How would you handle this suspected violation with your co-worker? You
notice that your co-worker, Todd, has been meeting with the OSC, after
business hours each day of the assessment. Todd is in charge of reviewing
the policies, processes, and procedures being used as evidence for some of
the Assessment Objectives, and you noticed that all have been updated in the
evenings. You have a feeling Todd has been working with the OSC to update
their policies, processes, and procedures.
A: The first step you should take is to casually approach Todd and say "Hey, Todd, I noticed the
OSC's documentation has been updated every night, and they look way different than what I saw
during the day. What do you think about that?" Now, if you have witnessed Todd being cut a
check by the OSC that was most decidedly not for the assessment that is taking place, and Todd
has a temper, it is not recommended that you reach out to Todd regarding the check or his
activities after work.

--- PAGE 227 ---
Scoping Certification and
Assessment Boundaries
Lesson Time: 5 hours, 15 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you are familiar with the overall CMMC Model and the ecosystem, you can turn
your focus to learning about the core driver of the model, the sensitive information, and the
job tasks that you will perform as a Certified CMMC Professional (CCP). In this lesson, you
will learn about cataloging the sensitive information in an organization, determining how the
sensitive information moves, and identifying the systems that are in scope for the
Assessment as a result of this movement.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Use the CMMC Assessment Scope documentation.
•
Take inventory of the OSC's environment to prepare to identify the scope boundaries
for a CMMC Assessment.
•
Determine how sensitive information moves into, within, and out of the OSC
environment, which will prepare you to determine where protections must be
implemented.
•
Establish appropriate scope boundaries for a CMMC Assessment.
•
Apply separation techniques to limit scope.
5

--- PAGE 228 ---
TOPIC A
Use the CMMC Assessment Scope
Documentation
As a CCP working as a consultant with an Organization Seeking Certification (OSC), you should be
familiar with the CMMC Assessment Scope documentation in order to provide guidance on
determining which assets are in scope and will be assessed against the CMMC practices.
Scope
•
In-scope components of the OSC environment:
•
Will be evaluated through the CMMC Assessment process.
•
Are in scope because they handle FCI, CUI, or other sensitive information, or are not logically or physically
isolated from systems that handle FCI, CUI, or other sensitive information.
•
Must be protected from cybersecurity threats.
•
Benefits of carefully determining what is in scope include:
•
Helping to ensure the OSC is protecting every system they really need to protect.
•
Helping to ensure the OSC is not wasting resources building up security on systems that really don’t need it.
•
Enables organizations to consider how they might redesign their systems and business processes to limit
scope, when possible, thereby:
•
Limiting their implementation costs.
•
Improving security by reducing the attack surface.
Scope: The scale or extent of what will be evaluated for conformity, which
includes those assets (people, facilities, technology) within the OSC’s
environment that are targeted for CMMC Assessment because they interact
with sensitive information—for example, by containing it, touching it in
transit, or operating on the same network as it.
Scoping: The process of setting or determining the scope.
Figure 5-1: The process of establishing scope is an important task for a CCP serving as a
consultant to an OSC, and it is also performed by the CMMC Assessment Team.
Notes
216 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic A
Scope

--- PAGE 229 ---
High Level Scoping
•
Documented within the:
•
OSC asset inventory.
•
SSP.
•
Network diagrams of the OSC.
Headquarters (HQ) Organization: The legal entity that will be delivering services or products
under the terms of a DoD contract.
Host Unit: The specific people, procedures, and technology within an HQ Organization that
would be applied to the DoD contract and that are to be considered as the OSC for CMMC
Assessment purposes.
Supporting Organizations/Units: The people, procedures, and technology external to the HQ
Organization that support the Host Unit. The affiliated assets may need to be included as part
of the CMMC Assessment Scope.
•
Directly linked to the Systems Security Plan (SSP) and/or associated practices that protect
FCI and/or CUI.
•
The HQ Organization itself could be the OSC, or it could designate a Host Unit as the OSC.
•
Subcontractors.
•
External service providers (ESPs) /Third-Party Providers.
•
(e.g., AWS, Microsoft 365).
•
May also include administratively separate entities within the same organization.
•
(e.g., IV&V support, Human Resources, Training).
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Figure 5-2: High level scoping involves an understanding of whether the OSC is the entire HQ
organization or a host unit within the HQ organization, as well as knowledge of what supporting
organizations/units will be included in the CMMC Assessment Scope.
Notes

Guidance on Level 1 Scoping Activities
In order to appropriately scope at Level 1, the contractor must consider all people, technology,
facilities, and external service providers involved with the processing, storage, or transmission of FCI
within their environment.
•
People
•
Employees, contractors, vendors, and external service provider personnel.
•
Technology
•
Servers, client computers, mobile devices, network appliances (e.g., firewalls, switches, APs,
and routers), VoIP devices, applications, virtual machines, and database systems.
•
Facilities
•
Physical office locations, satellite offices, server rooms, datacenters, manufacturing plants, and
secured rooms.
•
External Service Providers (ESPs)
•
External people, technology, or facilities that the organization uses, including cloud services, co -
located data centers, hosting providers, and managed security service providers.
ESP
Figure 5-6: The scoping activities for Level 1 involve considering the people, technology,
facilities, and external service providers that process, store, or transmit FCI.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 221
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic A
Guidance on Scoping
Activities

--- PAGE 234 ---
Cloud-Based External Service Providers
•
Cloud-based environments (or virtualized environments) provide an excellent opportunity for cost
savings, both in terms of capital and operational expenses.
•
Provides convenient, on-demand network access to a shared pool of configurable computing resources (e.g.,
networks, servers, storage, applications, and services) that can be rapidly provisioned and released with
minimal management effort or service provider interaction (NIST SP 800-145).
•
Facilitates sharing of leading-edge technologies and services which could subsequently help to offset
the scope/cost of the OSC's CMMC Assessment.
•
Provides implementation of some cybersecurity controls.
Figure 5-7: Cloud-based External Service Providers are common in business today because they
help save costs.

Level 1: FCI Assets
CMMC Level 1
FCI Assets process, store, or transmit FCI.
•
FCI can be used by an asset.
•For example, accessed, entered, edited, generated, manipulated, or printed.
Process
•
FCI is inactive or at rest on an asset.
•For example, located on electronic media, in system component memory, or in
physical format such as paper documents.
Store
•
FCI is being transferred from one asset to another asset.
•
For example, data in transit using physical or digital transport methods.
Transmit
Figure 5-8: In Level 1, any assets that process, store, or transmit FCI are in scope.

CUI is inactive or at rest on an asset.

such as paper documents.
CUI is being transferred from one asset to another asset.
Transmit
•
For example, data in transit using physical or digital transport methods.

Security Protection
Assets

--- PAGE 238 ---
Contractor Risk Managed Assets
CMMC Level 2
•
Contractor Risk Managed Assets are part of the CMMC Assessment Scope.
•
Capable of, but not intended to, process, store, or transmit CUI because of the security policy, procedures,
and practices in place.
•
Not required to be physically or logically separated from CUI Assets.
•
Managed using the contractor’s risk-based information security policy, procedures, and practices and are not
assessed against CMMC practices.
Figure 5-11: The Contractor Risk Managed Assets must be documented in the asset inventory,
the SSP, and the network diagram and will only be assessed against CA.L2-3.12.4.
Notes
226 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic A
Contractor Risk
Managed Assets

--- PAGE 239 ---
Specialized Assets
•
Specialized Assets are not part of the CMMC Self-Assessment Scope at Level 1.
•
Are not assessed against CMMC Practices.
•
Specialized Assets are part of the CMMC Assessment Scope for Level 2.
•
Must be documented in the SSP and include details of how they are managed.
•
Specialized Assets consist of:
•
Government Property: All property owned or leased by the government.
•
Internet of Things (IoT) or Industrial Internet of Things (IIoT): Interconnected devices having physical or
virtual representation in the digital world, sensing/actuation capability, and programmability features.
•
Operational Technology (OT): Used in manufacturing systems, Industrial Control Systems (ICS), or Supervisory
Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) systems.
•
Restricted Information Systems: Includes systems (and associated IT components comprising the system) that
are configured based entirely on government requirements and are used to support a contract.
•
Test Equipment: Include hardware and/or associated IT components used in the testing of products, system
components, and contract deliverables.
Sources: CMMC Self-Assessment Scope – Level 1, version 2.0
CMMC Assessment Scope – Level 2, version 2.0
Figure 5-12: The Specialized Assets must be documented in the asset inventory, the SSP, and the
network diagram and will only be assessed against CA.L2-3.12.4.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 227
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic A
Specialized Assets

--- PAGE 240 ---
Out-of-Scope Assets
Source: CMMC Assessment Scope – Level 2
Out-of-Scope Assets: Assets that cannot process, store or transmit FCI or CUI because they are
physically or logically separated from CUI assets or are inherently unable to do so.
•
Assets can be categorized as Out-of-Scope Assets in both Levels 1 and 2.
•
Not part of the CMMC Assessment engagement.
•
Not evaluated against CMMC practices.
•
Do not have documentation requirements.
•
Do not provide security protections to FCI or CUI assets.
Figure 5-13: Assets that cannot process, store, or transmit FCI or CUI are out-of-scope for Levels
1 and 2, respectively.

ACTIVITY 5-1
Using the CMMC Scoping Guidance
Data Files
C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources\Scope_Level1_V2.0_FINAL_20211202_508.pdf
C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources\Scope_Level2_V2.0_FINAL_20211202_508.pdf
Scenario
Company A is a Department of Defense contractor providing secure payment card processing
devices to the U.S. Government. The devices are manufactured within their facility and incorporate
biometric scanning as a primary mechanism for authentication. These devices store, process, and
transmit transaction data which is retained within Company A’s cloud-based environment for a
period of 5 years. The cloud-based environment is hosted within the AWS GovCloud (US), a U.S.
Government secure cloud solution that complies with the FedRAMP High baseline. Use this
scenario to answer the questions in steps 2, 3, and 4.
1.
Open the Level 1 and Level 2 CMMC Scoping Guidance documentation.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named
Scope_Level1_V2.0_FINAL_20211202_508.pdf.
b)
From the same CMMC Resources folder, open Scope_Level2_V2.0_FINAL_20211202_508.pdf
2.
Using the Level 1 and Level 2 CMMC Scoping Guide, answer the following:
Based on the scenario above, which Scoping Guidance document (Level 1 or
Level 2) would be applicable?
A: Level 2, because CUI (biometric data and payment information) is being processed and stored for
the U.S. Government.
3.
Which CMMC Asset Categories would likely apply?
A: CUI and Specialized Assets
4.
Would the cloud-based environment be considered in/out of scope? Why?
A: This is a tricky question and is intended to encourage students to follow the information to
determine the answer. We know that biometric and payment information is hosted on GovCloud
*and* that this data are likely CUI, which means that the environment would be "in scope". Access
to the GovCloud is managed by the USG, however, tenants are responsible for assuring security
controls are implemented in all systems and access points from the device TO the cloud. There
are implications in this: the devices and access to the devices are controlled, that accounts
accessing the data are in fact authorized, and that they still have a business need for such data.
Some Assessment Objectives (e.g., "AC.L2-3.1.3 – CONTROL CUI FLOW") may be a shared
responsibility. OSCs are encouraged to develop a shared responsibility matrix delineating roles
and responsibilities related to the Assessment Objectives.
5.
What assets are in scope for a Level 1 self-assessment?
A: Assets that process, store, or transmit FCI are included in the self-assessment scope.

6.
Based on the broad definition of FCI Assets, which of the following statements
best describes the self-assessment scope?
○
The self-assessment scope must include everything in the OSC's environment.
○
The self-assessment scope must include all government property.
○
The self-assessment scope will probably include everything in the OSC's environment.
○
The self-assessment scope will include only Specialized Assets.
7.
Describe the four types of assets and provide examples of each.
A: People refers to personnel, such as employees, contractors, vendors, and external service
providers. Technology refers to the systems involved in processing, storing, and transmitting FCI
and CUI, such as servers, network appliances, database systems, and mobile devices. Facilities
refers to the physical locations, such as office buildings, server rooms, datacenters, and secured
rooms. External Service Providers (ESPs) refers to the people, technology, or facilities provided
by an organization that is outside of the OSC.
8.
Comparing the two scoping documents, what are some differences between
the self-assessment scope and the Level 2 Assessment Scope?
A: The Level 2 Assessment Scope document has more detailed information because there are more
assets included at the higher level. You will find descriptions of the five Asset Categories and the
requirements that contractors must satisfy for each Asset Category. Also included at Level 2 is
guidance on defining the CMMC Assessment Scope, separation techniques, and External Service
Provider considerations.
9.
Similar to FCI Assets, CUI Assets are defined as assets that process, store,
or transmit CUI. What are the additional requirements that an OSC must
satisfy when dealing with CUI Assets?
A: The contractor, or OSC, must document CUI Assets in an asset inventory, in the SSP, and provide
a network diagram of the Assessment Scope. This additional CUI Asset information will be used
during scoping discussions in the pre-assessment process.
10.

TOPIC B
Get Oriented to the OSC Environment
A critical step in identifying what information needs to be protected is establishing where that
information might be present. While ultimately you will focus on those parts of the Organization
Seeking Certification (OSC) environment that will contain sensitive information, you need to begin
with a good general understanding of the environment itself.
Who Defines Scope
•
The process of determining what is in scope:
•
Is a negotiation involving:
•
The systems that will be used to handle (create, process, store, etc.) the
sensitive information.
•
The OSC’s initial determination of scope.
•
The Lead Assessor’s evaluation of the scope.
•
Some OSCs may want to hire a contractor to help them:
•
Determine what their scope should be.
•
Identify strategic ways to limit scope that will help to reduce costs and reduce
the overall attack surface.
•
Properly prepare portions of the environment deemed to be in scope to meet
CMMC Level requirements.
Figure 5-15: Several factors work together to determine what is in scope for a CMMC
Assessment.
Notes
232 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic B
Who Defines Scope

--- PAGE 245 ---
Scope Negotiation for Level 2 Assessments
•
Before the Assessment:
•
The OSC identifies what they think should be in scope.
•
They might work with a consultant to make this determination.
•
During the Assessment process:
•
The Lead Assessor may identify other systems they feel should be in scope.
•
The Lead Assessor, C3PAO and OSC Assessment Official may negotiate whether other systems are in scope.
•
Example:
•
A simple network with five computers:
•
One workstation each for Brian, Laurie, and Tamara.
•
Two servers: one for e-mail/file server and another that runs the accounting software.
•
Before Assessment, OSC decides the accounting software is not within scope as it does not contain FCI/CUI.
•
During Assessment (either the CA-RR or the Assessment itself):
•
The Lead Assessor realizes the accounting server is not isolated from the other systems
and is, therefore, in scope.
•
The Lead Assessor and OSC can try to negotiate:
•
Whether the accounting system is in or out of scope.
•
The additional cost of the Assessment due to the addition of the new system.
•
Other factors in the relationship.
Figure 5-16: Scope is negotiated between the OSC and the Lead Assessor.
Notes

Follow the Information
•
Use a strategy of “following the information” to determine scope.
•
Identify where sensitive information resides within the OSC environment.
•
Determine what types of sensitive information they have.
•
Identify where the sensitive information moves, to ensure all affected systems are identified.
•
To ensure you are thorough in your analysis, you should be familiar with your environment, or the
OSC’s environment.
FCI
CUI

Tools for Taking Inventory
When gathering information to prepare for scoping:
•
Use tools to keep that information organized.
•
Examples include spreadsheet, database, or word processing programs.
•
Set up a database or document format in advance (a template) to make data entry fast and easy.
•
Diagrams can help to document networks, system workflows, or building layouts.
•
Use tools you can easily carry to the customer site such as a notebook computer, tablet, or paper.

You will do a better job “following the information” if you
have at least a basic awareness of the systems that exist in
their environment and how Rudison uses them.
•
The CMMC Glossary describes an information system as:
•
“…a discrete set of information resources organized for the
collection, processing, maintenance, use, sharing,
dissemination, or disposition of information.”
•

Rudison systems that may be involved in the handling of
sensitive information.
A complete systems list can
serve as a validation check
later.
By knowing all the systems
in the environment, you will
be better able to ensure you
haven’t missed any systems
that might handle sensitive
information.

--- PAGE 261 ---
Shared Responsibility Matrix
•
Determines relationships between in-scope controls and responsible parties.
•
Clarifies what questions should be asked of their external service provider.
•
Which entity is responsible for the implementation, configuration, and maintenance of the control?
•
Are the flow down requirements explicitly defined?
•
Do the terms and conditions of the provider’s contract specifically and accurately reflect expectations?
•
Validates assumptions regarding performance of in-scope controls between the OSC and the ESP.
•
Captures risks and remediates any risks resulting from this shared responsibility.
•
Determines/validates correct configuration of these services to support compliance.
Shared Responsibility Matrix: A mechanism that Identifies the person(s) or team(s) in the OSC or
the ESP responsible for the implementation and sustainment of the technical controls, as reflected
in the terms of service between the ESP as provider and the OSC as customer.
Figure 5-30: A shared responsibility matrix identifies who is responsible for each Assessment
Objective—the OSC or the ESP.

--- PAGE 263 ---
Cloud Services Scope
•
Does the cloud provider’s environment contain FCI or CUI?
•
Yes – Then it is in scope for the Assessment.
•
No – Then it is out of scope for the Assessment.
•
With cloud services, the OSC may have little access or control of cybersecurity configuration.
•
If the OSC uses a cloud service to handle FCI/CUI and that cloud service is not properly secured, it
will prevent the OSC from obtaining a CMMC Certification.
•
Therefore, it is necessary to establish compliance for any cloud services deemed to be in scope.
•
The cloud provider may hold CMMC or other certifications the OSC can, at least partially, "inherit"
when using the provider.
Figure 5-32: Just because assets are maintained in the cloud doesn't mean the OSC is not
responsible for compliance. You must work with the cloud provider to provide observable
evidence or establish that you can "inherit" security controls the cloud provider has implemented.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 251
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic B
Cloud Services Scope

--- PAGE 264 ---
Inheritance
•
Cloud service providers:
•
Typically implement underlying security controls.
•
Can provide observable evidence to prove such controls are implemented.
•
OSCs that provision applications, virtual machines, and other systems through service providers:
•
Under some circumstances, can inherit security controls implemented by service providers for their own
CMMC Assessment.
•
Can thereby avoid some of the time and effort needed to implement and document those controls.
Security Control Inheritance: A situation in which an information system or application receives
protection from security controls (or portions of security controls) that are developed, implemented,
assessed, authorized, and monitored by entities other than those responsible for the system or
application; entities either internal or external to the organization where the system or application
resides.
Source: Security and Privacy Controls for Federal Information Systems and Organizations (NIST SP 800˗53 Rev. 4)
Figure 5-33: To establish compliance, the OSC may be able to inherit security controls
implemented by the service provider.
Notes
252 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic B
Inheritance

--- PAGE 265 ---
Cloud Service Scoping Considerations
•
The Lead Assessor and the Assessment Team Members must consider the role and interaction of
these platforms/services in the OSCs environment, to determine:
•
Shared responsibility in terms of implementation and sustainment of the in -scope controls.
•
Ability to leverage 'inherited' controls where inheritance aligns with the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP).
•
Whether there may be impact to:
•
The OSC's existing technical and/or administrative controls.
•
The OSC's responsibilities with respect to configuration of these technologies and/or services.
•
Any potential impacts to the OSC's risk posture.
•
Where a control implemented via a service agreement may be insufficient or introduce secondary
vulnerabilities.

a)
In rows 13 through 15, examine the information provided about other cloud services Rudison uses.

3.
When it comes to scoping, how is the development environment significantly
different from Gusto, Office 365, and QuickBooks?
A: The difference is the (expected) presence/absence of FCI and CUI. We know from the fact pattern
that there is FCI and CUI in the dev environment. Gusto "just" has HR information in it, which
shouldn't have FCI or CUI in it. In the fact pattern, we looked at the information in QuickBooks and
determined that there wasn't any FCI or CUI in there, either. That's why they are out of scope.
Office 365 and Dev, though, have FCI and CUI, so they are in scope. If you want to wade into the
waters of inheritance, you could contrast the controls that Office 365 will inherit versus those the
dev environment will inherit. The Office 365 environment is likely to inherit more of the certification
than the AWS environment due to the OSC's ability to deviate from/change those controls.
4.
The datacenter is physically located in the U.S. Is this relevant to the security
of government-owned information?
A: To some extent, the location of the datacenter is relevant. However, the issue isn't just the
physical location of the resources, it also is who has administrative control over the resources. In
this case, although it is in the US-East region, the administrators could be in the U.S., Canada,
China, India, etc. Ironically, this isn't really an issue under CMMC except when it comes to export-
controlled information. Systems containing export-controlled information must only be
administered by U.S. citizens (no non-U.S. citizens, including green card holders).
5.
AWS and Office 365 are both cloud-based external service providers that are
in scope because they process, store, or transmit FCI. What does that mean
for Rudison as they move forward in their level 1 self-assessment?
A: Rudison needs to build shared responsibility matrices for both companies that include all the
assessment objectives for the Level 1 practices so that they are clear on what the ESPs are
responsible for and what they are responsible for.

Leave the file open and minimize the Excel application window.

TOPIC C
Determine How Sensitive Information Moves
You have familiarized yourself with the OSC environment and have identified where sensitive
information exists within that environment. But information does not always remain in one place.
Before you can determine what parts of the environment are in scope for the CMMC Assessment,
you must determine where the sensitive information moves, which might bring other parts of the
OSC environment into play.
Catalog the Sensitive Information
•
The first step is to catalog the sensitive information.
•
Identify instances of sensitive information.
•
Determine the type of sensitive information.
•
Identify which systems contain the information.
•
For new environments, you start by asking:
•
What types of sensitive information does the OSC expect to handle?
•
What systems are likely to be involved in handling the sensitive
information?
Before you begin, inventory all the systems.
1.
Catalog the sensitive information.
2.
Determine how sensitive information moves.
1. Received from the government?
2. Generated by OSC?
3. Accessible to or shared with others?
4. Who in OSC has access to it?
5. Will sensitive information be sent to the
government?
3.
Identify systems and enabling systems in
scope.
Once scope has been established, the systems
in scope can be evaluated against the targeted
CMMC Level.
Figure 5-36: Start the scoping process by identifying and categorizing the types of sensitive
information that exist within the OSC environment.

Prevent Sensitive Information from Moving to Reduce Risk
•
As an alternative to moving information, the government
may:
•
Keep some information in government systems only.
•
Require logging into the government system to view the
information.
•
Prevent contractor from copying information to their own
environment.
•
This arrangement:
•
Keeps data within the government’s environment.
•
Reduces the government’s risk.

ACTIVITY 5-4
Examining an Information Inventory
Before You Begin
You have already opened Rudison Systems and Information.xlsx in Excel.
Scenario
In this activity, you will examine details that a CCP might capture regarding sensitive information
that exists within an OSC environment—in this, information handled by the Rudison company.
1.
Examine the information inventory documented in the workbook.
a)
In Excel, in the Rudison Systems and Information.xlsx workbook you opened previously, select the
Information worksheet tab.
This worksheet contains an inventory of the sensitive information contained in the organization's
systems.
b)
Scroll as needed to view columns B through G, and examine the information that has been entered.
•
Column B – A short description of the information.
•
Column C – Relevant contract numbers.
•
Column D – Whether the information type is FCI or CUI and, if CUI:
•
Column E – The agency that designated it as CUI.
•
Column F – Whether the Designating Agency or the OSC has associated the information with
a CMMC Level.
•
Column G – The CMMC Level associated with that CUI or, if the government hasn’t provided
one, the Level the OSC thinks is likely to be needed.
In this case, all of the identified information is FCI, not CUI.
2.
Observe that the worksheet collects information about the source and flow of
the information.
276 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic C
Activity

--- PAGE 289 ---
a)
Scroll to the Information Origination Details section, and examine details regarding the information's
origin.
•
If externally created:
•
Column I – The name of the agency or office from whom the information was originally
received.
•
Column J – The Prime contractor, or mid-tier contractor, from whom the information was
received by the OSC.
•
If internally created:
•
Column K – The organizational unit or business unit that created the information.
b)
Scroll to view columns L-AG.
These columns include details about how the information is handled within the OSC.
Note: Information inventories can be time consuming and complex, and they
also sometimes turn up information that is unexpected. For example, an OSC
may not think that it stores credit card numbers or social security numbers, but
during the course of the information inventory such information may be
identified. The worksheet includes additional fields for tracking attributes of
personally identifiable information, personal healthcare information, etc., that
may be found in the environment. Recording this information when it is found
can help the OSC in the event of a data breach.
3.
Close the file and exit Excel.

Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic C

TOPIC D
Identify Systems in Scope
Now that you are familiar with the types of information to be protected, you can turn your focus on
understanding how the boundaries of a CMMC Assessment are defined. In this topic, you will learn
how an assessor will search for the sensitive information and follow how it flows within an OSC to
determine what needs to be evaluated against the CMMC Model requirements during an
Assessment.
Identify Systems in Scope
•
To identify what will be in scope, refer to:
•
The list of sensitive information you have cataloged.
•
Who has access to it.
•
How it moves.
•
Validate your analysis by looking at the organization from a
systems perspective as well.
Before you begin, inventory all the systems.
1.
Catalog the sensitive information.
2.
Determine how sensitive information moves.
1. Received from the government?
2. Generated by OSC?
3. Accessible to or shared with others?
4. Who in OSC has access to it?
5. Will sensitive information be sent to the
government?
3.
Identify systems and enabling systems
in scope.
Once scope has been established, the systems
in scope can be evaluated against the targeted
CMMC Level.
Figure 5-54: The third major step in Assessment preparation is to use the information you've
gained about the company and the contract to identify the systems that are in scope for
Assessment and Certification.
Notes
278 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic D
Identify Systems in
Scope

--- PAGE 291 ---
Scope Boundaries
CMMC Certification Boundary:
Defines the assets to which an assessor will evaluate conformity with applicable CMMC practices.
This is the boundary to which a CMMC Certificate will be applied.
Assessment Boundary:
Identifies all assets in the contractor’s environment for the Assessment engagement. Assets within
the Assessment Boundary can be part of the CMMC Certification Boundary or Enabling Assets.
Assessment Boundary

Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic D
Scope Boundaries

--- PAGE 292 ---
System Security Plan (SSP)
•
An SSP helps with scoping by documenting:
•
Boundaries.
•
Information flows.
•
Responsibilities for protecting sensitive information.
•
Having an SSP will help to foster agreement:
•
Among OSC staff involved in creating and reviewing the SSP.
•
Between the OSC and the Lead Assessor.
System Security Plan: The formal document prepared by the information system owner (or
common security controls owner for inherited controls) that provides an overview of the security
requirements for the system and describes the security controls in place or planned for meeting
those requirements. The plan can also contain as supporting appendices or as references, other
key security-related documents such as a risk assessment, privacy impact assessment, system
interconnection agreements, contingency plan, security configurations, configuration
management plan, and incident response plan.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (CNSSI 4009)
Figure 5-56: A System Security Plan is a formal document that can streamline the Certification
effort for an OSC and help with scoping.

Best Practices for Establishing the OSC Scope
•
Follow the Information.
•
Begin by examining how the OSC receives information during the proposal development phase of
their proposal/performance lifecycle.
•
Did the government provide the OSC information not available on SAM.gov?
•
Did the government provide data to the OSC immediately after winning the contract?
•
Sensitive information must be protected from start to finish.
•
Build a Data Flow Diagram.
•
Even at CMMC Level 1, have a System Security Plan.

Scenario
In this activity, you'll walk through each of Rudison's systems to determine where you are likely to
find FCI, given your previous analysis.

Figure 5-60: Where FCI might exist in the Rudison environment.
1.
Is FCI likely to be on the WordPress site?
A: Our analysis did not determine that the WordPress site would be used to create or receive any
FCI. Let’s now validate this by looking at it from a systems perspective. The WordPress site is a
marketing site. It talks about Rudison and its capabilities. If it isn’t used to send or receive files or
other information, is it likely to contain FCI? Probably not. So, we can feel pretty confident that the
WordPress site can be excluded from the Assessment scope boundary.
2.
What about QuickBooks?
A: Our analysis did not identify any FCI that would be stored in QuickBooks. Let’s look at it from a
systems perspective to confirm. In some cases, shipping information, employee names and
contact information, or other information that would go into an accounting system might be
nonpublic and, therefore, would need to be treated as FCI. So, did we miss something? It
depends on the nature of the data that will be received. It is good to flag issues such as this as
needing additional discussions with the OSC. For our purposes, we will assume that Rudison has
been told that the contract information will only be public information (e.g., only the information in
Beta.SAM.gov) and, therefore, is not FCI. That means that QuickBooks is not in scope of an
eventual CMMC Assessment.
284 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic D
Activity
Where FCI Might Exist in
the Rudison
Environment

--- PAGE 297 ---
3.
What about Gusto?
A: Our analysis did not identify any FCI that would be stored in Gusto. Let’s look at it from a systems
perspective to confirm. Gusto provides HR and payroll services. Is HR or payroll information likely
to contain FCI? Probably not. Payroll and HR information is proprietary to Rudison, but it probably
won’t contain FCI. In some cases, such as where a services company is working for the
government on a time and materials basis, the notes that accompany each employee’s billing
records may contain FCI. Again, this is something that you will need to confirm on a case-by-case
basis with the OSC. For our purposes, we will assume that Gusto does not contain any FCI.
4.
How about the AWS environment?
A: Our analysis indicated that the AWS development environment would be used to create FCI.
Looking at this from a systems perspective, we know that the FCI software will be developed in
that environment. So yes, it will be in the Assessment scope.
5.
Will the Office 365 environment contain FCI?
A: Our analysis showed that, at a minimum, the SharePoint site, emails, and OneDrive would contain
FCI. Looking at this from a systems perspective, we know that the Office 365 environment is used
to communicate with the government. Therefore, we can be pretty confident that the Office 365
environment will contain FCI. That means the Office 365 environment will be within the
Assessment scope boundary.
6.
Are there any other locations where you might find FCI?
A: Yes. You also need to look at end-user devices.

Scenario

edit documents and emails. Looking at it from a system/device perspective, you know the users
create and edit documents, such as reports, on those devices and author emails on them as well. So,
those devices will be in the Assessment scope boundary.

End-user Devices
Jen

Figure 5-61: End-user devices used by Rudison employees.
Note: For the purposes of this lesson, we will assume that Jen, Chris, and Mark are all single,
live alone, and that the devices illustrated on this slide are the only devices on their home
networks.
1.
Could these end-user devices potentially be removed from the scope of the
Assessment if the Rudison users worked in a fully VDI (virtual desktop
infrastructure) environment?
A: Potentially, yes. You would need to investigate how the VDI worked, but if the FCI is only
accessed via the end-user device and never actually exists on the device, then the end-user
devices could be moved outside the Assessment scope boundary. If Rudison did employ a fully
VDI environment, it would be up to them to demonstrate to an assessor that the end-user devices
should be out of scope. If you were a CCP helping Rudison to prepare their environment for
Assessment, you should help them understand the advantages and disadvantages these different
architecture options pose for the company.
286 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic D
Activity
Rudison End-User
Devices

--- PAGE 299 ---
2.
Like most people today, the Rudison employees all have mobile phones, and
they access their Rudison emails from those phones. Will these mobile
devices be within scope for Certification and/or Assessment?
A: You have already established that the emails need to be treated as FCI. Therefore, the mobile
devices will also be in both the Certification and Assessment scope.
3.
The Rudison employees all work from home, and their home networks are
very simple. They all use a Wi-Fi router supplied by their Internet providers to
access the Internet and the Rudison shared resources. Are these routers and
home networks in scope?
A: The routers, and the networks they create, are also in scope since they are used to create, store,
or transfer FCI.
4.
What about the other devices on their home networks, such as smart phones
owned by other family members?
A: It depends. If the network uses an appropriate separation technique, such as VLANS or separate
subnetworks with routing controls, then the other devices are likely to be out scope for both the
Assessment and Certification, assuming they do not have access to the FCI. If separation
techniques are not used, those devices are likely to still be considered in the Assessment scope
but outside the Certification scope, even though they do not themselves contain any FCI.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 287
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic D

Now you and the team will confirm your initial conclusions by discussing the various systems you
deemed to be out of scope, and identifying the rationale for not including those systems within the
scope boundaries.
1.
Why is the website out of scope?
A: There is no FCI on the website, so we can assume it is out of scope for a CMMC Assessment.
You should document the separation techniques that ensure FCI will not end up on the site. For
example: a) Limit ability to publish content on the site to a single individual; b) Require review and
approval of all posted content required by another individual.
288 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)

Rudison Assessment

Rationale: Why the

--- PAGE 301 ---
2.
Why is QuickBooks out of scope?
A: QuickBooks is out of scope because it should never contain FCI. You need to document the
information that will be contained in QuickBooks, so you can demonstrate to the Assessment
Team why QuickBooks should not be in scope for Assessment. This can be tricky because we
don’t need to make changes to QuickBooks or other overt actions for us to suddenly wind up
storing FCI. Instead, it can happen simply by using the software as intended. Rudison should be
able to demonstrate that they have a practice in place for keeping FCI out of QuickBooks. Rudison
should also periodically review the information it stores in QuickBooks to ensure FCI is not stored
in it. Alternatively, Rudison could assume QuickBooks is within scope and include it in its CMMC-
related efforts.
3.
Why is Gusto out of scope?
A: HR and benefits systems typically do not include FCI. Of course, you should investigate to be
certain that this Gusto instance does not contain FCI. Document the data contained in Gusto so
Rudison can later demonstrate why Gusto should be out of both the Assessment and Certification
scopes.
4.
Gusto and QuickBooks are integrated, enabling them to share data. How
does this affect scope?
A: Integration is something you need to pay careful attention to when you determine scope, as it
provides a way for sensitive information to move quickly and possibly without anyone noticing. In
this example, since QuickBooks does not contain any FCI and no FCI is placed directly into
Gusto, Gusto inherently cannot contain or have access to FCI.
