--- PAGE 302 ---
TOPIC E
Limit Scope
Various methods can be used to limit the range and extent of sensitive information within an
organization, thereby limiting what must be in scope for an Assessment.
Benefits of Limiting Scope
•
Limiting scope enables an OSC to reduce:
•
The effort needed to reach compliance.
•
The time needed to reach compliance.
•
The cost needed to reach compliance.
•
The size of the attack surface, potentially
resulting in improved security.
•
You can limit scope:
•
By keeping sensitive information isolated to a small part of the OSC’s environment.
•
Using isolation methods such as:
•
Physical Isolation – Providing “real world” barriers, such as walls and locked doors.
•
Logical Isolation – Providing electronic barriers, such as firewalls and gateways.
•
Controlled Access – Restricting users in some circumstances based on configuration or parameters.
•
Identify existing scope boundaries by finding where isolation methods are currently used.
•
Establish new scope boundaries by using isolation methods where they’re not currently used.
Figure 5-63: There are various good reasons for an OSC to undertake the effort to limit scope.
Notes
290 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic E
Benefits of Limiting
Scope

--- PAGE 303 ---
Justification for Limiting Scope
•
This statement:
•
Allows organizations to create “security domains” or “enclaves” where sensitive information may be handled.
•
Justifies an organization using separation methods to limit scope.
•
By implication, means that where separation methods are not used, the entire network is in scope.
•
Example: System A contains CUI/FCI, and is on the same network as System B.
•
Therefore, System B is also in scope, regardless of whether it contains CUI.
If nonfederal organizations designate specific system components for the processing, storage, or
transmission of CUI, those organizations may limit the scope of the security requirements by
isolating the designated system components in a separate CUI security domain. Isolation can be
achieved by applying architectural and design concepts (e.g., implementing subnetworks with
firewalls or other boundary protection devices and using information flow control mechanisms).
Security domains may employ physical separation, logical separation, or a combination of both.
This approach can provide adequate security for the CUI and avoid increasing the organization’s
security posture to a level beyond that which it requires for protecting its missions, operations,
and assets.
Enclave: A segmentation of an organization's network or data that is intended to "wall off" that
network or database from all other networks or systems. A CMMC Assessment scope can be within the
Assessment scope of an enclave.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Source: NIST SP 800˗171 rev 2, para 1.1
Figure 5-64: It is important for the CCP to be able to recognize and understand separation
methods, which can be used to limit scope.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 291

ACTIVITY 5-8
Using Separation Techniques to Limit Scope
Scenario
In one department of the Consolidated Flange Company, the engineering office has a network-
attached storage (NAS) device that contains documents labeled as CUI. The NAS device is in the
engineering office. The door to the engineering office has a cipher lock, requiring that personnel key
in a passcode to enter. The NAS itself is configured with user authentication, so that anyone
attempting to access the CUI documents must log in by providing a username and password.
Outside of the engineering office is the manufacturing floor, where there are computers used to
monitor the assembly line and control some production robots. The engineers are responsible for
not only creating new designs, but also monitoring and maintaining the production equipment, so
engineering and production share the same network.
Engineering Office
Manufacturing Floor
PC
Switch
PC
NAS
PC
PC
PC
Door with
cipher lock
Production Area
Control Area
Figure 5-70: The factory layout.
1.
It is intended that no CUI will be stored or processed in the systems located
on the manufacturing floor. Is the manufacturing floor, therefore, out of
scope?
A: No. The NAS being in a separate, access-controlled room helps to limit the physical scope of the
Assessment. However, the fact that the NAS, which contains CUI, is on a network that is not
physically or logically isolated from the manufacturing floor’s network means the manufacturing
floor’s network must also be considered in scope. Furthermore, while the physical barrier provided
by the door with a cipher lock is a good step, CUI will not be completely protected from
unauthorized viewing if there are windows throughout the office's outer walls.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 297
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic E
Activity
Factory Layout

--- PAGE 310 ---
2.
How can you increase the likelihood that the manufacturing floor will be
considered out of scope by the assessor?
A: To prevent access of the CUI from outside the engineering office, you should segment the network
so the engineering office and manufacturing floor are on separate networks, and limit network
traffic so the NAS can only be accessed from within the engineering office. By isolating the NAS
from access over the machines on the manufacturing floor, you can take the manufacturing floor
out of scope. Any possibility of viewing CUI from outside the office (through windows, for example)
must be eliminated.
298 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 5: Scoping Certification and Assessment Boundaries | Topic E

--- PAGE 313 ---
Preparing the OSC
Lesson Time: 1 hour, 50 minutes
Lesson Introduction
As a CMMC Professional (CCP) consultant, it is imperative that you recognize your
responsibilities within the ecosystem. You will work with an Organization Seeking
Certification (OSC) to ensure that they can present information for the Assessment that is
appropriate in context, that demonstrates the sufficient adoption of the CMMC practices,
and that preserves the fidelity of the Assessment process. The OSC’s ability to contract with
the Department of Defense (DoD), and the protection of our nation’s information, will
depend heavily on the quality of your work. In this lesson, you will learn what is required to
shepherd an OSC on their path to CMMC Certification.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Provide guidance for OSCs to establish cybersecurity maturity.
•
Evaluate whether an organization seeking to undergo a CMMC Assessment is ready.
6

--- PAGE 314 ---
TOPIC A
Foster a Mature Cybersecurity Culture
As a consultant working with an OSC, it's important for you to understand how to help the OSC
establish a mature cybersecurity culture.
Organizational Culture
•
Personality of an organization.
•
Some values may be written.
•
Mission and vision statement.
•
Policies.
•
Many values will be unwritten and assumed.
•
Just how we do things.
•
Learned through observation and informal communications.
•
Employees develop an organization’s culture over time.
•
As it becomes more ingrained, it can be difficult to change.
Organizational culture: A set of values and ideas that reflect acceptable and unacceptable
practices and behaviors within an organization.
Figure 6-1: Organizational culture is like the personality of the organization.

--- PAGE 315 ---
Cybersecurity Culture
•
Strong cybersecurity culture involves alignment between:
•
Organizational cybersecurity elements (e.g., policy, procedures, leadership, and social norms).
•
Individual employee’s attitudes, knowledge, and assumptions about cybersecurity.
•
To create a stable cybersecurity culture, cybersecurity needs to be a company -wide focus.
•
All departments, not just IT.
•
People make an organization secure, not technology.
•
Weakest link in many breaches is people, so everyone needs to be trained and conscious of the risks.
•
All employees must:
•
Recognize they play a role in protecting critical information from digital attacks.
•
Participate in regular training programs.
•
Actively engage in behaviors and habits outlined by their cybersecurity program.
Cybersecurity culture: The combined organizational factors that put every employee in the
position to behave in ways that support cybersecurity or ways that place the company at risk.

Minimum Attributes of a Policy
•
Policies should:
•
Clearly state the policy’s purpose.
•
Clearly define the scope of the policy (enterprise, department, or a specific information system).
•
Identify the roles and responsibilities of activities covered by the policy.
•
Identify the responsibility, authority, and ownership of activities in the domain.
•
Identify the procedures used to carry out and meet the intent of the policy.
•
Include any regulatory guidelines the policy addresses.
•
Be endorsed by management and disseminated to stakeholders.
•
Be periodically reviewed and updated.
Policy: An artifact or collection of artifacts that establishes governance over the implementation of
CMMC practices and activities.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms

Minimum Attributes of a Procedure
•
Procedures should minimally specify:
•
Purpose (e.g., why the procedure is necessary).
•
Objective (e.g., what needs to be done and how this will be accomplished).
•
A description of roles, responsibilities, and authorities.
•
A description of inputs/outputs.
•
Criteria that must be met in support of the procedures.
•
Any tools, information, and/or supporting resources, as required.
•
A definition of key terminology.
•
Detailed steps of how to perform the procedure.
Procedure: The documented details for how an activity is implemented to achieve a desired
outcome. A procedure should provide enough detail for a trained individual to perform the
activity.

Quantitative Framework Establishment
•
Provides for day-to-day measurement and control of the procedures in order to:
•
Provide for appropriate visibility into the procedures, as performed, taking corrective action where necessary.
•
Establish the right set of metrics and measure the appropriate attributes of the procedures and/or work
products produced.
•
Metrics may be qualitative or quantitative.
•
Qualitative – Observable vs. measurable (i.e., as derived through observations and interviews).
•
Quantitative – Measured and structured, based on characteristics or traits (i.e., as derived through metrics
and testing).
•
Measurement examples include:
•
Actual vs. planned (performance of procedure).
•
Identification and evaluation of significant deviations.
•
Results of corrective actions.
•
Reviews with “higher level management” are critical to the continued success of the
organization’s cybersecurity program.
•
Reviews should provide sufficient visibility into the issue identified and
status/impacts of resulting corrective actions.
Figure 6-11: Establishing a quantitative framework and using the data to make adjustments in
cybersecurity planning and implementation shows a high level of maturity.

•
Standardization provides for consistency in the implementation of policies, practices, and controls
across the organization, defining activities to be performed while providing guidance in tailoring.
•
A standardized organizational practice typically provides:
•
Ample description of the practices and practice objectives.
•
Process flow diagrams (including, entry/exit criterion).
•
Specified performance metrics.
•
Procedures for incorporation of lessons learned and improvements.
•
Optimization is contingent upon practice stability.

Optimization for Long-

--- PAGE 326 ---
ACTIVITY 6-1
Fostering a Mature Cybersecurity Culture
Scenario
You have been hired as a consultant to help an OSC prepare for a CMMC Assessment. Before
proceeding to any pre-assessment steps, you decide that it's beneficial for the OSC to understand
what it means to foster a solid cybersecurity culture.
1.
What is the relationship between a policy and a procedure?
A: A policy describes the overall characteristics of a required activity. (For example, the purpose, who
needs to follow the policy, and the roles involved in the implementation and the responsibilities of
each.) A policy is focused on the "why" of an organizational action. Procedures explain the "how,"
providing the detailed steps necessary to implement a policy on a day-to-day basis.
2.
Why do you need a plan as part of governance?
A: Plans are necessary to ensure the ongoing monitoring of the activities mandated in policies and
procedures. A plan provides the details of the implementation timeline, the budget for resource
management, and required training.
3.
Why is implementing cultural change a critical step in building cybersecurity
maturity?
A: To achieve real institutionalization of cybersecurity in an organization where all employees
understand they play a role in protecting sensitive information, you have to consciously build such
a culture by obtaining executive support, identifying employees views, and implementing regular
cybersecurity training. Without that institutionalization, policies and procedures are just
documents.
4.
How does having a mature cybersecurity culture help with a CMMC
Assessment?
A: A CMMC Assessment is more likely to go smoothly when everyone in the OSC understands the
importance of protecting sensitive information and their specific cybersecurity responsibilities.
When cybersecurity is institutionalized and mature, the artifacts an assessor examines will be
comprehensive and clear, the people an assessor interviews will speak knowledgeably about their
responsibilities, and the tests an assessor conducts will demonstrate that the technical controls
are correctly and consistently implemented.
314 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 6: Preparing the OSC | Topic A

TOPIC B
Evaluate Readiness
Now that you understand how to foster a culture of good cybersecurity hygiene in an OSC, you are
ready to take the first step towards helping the OSC prepare for a CMMC Assessment. In this topic,
you will learn how to evaluate whether the OSC is ready for a formal Assessment.
Identify the Desired CMMC Level
•
Which CMMC Level?
•
CUI information not found or expected – CMMC Level 1 is likely sufficient.
•
CUI information is found or expected – CMMC Level 2 is required.
•
Once rule-making is completed, new contracts will indicate CMMC
requirements in solicitations.
•
Per DoD Policy, CMMC requirements cannot be retroactively
incorporated into existing contracts without a contract
modification.
•
When the current contract draws to a close, CMMC can be incorporated
into any extensions or other contract modifications.
•
When a contract is up for re-competition, CMMC requirements can be
incorporated into contract at that time.
•
OSCs can contact their DoD Contracting Officer Representative
(COR) to determine the CMMC Level that their existing contract(s)
would likely require.

Opportunities to Evaluate Preparedness for an Assessment
Gap Analysis: An evaluation that examines the organization’s processes ‘as
performed’ to identify issues, impediments, and potential risks to sustained
implementation.
•
Performed by the OSC or by a CCP or RP consultant.
•
Consultant might be part of the CMMC ecosystem or external to it.
Evidence Validation: An evaluation that examines sufficiency of evidence
presented by the OSC, ensuring it meets the intent and objectives of the
control or practice.
•
Performed by the OSC or by a CCP or RP consultant.
•
Optional event that verifies OSC is prepared for the formal Assessment.
Certification Assessment Readiness Review (CA-RR): A preliminary but formal
review to verify the OSC’s readiness for the Assessment against the identified
Assessment planning parameters and Assessment scope.
•
Conducted by the Lead Assessor and, as applicable, the Assessment Team.
•
Part of the pre-work for an Assessment.
Note: The terms “Gap Analysis” and “Certification Assessment Readiness Review” appear within the CMMC documentation. “Evidence
Validation,” while not an official CMMC term, is used here to refer to this task that is commonly performed by OSC’s preparing

Gap Analysis
•
A CMMC Gap Analysis:
•
Helps to guide initial understanding of current OSC capabilities and controls.
•
Helps to identify insufficiencies in the implementation of critical controls and practices.
•
Encourages engagement of OSC stakeholders earlier in the Assessment process.
•
Conducted by:
•
The OSC or optionally by a RP or CCP acting as a consultant.
•
A provider outside of the CMMC ecosystem.
•
Potential pitfall is they have no access to supporting
mechanisms to ensure consistency.

Lesson 6: Preparing the OSC | Topic B
Gap Analysis

--- PAGE 331 ---
Closing Gaps
•
When gaps in implementation are identified, it’s critical to not only understand the present issue,
but also to create a meaningful plan for resolution of these issues.
•
As a CCP working as a consultant for the OSC, this may include:
•
Describe the nature and impact of issues (current state).
•
Propose actions to help resolve gaps (future state).
•
Identify changes to existing policies, plans, practices, resources and artifacts.
•
Develop a resourced plan to address approved gap action items.
•
Verify that gaps are properly closed, and that the corresponding implementation demonstrates that the
control/objective is met (persistent and habitual).
•
Articulate any issues in a timely manner with leadership and designated points of contact.
•
Communicate preliminary readiness with OSC.
Figure 6-17: As a consultant, a CCP might help an OSC close any identified gaps as they prepare
for an Assessment.

--- PAGE 340 ---
ACTIVITY 6-2
Evaluating Readiness
Scenario
The company where you work, an Organization Seeking Certification (OSC), has put you through
training to get you certified as a CCP. They want to have someone on staff who will help them
prepare the company for CMMC Certification.
1.
When you return to your company, how might you start helping them
prepare?
A: Answers will vary, depending on the organization, what they do, the CMMC Level they are
seeking, how mature their practices and processes already are, and how far into the process of
preparing for CMMC Assessment they already are. In general, any organization must start by
determining what CMMC Level they are seeking, as this is necessary to determine the practices
and processes that will be evaluated. Also, the organization needs to determine where sensitive
information will exist within their environment, as this will help them identify the Assessment
scope. You can inventory the company's systems to help determine where sensitive information
exists and how it flows through the organization's business processes. Once you have identified
what should be considered "in scope", you can perform a gap analysis against the Level the
organization is targeting.
2.
You've completed a gap analysis for your company, and you believe you have
helped them close all the gaps. At that point, is the OSC ready for
Assessment, or is there something else that must also be done?
A: It depends on what you did as you closed the gaps. If you updated the Evidence as you closed the
gaps, then the OSC may be ready for Assessment. With all the changes you've made, however,
you may need to circle back and verify that all of the Evidence is indeed in place and up to date.
328 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 6: Preparing the OSC | Topic B
Activity

Summary
In this lesson, you started with the fundamentals of how to foster a cybersecurity culture within an
organization. You also learned how to perform a gap analysis that evaluates the Evidence and
determines an OSC's readiness for a formal Assessment. You learned how to plan, collect, and
organize Evidence, as well as how to review that Evidence using the same methods that an assessor
uses to ensure the OSC is ready for the formal Assessment.
How will you plan to implement a cultural change to institutionalize cybersecurity
in your organization?
A: Answers will vary.
Do you think Gap Analyses or Evidence Validations will be more challenging for
you? Why?
A: Answers will vary.

Determining and
Assessing Evidence
Lesson Time: 2 hours, 40 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you understand the general methods that Certified CMMC Assessors (CCAs) will
utilize, you can turn your attention to the details of using the CMMC Assessment Guides to
assess practices and their associated Assessment Objectives. Obviously, using the
Assessment Guides is a key task if you are a member of an Assessment Team, but they will
also guide your work as a Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) working as a consultant,
especially in performing a gap analysis and an Evidence Validation. In this lesson, you will
be introduced to the structure and components of the CMMC Assessment Guides and how
to use them to determine Evidence and assess practice requirements.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Determine the Evidence needed for the CMMC Assessment.
•
Assess practices using the CMMC Assessment Guides.
7

--- PAGE 344 ---
TOPIC A
Determine Evidence
Now that you know how to evaluate readiness, you can turn your attention to working with the
Evidence. In this topic, you will learn how assessors will assess the practices. By being able to think
like an assessor, you can better prepare an OSC as a consultant or work on an Assessment Team
under the leadership of CMMC Lead Assessor.
Effective Assessments
•
Effective assessments are:
•
Objective
•
Comprehensive
•
Flexible
•
Accurate
•
Consistent
•
Thoughtful
•
The CMMC Assessment Process (CAP):
•
Helps to organize and leverage these characteristics.
•
Produces consistently objective, targeted assessments.
•
Ensures that context is preserved.
•
Offers additional guidance and documentation for conducting assessments to:
•
CCPs
•
CCAs
•
Lead Assessors
•
Assessment Team Members

The CMMC Assessment Process (CAP)
•
Provided by The Cyber AB, the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) documentation:
•
Is the definitive source for conducting CMMC Certification Assessments.

Describes the critical activities that need to be performed.

Phase 1: Plan and Prepare the Assessment
Phase 2: Conduct the Assessment
Phase 3: Report Recommended Assessment Results
Phase 4: CMMC Plans of Action and Milestones (POA&M) Close-Out Assessment

CMMC Assessment

--- PAGE 347 ---
Evidence
•
The body of data, or proof, that an organization either
meets or does not meet the objectives of the domain,
control, or practices.
•
Must meet CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) requirements:
•
Adequacy
•
Does the presented artifact, interview, or test/demo meet the
intent of the CMMC practice?
•
Sufficiency
•
Does the presented artifact, interview, or test/demo provide
the Assessment Team with enough of the “right data”?
Evidence: The observable proof that an organization has either met or not met the standard for a
particular CMMC practice.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4

adequacy and sufficiency.

--- PAGE 349 ---
Key Terminology
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Artifacts: Tangible and reviewable records that are the direct outcome of a practice or process being
performed by a system, person or persons performing a role in that practice, control, or process.
Artifacts may be a printed hard-copy or a soft- or electronic copy of a document or file embedded in
a system or software but must be a result or an output from the performance of a process within the
OSC.
Observation: A real-time demonstration or review of a test, system, tool, software, hardware,
practice, control, or process being performed and witnessed first-hand by the Lead Assessor and, if
applicable, Assessment Team.
Stakeholder: A person with an interest or concern in the success of a business. Interviews with them
provide an excellent opportunity to gain insight and establish context.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Figure 7-6: Some key terminology for the Assessment Process.

Evidence Collection, Preparation, and Generation
•
Evidence must demonstrate adequacy and sufficiency of the practice or control.
•
The Assessment Team can examine and accept any of the three Evidence types:
•
Examination (artifacts)
•
Interview
•
Observation/Test
•
Evidence must be applicable to the practice or control being evaluated.
•
The Evidence accepted must:
•
Be relevant to the domain, practice or control.
•
Satisfy the practice objectives.
•
Fully represent the performance of the practice or control.

(adequacy) and if you have enough of that Evidence (sufficiency).

Evidence Collection,

--- PAGE 352 ---
Evidence Method Intent and Usage
•
Evidence methods for each domain, practice, and/or control to ascertain:
•
Context – Is the Evidence supportive and applicable to both Assessment scope and the practice objectives?
•
Integrity – Is the Evidence representative of the current practices of the OSC being assessed?
•
Degree of instantiation – Does the Evidence presented support the requirements of adequacy and sufficiency?
•
The results are used to support the determination of:
•
Security safeguard existence
•
Functionality
•
Correctness
•
Completeness
•
Potential for institutionalization and improvement over time
INTENT
Figure 7-9: The intent behind the Examine, Interview, and Test methods is to determine
institutionalization of security safeguards.

340 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic A
Evidence Method Intent
and Usage

--- PAGE 353 ---
Evidence Acceptability
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Method
Acceptability
Examine
For an artifact to be accepted as Evidence in an Assessment, it must:
•
Demonstrate the extent of implementing, performing, or supporting the organizational or
project procedures that can be mapped to one or more CMMC practices;
•
Be produced by people who implement or perform or support the procedures.
Interview
For an interview statement to be accepted as Evidence in an Assessment, it must:
•
Demonstrate the extent of implementing, performing, or supporting the CMMC practice;
•
Be provided by people who implement, perform, or support procedures.
Test
For a test/demonstration to be accepted as Evidence in an Assessment, it must:
•
Pass its requirements and criteria while being observed by the Assessment Team.
Any failed test results in a failed CMMC practice.
Figure 7-10: For Evidence to be acceptable, it must be created, provided, or demonstrated by
people who implement the practice.

Evidence Acceptability

--- PAGE 354 ---
Organization of Evidence in Preparation for an Assessment
•
As a reminder, for each in-scope practice, the OSC must demonstrate the following:
•
The practice is being performed.
•
The control, and any related policy, plan, or procedure is effectively implemented.
•
The Evidence is current and produced by those members of the OSC performing the task.
•
Evidence should be organized in a manner that:
•
Supports Assessment objectives.
•
Provides for ease of access, interpretation, and review.
•
The OSC should maintain a “mapping” of Evidence to its source, including:
•
Traceability to domain/practice
•
Artifact name/version number/date
•
Comments or contextual data
•

Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic A
OSC Documents Helpful

--- PAGE 356 ---
Access Limitations to the Organization's CUI and FCI
•
The OSC determines what information the Assessment Team has access to and what they can take
off site post-assessment.
•
Most Assessment Examine, Interview, and Test steps will be focused on systems and their
controls—not the data flowing through those systems.
•
Assessors will obviously see some data, that cannot be helped, but it will only be tangential to their
Assessment, not the focus of it.
•
During the scoping decision-making for the Assessment, decisions are made as to which systems have
FCI/CUI, so there is no need to confirm that fact.
•
If the Assessment Team requests to take information off site:
•
The OSC will likely be very resistant to such requests.
•
The OSC will probably demand an NDA or other protections from the Assessment Team.

ACTIVITY 7-1
Determining Evidence
Scenario
You are helping an OSC prepare for a Level 2 CMMC Assessment. As part of that process, you are
helping them determine what Evidence they can use to demonstrate they have implemented
required practices.
Your current task is to obtain Evidence that removable media, such as USB thumb drives and
external hard drives, can't be mounted on the systems that are in scope.
1.
Through discussion with the OSC's IT administrator, you identify which
machines have been configured with a security control that prevents USB
drives from being mounted. What Assessment method have you used here?
○
Examine
○
Test
○
Interview
2.
You insert a thumb drive into a computer's USB port. In this case, what
method are you most likely using to obtain Evidence?
○
Examine
○
Test
○
Interview
3.
Next, you have the IT administrator open some configuration utilities to
confirm how the computer was configured to prevent the USB drive from
being mounted. What method are you using to obtain Evidence in this case?
○
Examine
○
Test
○
Interview
4.
You are trying to find Evidence to prove that a particular practice has been
put into place at the OSC. How might you come up with ideas for the type of
Evidence you might use?
A: A good place to begin is by interviewing the people at the OSC who have implemented the
practice in question. Also, you can turn to the CMMC documentation for guidance. For example,
the CMMC Assessment Guide not only identifies the practices that must be evaluated, it also
provides tips regarding the security controls and types of Evidence you might look for. You can
refer to the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) document for guidelines on evaluating the quality
or suitability of the Evidence you find. Of course, your own experience working with information
systems can also be quite helpful in informing what type of Evidence you should look for.

Structure of Practice Descriptions
•
Practice Number, Name, and Statement
•
The number, short name identifier, and the complete CMMC practice statement.
•
Assessment Objectives [SOURCE]
•
Specific list of objectives that must be met to fulfill the requirement.
•
Potential Assessment Methods and Objects [SOURCE]
•
Discussion [SOURCE]
•
Information from the original source of the requirement (e.g., NIST SP 800˗171 R2).
•
Further Discussion
•
Information about the intent of the practice.
•
Examples for insight that are not intended to be prescriptive or comprehensive.
•
Assessment considerations and potential questions.
•
Key References
•
The related security requirements from FAR Clause 52.204˗21 and NIST SP 800˗171 R2.
•
For example, NIST SP 800˗171 Rev 2 3.1.1.
•
Sources that provide information to further understand the concepts.
•
Remember, the CMMC Assessment Guides and the CMMC Model Overview are the only authoritative
sources for CMMC practices.

CMMC Assessment
Guide Procedure

--- PAGE 364 ---
Assessment Methods and Objects Used by CCPs and CCAs
Examine
The process of reviewing, inspecting, observing, studying, or analyzing assessment objects (i.e.,
specifications, mechanisms, activities). The results are used in making specific determinations called
for in the determination statements and thereby achieving the objectives for the assessment
procedure.
Objects
Specifications
Examples: policies, plans, procedures, system requirements, designs.
Mechanisms
Examples: functionality implemented in hardware, software, firmware.
Activities
Examples: system operations, administration, management, exercises.
Interview
The process of holding discussions with individuals or groups of individuals to facilitate
understanding, achieve clarification, or obtain evidence. The results are used in making specific
determinations called for in the determination statements and thereby achieving the objectives for
the assessment procedure.
Objects
Individuals or
Groups
Examples: Personnel with risk assessment responsibilities; personnel with
information security responsibilities; system or network administrators;
personnel with account management responsibilities.
Test
The process of exercising assessment objects (i.e., activities, mechanisms) under specified
conditions to compare actual with expected behavior. The results are used in making specific
determinations called for in the determination statements and thereby achieving the objectives for
the assessment procedure.
Objects
Mechanisms
Examples: hardware, software, firmware.
Activities
Examples: system operations, administration, management, exercises.
Source: CMMC Assessment Guide – Level 2
Figure 7-20: The three CMMC Assessment methods used by CCPs and CCAs are Examine,
Interview, and Test.

Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic B
Example: Separation of
Duties

--- PAGE 372 ---
Separation of Duties: Discussion
AC.L2
-3.1.4
Separate the duties of individuals to reduce the risk of malevolent activity without collusion.
DISCUSSION: [NIST SP 800
-
171 R2]
•
Separation of duties addresses the potential for abuse of authorized privileges and helps to
reduce the risk of malevolent activity without collusion. Separation of duties includes dividing
mission functions and system support functions among different individuals or roles;
conducting system support functions with different individuals (e.g., configuration
management, quality assurance and testing, system management, programming, and network
security); and ensuring that security personnel administering access control functions do not
also administer audit functions. Because separation of duty violations can span systems and
application domains, organizations consider the entirety of organizational systems and system
components when developing policy on separation of duties.
Figure 7-28: All practices include Discussion from a relevant source.
Notes
360 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic B
Separation of Duties:
Discussion

--- PAGE 373 ---
Separation of Duties: Further Discussion
AC.L2
-3.1.4
Separate the duties of individuals to reduce the risk of malevolent activity without collusion.
FURTHER DISCUSSION:
No one person should be in charge of an entire critical task from beginning to end. Documenting and dividing
elements of important duties and tasks between employees reduces intentional or unintentional execution of
malicious activities.
Example 1
•
You are responsible for the management of several key systems within your organization. You assign the task
of reviewing the system logs to two different people. This way, no one person is solely responsible for the
execution of this critical security function [c].
Example 2
•
You are a system administrator. Human Resources notifies you of a new hire, and you create an account with
general privileges, but you are not allowed to grant access to systems that contain CUI [a, b]. The program
manager contacts the team in your organization that has system administration authority over the CUI systems
and informs them which CUI the new hire will need to access. Subsequently, a second system administrator
grants access privileges to the new hire [c].
Potential Assessment Considerations
•
Does system documentation identify the system functions or processes that require separation of duties (e.g.,
function combinations that represent a conflict of interest or an over-allocation of security privilege for one
individual [a]?
Figure 7-29: All practices have a Further Discussion section that provides some descriptive (not
prescriptive) Examples and Potential Assessment Considerations.
Notes

Further Discussion

Discussion and Further Discussion Sections
•
Do not read too much into the Discussion or Further Discussion sections.
•
Not comprehensive.
•
Descriptive about possible ways to address the practice.
•
Not prescriptive.
•
Don’t consider these sections directions on how practices should be implemented.
•
Focus on the Assessment Objectives.
•
Provide crucial detail on what will be assessed as MET/NOT MET.
•

•
“Separate the duties of individuals to reduce the risk of malevolent activity without collusion.”
•
[a] {determine if} the duties of individuals requiring separation are defined.

processes that require separation of duties (e.g., function combinations that represent a conflict of interest

Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic B
Discussion and Further
Discussion Sections

--- PAGE 375 ---
CMMC Assessment Procedures
•
The Assessment Team must assess all Assessment Objectives for each practice.
•
Each Assessment Objective is scored as MET, NOT MET, or N/A.
•
CCPs can only assess and score Level 1 practices.
•
CCAs can assess and score Levels 1 and 2 practices.
•
Where multiple systems or systems of type overlap to provide the capability (e.g., on -premise file share and
Office 365 Teams), each system should be evaluated independently.
•
The Assessment Team meets daily with the OSC participants to review preliminary results.
•
Daily sessions are intended to iteratively validate the observations and ultimately inform the final scores of
the in-scope practices.
•
Generate and deliver preliminary recommended findings.
•
Provides a summarization of all practice MET/NOT MET scores indicating the extent to which the in -scope
practice, control, or related procedures and policies meet the intent of CMMC practices.
•
Provides opportunity for feedback by and between the OSC and the Lead Assessor/CCA /CCP.
•
Must be presented prior to Final Findings.
•
During these sessions, OSC participants are given the opportunity to:
•
Verify the Assessment Team’s understanding of the practices to validate
preliminary results.
•
Provide context for Evidence that has been reviewed.
•
Provide additional Evidence for the Assessment Team to review.
Figure 7-31: CMMC Assessments are comprehensive and all Assessment Objectives must be
fulfilled in order to achieve a MET score for a practice.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 363
Lesson 7: Determining and Assessing Evidence | Topic B
CMMC Assessment
Procedures

--- PAGE 376 ---
Steps Used by CCPs and CCAs to Assess the Assessment
Objectives
•
Use the Assessment Guide to review the practice.
•
Follow these steps, for each assessment objective (AO) for every practice:
1.
Identify the evidence you have or need to collect.
2.
Mark the assessment methods.
3.
Determine if the Evidence is adequate and sufficient.
•
Does the evidence provided meet the minimum intent of the AO? If so, how?
•
Does a specific policy or procedure exist that tells how to meet this objective? If so, how?
•
Has the OSC provided any other evidence that the OSC is meeting the AO?
4.
Determine if there are any indicators that adversely impact satisfying the AO.
•
Are there any areas where the OSC is not meeting the AO? If so, name the areas.
•
After reviewing all the evidence, are there any areas of conflict? Do these conflicts require
clarification, or do they adversely impact the AO?
5.
Determine the assessment finding – MET, NOT MET, or NA.
6.
Write a rationale for your finding.
Figure 7-32: For each practice, CCPs and CCAs must assess every Assessment Objective (AO).

Assessment Findings
Score
Description
MET
•
The OSC successfully meets all objectives for the practice.
•
For each practice marked MET, a CCP or a CCA:
•
Includes statements that indicate the response conforms to all objectives.
•
Documents the appropriate evidence to support the response.
NOT MET
•
The OSC has not met all objectives for the practice.
•
For each practice marked NOT MET, a CCP or a CCA:
•
Includes explanation why the contractor does not conform fully to all objectives.
•
Documents the appropriate evidence.
NOT APPLICABLE
(N/A)
•
The practice does not apply for the assessment.
•
Lead Assessor is responsible for verifying that the CMMC practice is truly not applicable.
•
For each practice marked N/A:
•
Lead Assessor explains why the practice does not apply to the contractor.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4

--- PAGE 381 ---
Guidelines for Implementing the CMMC Criteria and
Assessment Practices
•
CMMC Assessments are comprehensive assessments at all levels.
•
CMMC Level 1 Self-Assessments follow the CMMC Self-Assessment Guide—Level 1.
•
Which is based on the NIST SP 800˗171A.
•
CMMC Level 2 Practices are evaluated per the defined Objectives, Methods, and Objects in the
CMMC Assessment Guide—Level 2.
•
The Lead Assessor/CCA has the authority to:
•
Evaluate any practice and system or system of type in scope.
•
Interview and meet with any people involved with the related practice or system management.
•
Each Objective must be satisfied based on Evidence from Interviews, Examination, or Testing.
•
Each Assessment Method can be used multiple times per Objective.
•
The Lead Assessor/CCA determines the number and type of Evidence that are needed.
•
Objective Assessments for each practice/system in scope roll -up at the lowest level of
compliance/conformity.
•
If System X is non˗conformant on one requirement objective while the other systems
are conformant, then the overall Objective is evaluated as Fail.
•
If any of the rolled˗up Objectives are scored as Fail, then the requirement Fails.

C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources\AG_Level1_V2.0_FinalDraft_20211210_508.pdf
Scenario
For a CMMC Assessment, the OSC must be prepared to demonstrate how they are meeting each
requirement they are responsible for. If the requirement is not applicable, the OSC should be able to
justify that as well. So to prepare for the Assessment, you must determine what the requirements are
for the particular Level the OSC is targeting. In this case, Rudison is looking to obtain CMMC
Certification at Level 1, so you will refer to the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification and
CMMC Self-Assessment Guide – Level 1 documents to determine what requirements Rudison
must meet.
1.
Examine the type of guidance provided by the CMMC Model documentation.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named
ModelOverview_V2.0_FINAL2_20211202_508.pdf.
b)
Navigate to the Table of Contents and examine what's contained in the document.
There is an Introduction and a Summary, but the bulk of the document is the information on the
CMMC Model itself. This middle section provides some background on the concept of a Maturity
Model, and provides an overview. The remainder covers the model's levels, domains, and practices.
c)
Navigate to the section on CMMC Practices.
d)
Scroll through this section until you find the List of Practices.
2.
What is the first CMMC Level 1 practice in the list?
A: AC.L1-3.1.1 is the first practice listed for CMMC Level 1.
3.
Read the description of AC.L1-3.1.1. Does this give you enough information
to determine if this is being performed in a client’s environment?
A: The description is "Limit information system access to authorized users, processes acting on
behalf of authorized users, or devices (including other information systems)." This does not really
provide enough information to determine if this is being performed in the client's environment. The
first place to turn for more guidance is the CMMC Assessment Guides.
4.
Close the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification Model Overview
document.
5.
Examine the CMMC Self-Assessment Guide – Level 1 document.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named
AG_Level1_V2.0_FinalDraft_20211210_508.pdf.
b)
Review the Self-Assessment and Compliance and CMMC-Specific Terms sections.

--- PAGE 383 ---
6.
Why do you think these terms were identified as being important to Level 1
self-assessment?
A: Asset, component, and information systems are terms critical to defining the scope of the self-
assessment. Monitor and organizationally defined are terms used frequently in the practice
descriptions so it is important to know how they are defined in the context of CMMC.
7.
Examine the type of guidance provided for the practice descriptions.
a)
Referring to the Table of Contents, navigate to the section on Access Control (AC).
b)
Under the sections for Level 1 AC Practices, examine the documentation provided on AC.L1-3.1.1.
This section provides:
•
The Assessment Objectives for this practice.
•
Potential assessment methods and objects.
•
Discussion and further discussion on assessing the practice, including examples.
•
A list of key references.
c)
Examine the Assessment Objectives for AC.L1-3.1.1.
There are six different Assessment Objectives. All must be met in order for the practice to be
marked as compliant.
d)
Examine the Potential Assessment Methods and Objects for AC.L1-3.1.1.
For each of the three assessment methods, it lists suggestions for artifacts to examine, possible job
titles and common tasks of people to interview, and processes and mechanisms you might observe
as tests.
e)
Examine the Discussion and Further Discussion sections for AC.L1-3.1.1.
This provides some additional help. It tells you that you are expected to: control who can use
computers and who can log on to the company network; limit the services and devices, like printers,
that can be accessed by company computers; and, set up your system so that unauthorized users
and devices cannot get on the company network.
8.
If you have lingering questions about AC.L1-3.1.1, where can you find more
information to help you better understand the concepts in this practice?
A: As noted in the Key References section, you can read FAR Clause 52.204-21 b.1.i and also NIST
SP 800-171 Rev 2 requirement 3.1.1 to find additional information to further understand the
concepts.
9.
Leave the CMMC Self-Assessment Guide – Level 1 document open.

Before You Begin
The CMMC Self-Assessment Guide Level 1 is open on the page for AC.L1-3.1.1.
Scenario
You are a CCP working as a consultant with a client who doesn't have a lot of IT experience. You
decide to explore the supplemental documents that support CMMC to see if they provide any
additional explanation for implementing the CMMC practices.
1.
Find the NIST SP 800-171 requirement from the key references for CMMC
practice AC.L1-3.1.1.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named C:\093200Data
\CMMC Resources\NIST.SP.800-171r2.pdf.
b)
Navigate to page 22 to view Access Control requirement 3.1.1.
c)
Arrange your screen so that you can see the two documents side-by-side.
Notice how the requirement description for 3.1.1 is the same as AC.L1-3.1.1 and the Discussion
paragraphs are also the same.
2.
Determine the security controls that this requirement maps to.
a)
In the NIST document, select the 3.1.1 link to view Appendix D.
Appendix D maps the NIST SP 800-171 requirements to relevant security controls in NIST SP
800-53 and ISO/IEC 27001. These tables are only for information and do not convey any additional
security requirements.
3.
What three security controls from NIST SP 800-53 are listed for the Access
Control requirement 3.1.1?
A: AC-2 Account Management, AC-3 Access Enforcement, and AC-17 Remote Access.
4.
Review the NIST SP 800-53 security controls.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named C:\093200Data
\CMMC Resources\NIST.SP.800-53r5.pdf.
b)
Navigate to page 46 to look at the first mapped security control.
5.
How might you use this information with your client?
A: This appears to lay out the details of account management in a way that a non-IT person can
understand. You might use these details as a checklist of to-do items to guide your client toward
successfully implementing AC.L1-3.1.1 for CMMC.
6.
Close all of the PDF files.

--- PAGE 385 ---
Summary
In this lesson, you learned about the Assessment Methods used to assess Evidence and the
importance of collecting adequate and sufficient Evidence to prove the OSC has met the standard
for each CMMC practice. You also learned how to use the Assessment Guides to assess practices.
You learned that each practice has specific Assessment Objectives that must all be met for that
practice to be marked as MET during the CMMC Assessment. You learned that the Discussion and
Further Discussion sections provide additional information to guide an OSC in preparing for an
Assessment and also help the Assessment Team Members who are participating in an Assessment.
These sections provide examples that are descriptive not prescriptive. Each OSC will determine the
best way for their environment to meet the Assessment Objectives.
Which section of the Assessment Guide structure will be the most helpful in
guiding your work as CCP?
A: Answers may vary.
What do you think will be the most challenging part of assessing practices?
A: Answers may vary.
Note: Check your CHOICE Course screen for opportunities to interact with your classmates,
peers, and the larger CHOICE online community about the topics covered in this course or
other topics you are interested in. From the Course screen you can also access available
resources for a more continuous learning experience.

--- PAGE 387 ---
Implementing and
Evaluating Level 1
Lesson Time: 2 hours, 55 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you have been introduced to preparing for the Assessment process by collecting
and assessing Evidence, you are ready to evaluate the Organization Seeking Certification
(OSC) against the CMMC Level 1 requirements. CMMC Level 1 is designed for those
companies who handle Federal Contract Information (FCI), and must secure that sensitive
data in order to accurately attest to their compliance with CMMC Level 1 practices. In this
lesson, you will learn about the Level 1 practices, how to determine an OSC's Assessment
readiness with a Gap Analysis, and assess the Level 1 practices.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Identify the domains and practices for basic cyber hygiene at CMMC Level 1.
•
Perform a CMMC Level 1 gap analysis.
•
Assess the CMMC Level 1 practices.
8

--- PAGE 388 ---
TOPIC A
Identify CMMC Level 1 Domains and Practices
Before diving into the details of the CMMC Level 1 requirements, it's helpful to understand the
connections between the FAR regulation for FCI and CMMC Level 1. In this topic, you will be
introduced to the 17 practices in the six domains that must be met for Level 1 certification.
Equivalency of FAR 52.204-21 and CMMC Level 1
PE. L1-3.10.3
Escort visitors and monitor visitor activity.
PE. L1-3.10.4
Maintain audit logs of physical access.
PE. L1-3.10.5
Control and manage physical access devices.
ix.
Escort visitors and monitor visitor activity;
maintain audit logs of physical access;
and control and manage physical access devices.
viii.
Limit physical access to organizational
information systems, equipment, and the
respective operating environments to
authorized individuals.
PE.L1-3.10.1
Limit physical access to organizational
information systems, equipment, and the
respective operating environments to
authorized individuals.
Figure 8-1: As shown, the FAR 52.204-21 clause ix was broken into three Level 1 practices in
CMMC. All of the other FAR 52.204-21 clauses have a one-to-one match with a CMMC Level 1
practice.

--- PAGE 389 ---
FAR 52.204-21 vs. CMMC – Importance to a CCP
•
FAR 52.204-21 is incorporated into most government contracts.
•
Even if left out, it is likely to be read into the contract under the Christian Doctrine.
•
Submitting an invoice equals an attestation contractor meets requirements of contract.
•
DoD believes a Level 1 certification should be straightforward for contractors.
•
However, some small businesses claim a Level 1 certification will require additional investments in
security.
•
OSCs need to understand potential implications of such assertions.
•
Opens them up to False Claims Act claims, potential debarment, business reputation harm, and more.
•
DoD assumes that OSCs with current contracts will have an easy time with Level 1.
•
Do a gap analysis to be sure and if necessary, make changes.
CCP
CCP
Figure 8-2: FAR 52.204-21 is incorporated into most government contracts already, so the DoD's
position is that CMMC Level 1 should be straightforward for most of its contractors.

AC.L1-3.1.1 Authorized Access Control
Access Control
AC
Practice ID
Practice Statement and Assessment Objectives
AC.L1-3.1.1
Limit information system access to authorized users, processes acting on behalf of authorized
users, or devices (including other information systems).
[a] authorized users are identified;
[b] processes acting on behalf of authorized users are identified;
[c] devices (and other systems) authorized to connect to the system are identified;
[d] system access is limited to authorized users;
[e] system access is limited to processes acting on behalf of authorized users; and
[f] system access is limited to authorized devices (including other systems).
Level
AC Practices
Assessment
Objectives
1
4
19
More AC practices
Figure 8-4: At Level 1, there are four practices in the Access Control domain which involve
knowing who is accessing your networks. AC.L1-3.1.1 addresses authorized access control.
Notes
Key References
•
FAR Clause 52.204-21 b.1.i
•
NIST SP 800-171 Rev 2 3.1.1
•
NIST Handbook 162 Section 3.1.1
Assessment Methods for AC.L1-3.1.1
•
Examine: Access control policy; procedures addressing account management; system security
plan; system design documentation; system configuration settings and associated documentation;
list of active system accounts and the name of the individual associated with each account;
notifications or records of recently transferred, separated, or terminated employees; list of
conditions for group and role membership; list of recently disabled system accounts along with
the name of the individual associated with each account; access authorization records; account
management compliance reviews; system monitoring records; system audit logs and records; list
of devices and systems authorized to connect to organizational systems; other relevant
documents or records.
•
Interview: Personnel with account management responsibilities; system or network
administrators; personnel with information security responsibilities.
•
Test: Organizational processes for managing system accounts; mechanisms for implementing
account management.

AC.L1-3.1.22 Control Public Information
Access Control
AC
Practice ID
Practice Statement and Assessment Objectives
AC.L1-3.1.22
Control information posted or processed on publicly available information systems.
[a] individuals authorized to post or process information on publicly accessible systems are
identified;
[b] procedures to ensure FCI is not posted or processed on publicly accessible systems are
identified;
[c] a review process is in place prior to posting of any content to publicly accessible systems;
[d] content on publicly accessible systems is reviewed to ensure that it does not include FCI; and
[e] mechanisms are in place to remove and address improper posting of FCI.
Level
AC Practices
Assessment
Objectives
1
4
19

Assessment Methods for AC.L1-3.1.20
•
Examine: Access control policy; procedures addressing publicly accessible content; system
security plan; list of users authorized to post publicly accessible content on organizational
systems; training materials and/or records; records of publicly accessible information reviews;
records of response to nonpublic information on public websites; system audit logs and records;
security awareness training records; other relevant documents or records.
•
Interview: Personnel with responsibilities for managing publicly accessible information posted
on organizational systems; personnel with information security responsibilities.
•
Test: Mechanisms implementing management of publicly accessible content.

Identification

Authenticate (or verify) the identities of those users, processes, or devices, as a prerequisite to

prerequisite to system access.

TOPIC B
Perform a CMMC Level 1 Gap Analysis
In an earlier lesson, you identified how Federal Contract Information (FCI) flows within an
organization and the portions of the OSC environment are likely to be in scope for the Assessment
and Certification. Now you can analyze the in-scope systems and evaluate them against the CMMC
Level 1 practices to see where gaps exist.
CMMC Level 1 Gap Analysis
Before you begin, inventory all the systems.
1.
Catalog the sensitive information.
2.
Determine how sensitive information moves.
1.
Received from the government?
2.
Generated by OSC?
3.
Accessible to or shared with others?
4.
Who in OSC has access to it?
5.
Will sensitive information be sent to the
government?
3.
Identify systems and enabling systems in scope.
Once scope has been established, the systems in
scope can be evaluated against the targeted
CMMC Level.

evaluating the systems in scope against the CMMC Level requirements.

Guidelines for Performing a Level 1 Gap Analysis
•
Review all practices and their associated Assessment Objectives.
•
Ensure all applicable practices and Assessment Objectives are currently being met.
•
Document how the practices and Assessment Objectives are being met.
•
Identify additional Evidence that will be used to demonstrate adoption of the practice.
•
The more detailed the Evidence, the more confident the OSC will be in its Level 1 self -assessment.

Data File

Scenario
Now you will conduct a gap analysis of the Rudison cybersecurity program against the CMMC
Model's Level 1 requirements. Your materials include a CMMC Level 1 Gap Analysis Tool, an Excel
worksheet that lists each of the Assessment Objectives for the Level 1 practices. You use it to track
the implementation status of each objective, including notes that describe how the objective is met.
Those notes can include copies of, or links to, screen captures, external files, or other Evidence that
demonstrates how the objective is met.
1.
Open the Gap Analysis Tool.
a)
In your course data files at C:\093200Data\Implementing and Evaluating Level 1, open the Excel
workbook file named CMMC L1 Gap Analysis Tool.xlsx.
The gap analysis tool is an Excel workbook that lists each of the Assessment Objectives for the
various Level 1 practices. You use it to track the implementation status of each objective, including
notes that describe how the objective is met. Those notes can include copies of, or links to, screen
captures, external files, or other Evidence that demonstrates how the objective is met. This
workbook contains two worksheet tabs, Introduction and CMMC L1 Practices.
2.
Save a copy of the file.
a)
Save a copy of the workbook in the same folder, naming it My Rudison L1 Gap Analysis.xlsx.
3.
Provide basic information about the organization and the gap analysis.
a)
In the CMMC L1 Practices worksheet, select cell C2, and type
Rudison Technologies
to enter the
name of the OSC.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 401
Lesson 8: Implementing and Evaluating Level 1 | Topic B
Activity

--- PAGE 414 ---
b)
In cell E2, enter today's date.
c)
Select cell C3.
This is where you will enter the Commercial and Government Entity (CAGE) code. The CAGE code:
•
Is a unique, 5 character identifier that either NATO or the Defense Logistics Agency assigns to
organizations, depending on the organization's location.
•
Allows for consistent identification of an organization as it grows or changes, such as through a
merger or acquisition.
•
Can be obtained for a client who does not already have one by registering in the System for
Award Management, or SAM system at https://www.SAM.gov or via the NCAGE Code Request
Tool at https://eportal.nspa.nato.int/AC135Public/CageTool/home.
d)
Type
AAAAA
and press Enter.
For the purposes of this activity, you'll enter an imaginary CAGE code for Rudison.
e)
Select cell E3.
This cell will contain the names of team members involved in the gap analysis. This typically
includes employees of the organization being analyzed, as well as members of the consulting
company that is leading the gap analysis.
f)
Type
Rudison: Jane Doe, Chris Deer, Mark Ray; Consultant: Alex Drop, [Your Name]
The Rudison employees Jane, Chris, and Mark are all participating in the analysis, so they are
listed. You and Alex are members of the consulting company that is leading the gap analysis for
Rudison.
g)

Collaboration Environment, Development Environment, End-user Equipment
The systems you will analyze include the online collaboration environment, which in this case is the

a)
Examine the practices listed in column A, scrolling down as needed to see all of them.
Each of the practices required for L1 are listed in column A, along with a description of each practice
in column B.

Scroll back to the top of the worksheet.

The objectives associated with each practice are listed next to the practice. In the example of
AC.L1-3.1.1, there are six associated practices (a through f).
d)
Examine the implementation status cells in column D.
For each practice associated with a particular process, a cell is provided for you to note its
implementation status. Initially, all of these are set to "Not Yet Analyzed". You will change the value
in each of these cells as you perform the gap analysis.
e)

Since the CMMC Assessment Team will be looking for Evidence to back up practices the OSC

how it was determined, and the date the practices were implemented.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 403
Lesson 8: Implementing and Evaluating Level 1 | Topic B

--- PAGE 416 ---
f)
Examine the columns provided to record Plans of Action and Milestones in columns K, L, M, and N.
For any objectives that are not yet fully implemented, you should consider creating a Plan of Action
and corresponding Milestones, which you can record in these columns. The POA&M portion of the
worksheet is based on guidance from NIST SP 800-53, specifically CA-5 and PM-4. To use it, you
list the organization responsible for implementing the objective, the estimated resources needed to
implement the objective, and a series of projected milestones that are to be achieved along the path
to implementation. You should also track any changes to those milestones, such as acceleration of
the projected implementation timelines.
Note: To be clear, a POA&M is not explicitly required at Level 1. However,
using one as part of your gap analysis gives the organization's management a
concrete, structured view into the steps needed to achieve Level 1
certification, and the creation of a POA&M is considered a best practice.
Therefore, you are strongly encouraged to create one.

Minimize the Excel application window.

ACTIVITY 8-3
Analyzing Technical Cybersecurity Controls
Data Files
C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources\AG_Level1_V2.0_FinalDraft_20211210_508.pdf

Before You Begin
My Rudison L1 Gap Analysis.xlsx is open in Excel, in a minimized window.
Scenario
You are preparing to perform a gap analysis for Rudison's implementation of Level 1 practices. You
have looked for guidance from the CMMC documentation. While you would normally analyze all 17
Level 1 practices in the Rudison environment, to save class time, you will focus on just the technical
practices listed below.
•
AC.L1-3.1.1 – Limit information system access to authorized users, processes acting on behalf of
authorized users, or devices (including other information systems).
•
AC.L1-3.1.2 – Limit information system access to the types of transactions and functions that
authorized users are permitted to execute.
You will begin by looking at AC.L1-3.1.1. In the Rudison example, this practice provides a typical
example of cybersecurity controls you might need to evaluate.
1.
Refer to the Assessment Guide to determine the assessment criteria for
AC.L1-3.1.1.
a)
Navigate to the C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources folder, and open the file named
AG_Level1_V2.0_FinalDraft_20211210_508.pdf.
This PDF contains the CMMC Self-Assessment Guide — Level 1, Version 2.0.
b)
Locate the page that contains the assessment criteria for AC.L1-3.1.1.

c)

When assessors evaluate whether the AC.L1-3.1.1 practice is met, among other things, they must

Get guidance on how to determine whether authorized users are identified.
a)
Scroll down, if necessary, so you can view the Potential Assessment Considerations for
AC.L1-3.1.1.
b)
Observe the first consideration in this section.
•
Regarding the users' home and mobile devices, you know that they require a username and
password to gain access to the device.
•
Regarding the development and collaboration environments, you'll have to determine how you
can prove that access is limited to only authorized users. The Potential Assessment Methods
and Objectives section might provide some useful ideas for you to do this.

--- PAGE 419 ---
c)
Scroll up, if necessary, so you can see the Potential Assessment Methods and Objects [NIST SP
800-171A] section for AC.L1-3.1.1.
3.
Based on the ideas listed in this section of the Assessment Guide, what
methods and objects might you use to prove that access to the development
and collaboration environments is limited to only authorized users?
A: Looking specifically at Rudison, you could demonstrate that the objective is met by supplying a list
of all employees and their roles in the organization. This is illustrated in Example 1 a bit further
down in the Assessment Guide. It is important to remember that the assessment method and
objects listed are suggested, not mandatory, and that Rudison doesn’t need to demonstrate that
they have every assessment method and object covered. For example, the assessment methods
and objects listed under AC.L1-3.1.1 in the CMMC Level 1 Self-Assessment Guide come directly
from NIST SP 800-171A, and they include looking at procedures addressing account
management. In Level 1, procedures can be performed on an undocumented basis, so assessors
should not expect Rudison to have written processes. In your gap analysis and its associated
POA&M, you do not need to worry about creating written practice documentation.

Open the Employee List.
a)
Open C:\093200Data\Implementing and Evaluating Level 1\Rudison\EmpList.xlsx.
b)
Review the file.

Document Rudison's implementation status for Objective A of AC.L1-3.1.1.
a)
Restore the Excel application window.
The My Rudison L1 Gap Analysis.xlsx workbook is still open in the application, and the CMMC L1
Practices worksheet tab is selected.
b)
Select cell D7.
Initially, this status has been set to Not Yet Analyzed.

The next objective for AC.L1-3.1.1 is to determine if "processes acting on behalf of authorized users
are identified".
7.
The processes referred to in this objective include, for example, any
processes handling automatic updates of software in the system. Each such
process should be associated with the user who authorized the process to run
and should be tracked. So, what does this mean?
A: As a practical matter, this is a situation in which a user enters credentials into some software (i.e.,
a process) and that software does things on the user's behalf. One easy example of this is a
cloud-based backup system. The user has an account in the cloud-based backup system, and the
agent running on the user's computer (or, to use the language from the practice, the "process
acting on behalf of the user") uses that account to back up the data from the user's computer to
the cloud storage. To meet this objective, we need to be able to demonstrate that we know all of
those processes running in the Rudison systems.
8.
Based on the information provided, do we know all of the processes running
in the Rudison systems?
A: Not from the information we have been given.

Complete the implementation status for Objective B.
a)
Return to the Excel application window, and in cell D8, select Not Yet Addressed.
The objective has been analyzed, but not yet addressed. The “Description of How Objective is Met”
and “Date Met” fields would remain blank at this point.

15.
How would you demonstrate that Rudison's systems limit access to
authorized users?
A: You can take a screen capture of the accounts in the development and collaboration systems, and
show that only users who are defined in the employee list have accounts in those systems. You
can also show that the end-users' mobile devices are locked by a password or other identifier
(e.g., biometrics) and that the users' computers are set up to require the user to log in (i.e., they
do not automatically log in without a password).
16.
Evaluate Rudison's implementation of AC.L1-3.1.2.
a)
In the Assessment Guide, locate the page that contains the Assessment criteria for AC.L1-3.1.2.
b)
Examine the objectives listed for AC.L1-3.1.2.
c)

Rudison, how you might evaluate them, and how you might remediate if they are not already being
met.
17.
Are either of the underlying objectives for AC.L1-3.1.2 met? If not, what would
be a good plan of action for meeting them?
A: Rudison has only 3 employees and, given the work they do for the company, they all need access
to the FCI that is in the environment. The "Further Discussion" section of this practice states "Limit
users to only the information systems, roles, or applications they are permitted to use and are
needed for their roles and responsibilities [a]. Limit access to applications and data based on the
authorized users' role and responsibilities [b]. Common types of functions a user can be assigned
are create, read, update, and delete." In Rudison's case, the company limits access to the
development and collaboration environments to only those whose roles and responsibilities
require access to such environments. On the home computers, we know that the users must log in
to each device, which limits access to any applications on those devices to only the logged-in
user. Rudison, therefore, limits users to only the information systems, roles, and applications they
are permitted to use and are needed for their roles and responsibilities, so they have successfully
implemented the first objective. We also know that Chris has access to QuickBooks while the
others do not. Therefore, we can demonstrate that although all of the employees have access to
both the development and collaboration environments, it is because they need access, and that
Rudison does limit access to applications and data based on the users' roles and responsibilities,
as evidenced by their QuickBooks implementation. We would collect Evidence, including screen
captures of the QuickBooks access controls, as well as the screen captures and other information
used in AC.L1-3.1.1, to demonstrate the successful adoption.

ACTIVITY 8-4
Analyzing Non-Technical Cybersecurity
Controls
Before You Begin
You've already opened My Rudison L1 Gap Analysis.xlsx in Excel and the CMMC Self-
Assessment Guide — Level 1 in your PDF reader.
Scenario
If an organization has FCI in a particular environment (e.g., an office, testing environment, or
equipment room), technical assets, and non-technical assets, they are expected to know which
individuals are allowed physical access to those environments or assets. Specific environments must
be limited to authorized employees, and access is controlled with badges, electronic locks, physical
key locks, etc. In addition, output devices, such as printers, must be placed in areas where their use
does not expose data to unauthorized individuals. The organization is expected to develop and
maintain lists of personnel with authorized access, and personnel are issued appropriate
authorization credentials such as proximity cards, passcodes, combinations, or physical keys.
So far, you've analyzed Rudison's implementation of technical controls. But as these examples show,
not all cybersecurity controls involve "logical" or "technical" implementations. Some are "physical"
or "non-technical", such as walls, locked doors, and so forth. In this activity, you'll consider how you
might examine such controls by taking a look at:
•
PE.L1-3.10.1 – Limit physical access to organizational information systems, equipment, and the
respective operating environments to authorized individuals.
•
PE.L1-3.10.3 – Escort visitors and monitor visitor activity.
•
PE.L1-3.10.4 – Maintain audit logs of physical access.
•
PE.L1-3.10.5 – Control and manage physical access devices.
1.
Review the Level 1 physical protection practices in the Assessment Guide.
a)
In the Assessment Guide, using the Table of Contents, locate the page that contains the Level 1
physical protection practices for PE.L1-3.10.1.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 411
Lesson 8: Implementing and Evaluating Level 1 | Topic B
Activity

--- PAGE 424 ---
b)
Examine the description and objectives for this practice.
This practice involves making sure that only authorized individuals will have physical access to
sensitive systems, equipment, and operating environments. The four objectives needed to provide
the necessary controls are listed.
2.
Review an example provided in the Assessment Guide.
a)
Scroll down as needed to see the Example shown in the Further Discussion section, and read the
example.
These brief case studies help to illustrate each practice in a real-world context, and how they might
be assessed.
3.
For Rudison, do you know which individuals are allowed physical access to
each environment?
A: For their individual homes, that is likely to be each individual. At this point, you may not yet know if
anyone else has access to their home. So, for now we can't mark any of these objectives as “Fully
Implemented” because we need a list of the individuals who are allowed access, and we need to
be able to demonstrate that, at least for the environments containing the FCI, access to those
environments is limited to only the authorized individuals. Remember, too, that we are looking not
only at electronic equipment, such as computers and printers, but also paper copies of
documents. Access to all FCI must be limited to only those with appropriate permissions. For
example, we would need to know who else has keys to their homes. If others, such as a landlord,
have keys and can access the home without their knowledge, we will need to ensure that the
environment in which the FCI is stored (e.g., an office or closet in the home) has additional access
controls, such as additional locks, that keep those other people from accessing the FCI. Once we
identify this as a gap, we then turn to closing the gaps. In this case, we would help Rudison create
lists of the different environments, who has access to them, and how they are protected.
412 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 8: Implementing and Evaluating Level 1 | Topic B

--- PAGE 425 ---
4.
Once we've analyzed their home environments, are we finished with
PE.L1-3.10.1?
A: No, we aren't. Rudison has data in the collaboration and development environments, too. Those
are maintained by Microsoft and Amazon, respectively.
5.
So, how do we demonstrate that those environments have appropriate
physical access controls in place?
A: In some cases, service providers like Amazon and Microsoft will have their environments CMMC-
certified as well. An assessor may accept their certification as evidence that they are meeting
certain practices, like PE.L1-3.10.1. It should be noted, however, that assessors may not accept
certifications, on their own, as evidence for some practices which are more implementation-
specific. In some cases, Rudison will need to work with the service provider to obtain the
necessary Evidence to demonstrate that the control is met. For our purposes, since neither
Amazon nor Microsoft have environments that are CMMC-certified at this time, and since Rudison
does not have other evidence that the environments meet the physical access control
requirements, this practice would have to remain in a state other than "Fully Implemented" until
this information is obtained.

a)

that Rudison already has some homework to do before they will be ready for an Assessment.

TOPIC C
Assess CMMC Level 1 Practices
As you have progressed through the Assessment preparation cycle, you performed a gap analysis
against the systems in scope to determine whether any changes were needed to the environment.
During the gap analysis, you also collected Evidence to support your position that the OSC has
sufficiently adopted the CMMC Level 1 practices. More than likely, your gap analysis identified
practices or objectives that were not met, and you’ve spent some time making changes to the OSC's
environment to address those shortcomings.
Implementing fixes for any gaps that are identified is beyond the scope of this course, and we're
now going to assume that you are confident that those are done. In this topic, you will collect and
review Evidence as part of a Level 1 Self-Assessment or as part of a Level 2 Assessment, working as
an Assessment Team Member under the direction of the Lead Assessor.

•

Level 1 Practices only.
•
Report findings to Lead Assessor.

Guidelines Used by CCPs/CCAs to Assess the Level 1 Practices
•
Be comprehensive.
•
Ensure the scope has been identified according to the CMMC Self-Assessment Scope—Level 1.
•
Use the self-assessment criteria and methodology in the CMMC Self-Assessment Guide—Level 1.
•
Follow these steps to assess every Assessment Objective (AO) for all Level 1 practices:
1.
Identify the Evidence you have or need to collect.
2.
Mark the assessment methods (Examine, Interview, Test).
3.
Determine if the Evidence is adequate and sufficient.
4.
Determine if there are any indicators that adversely impact satisfying the AO.
5.
Determine the assessment score – MET, NOT MET, or NA.
6.
Provide a rationale for the practice scores.
•
Ensure that evidence is adequate and sufficient to demonstrate adoption of every AO.
•
Do the implemented controls represent the controls as defined?
•
Does the Evidence provided meet the minimum intent of the AO? If so, how?
•
Does a specific policy or procedure exist that tells us how to meet this objective? If so, how?
•
Has the OSC provided any other Evidence that the OSC is meeting the AO?

yourself with a practice and its corresponding objectives. Look at the Assessment Methods and
Objects DoD and NIST suggest, to help you understand what they are looking for in this practice. If
you need more details, look to NIST SP 800-171A (although it is largely repeated in the Assessment
Guide). If you need even more information, look to NIST Handbook 162.

Summary
In this lesson, you identified the CMMC Level 1 practices in the six domains that DIB contractors
will need to satisfy in order to accurately self-attest to their CMMC Level 1 compliance. You learned
how to perform a gap analysis so as a CCP, you are able to help an OSC determine their readiness
before a self-assessment or a formal Assessment by a C3PAO. Additionally, you will want to be able
to validate the Evidence that is collected to ensure that a Level 1 self-assessment is comprehensive.
Which of the CMMC Level 1 practices do you think will be the most challenging
for OSCs to meet?
A: Answers will vary.
Which of the CMMC Level 1 practices do you think will be the most challenging
to assess?
A: Answers will vary.
