Identifying Level 2
Practices
Lesson Time: 1 hours, 20 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you have experienced performing a gap analysis and an Evidence Validation for
an Organization Seeking Certification (OSC) at CMMC Level 1, you are ready to explore the
more complex requirements for CMMC Level 2. Keep in mind that the Levels are
cumulative, so you might need to review lower-level practices and Assessment Objectives as
you evaluate the current CMMC Level. In this lesson, you will identify the practice and
Assessment Objective requirements for Level 2.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Identify the advanced CMMC practices at L2.
9

--- PAGE 434 ---
TOPIC A
Identify CMMC Level 2 Practices
At CMMC Level 2, most OSCs will need to undergo a third-party assessment. In addition to
working with a C3PAO to verify compliance, going from CMMC Level 1 to Level 2 is a huge jump
that includes increasing the number of practices from 17 to 110 practices. In this topic, the L2
practices will be covered.

L2 Audit and Accountability Practices
Audit and
Accountability
AU
Practice ID
Practice Statement
AU.L2-3.3.1
Create and retain system audit logs and records to the extent needed to enable the monitoring,
analysis, investigation, and reporting of unlawful or unauthorized system activity.
AU.L2-3.3.2
Ensure that the actions of individual system users can be uniquely traced to those users so they can
be held accountable for their actions.
AU.L2-3.3.3
Review and update logged events.
AU.L2-3.3.4
Alert in the event of an audit logging process failure.
AU.L2-3.3.5
Correlate audit record review, analysis, and reporting processes for investigation and response to
indications of unlawful, unauthorized, suspicious, or unusual activity.
AU.L2-3.3.6
Provide audit record reduction and report generation to support on -demand analysis and reporting.
AU.L2-3.3.7
Provide a system capability that compares and synchronizes internal system clocks with an
authoritative source to generate time stamps for audit records.
AU.L2-3.3.8
Protect audit information and audit logging tools from unauthorized access, modification, and
deletion.
AU.L2-3.3.9
Limit management of audit logging functionality to a subset of privileged users.

L2 Configuration Management Practices
Configuration
Management
CM
Practice ID
Practice Statement
CM.L2-3.4.1
Establish and maintain baseline configurations and inventories of organizational systems (including
hardware, software, firmware, and documentation) throughout the respective system development life
cycles.
CM.L2-3.4.2
Establish and enforce security configuration settings for information technology product employed in
organizational systems.
CM.L2-3.4.3
Track, review, approve or disapprove, and log changes to organizational systems.
CM.L2-3.4.4
Analyze the security impact of changes prior to implementation.
CM.L2-3.4.5
Define, document, approve, and enforce physical and logical access restrictions associated with changes
to organizational systems.
CM.L2-3.4.6
Employ the principle of least functionality by configuring organizational systems to provide only essential
capabilities.
CM.L2-3.4.7
Restrict, disable, or prevent the use of nonessential programs, functions, ports, protocols, and services.
CM.L2-3.4.8
Apply deny-by-exception (blacklisting) policy to prevent the use of unauthorized software or deny-all,
permit-by-exception (whitelisting) policy to allow the execution of authorized software.
CM.L2-3.4.9
Control and monitor user-installed software.

Scenario
As a CCP, you must understand all the practices in order to provide consulting services. In this
activity, you will examine the L2 practices.
1.
Open C:\093200Data\CMMC Resources
\AG_Level2_MasterV2.0_FINAL_202112016_508.pdf.
2.
Which characteristic of the CMMC Model best describes the rationale for
Level 1 practices being included in the Level 2 Certification?
○
Institutionalized
○
Cumulative
○
Restrictive
○
Documented
3.
It is often stated that human behavior is the biggest risk to cybersecurity.
Which Level 2 practice ensures that new employees have been properly
vetted to guarantee that they can be trusted to handle CUI? Where in the AG
did you find the answer?
A: Personnel Security Domain Practice PS.L2-3.9.1. The Discussion section lists the individual
characteristics that attest to a person's trustworthiness.
4.
Review the Assessment Objectives for PS.L2-3.9.1.
Note: OSC must meet all Assessment Objectives in order for the practice to be
MET. As a CCP, you need to thoroughly understand each Assessment Objective
for all 110 Levels 1 and 2 practices.
5.
As part of the Assessment, you are interviewing the OSC's legal counsel
about the organization's privacy and security notices for CUI rules. What
practice are you assessing?
A: AC.L2-3.1.9. One of the assessment methods lists interviewing personnel who are responsible for
providing legal advice to determine if the privacy and security notices required by CUI rules are
identified.
6.
Close all open files.

--- PAGE 453 ---
Working through an
Assessment
Lesson Time: 3 hours, 20 minutes
Lesson Introduction
Now that you are familiar with the practice requirements for CMMC Levels 1 and 2 and
how they are assessed, you can turn your focus to the practicalities of how an Assessment is
conducted. In this lesson, you will work through the Assessment process from the request
for an Assessment to the final recommended findings briefing with the Organization
Seeking Certification (OSC). You'll also be introduced to the POA&M Close-Out
Assessment phase.
Lesson Objectives
In this lesson, you will:
•
Identify the roles and responsibilities of the organizations and people involved in a
CMMC Assessment.
•
Define the logistics required to plan and prepare for an Assessment.
•
Conduct a CMMC Assessment.
•
Report the Assessment results.
•
Define the POA&M Close-Out Assessment process.
10

--- PAGE 454 ---
TOPIC A
Identify Assessment Roles and Responsibilities
Before you begin the formal Assessment process, it's important to understand the organizations and
individuals that are involved in a CMMC Assessment and their respective responsibilities.
Contextual CMMC Assessments
•
Each CMMC Assessment will be different:
•
There is no way to anticipate the many ways in which evidence may be presented across varying
environments and configurations.
•
Far too many different types of businesses, systems, and configurations to account for.
•
CMMC Assessments evaluate:
•
Each practice within the context of the OSC's organizational ecosystem.
•
The Assessment Team needs to research the organization to be successful.
No other
company is
quite the same
as me!
OSC

--- PAGE 456 ---
OSC Roles
OSC Assessment Official: The most senior representative of an Organization Seeking
Certification who is directly and actively responsible for leading and managing the OSC’s
engagement in the Assessment and who possesses decision -making authority for the OSC with
regard to the CMMC Assessment.
•
Must be an employee of the organization that is being assessed.
•
Only the OSC Assessment Official can agree to and sign the Assessment Plan.
Point-of-Contact (PoC): The individual who provides daily coordination and liaison support
between the OSC and the Assessment Team.
•
Does not necessarily have to be an employee of the organization that is being assessed.
•
Could be a contractor, consultant, or advisor like a CMMC Registered Practitioner (RP).
Figure 10-3: The role of the OSC Assessment Official and the Point-of-Contact (PoC) might be
filled by the same person or separate people depending on the size of the OSC.
Notes
444 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic A

OSC Assessment Official and PoC Responsibilities
OSC Assessment Official

Identify Assessment funding and authorize
payment.

Sign off on the Assessment scope and
boundaries.

Delegate a PoC to work directly with the
Assessment Team.

Approve Assessment Plan.

Encourage honesty and candor for interviewees
during the Assessment.

Review Assessment results with Lead Assessor.

Manage logistics such as having adequate space
for the team to meet with OSC representatives.

Coordinate site access and communicate visitation
policies.

Handle facility access and daily visitor escort.

Ensure that the necessary SMEs and people
familiar with the organization’s operations are
available.

Ensure access to requested artifacts and
supporting data.

Minimize disruptions for the Assessment Team.

C3PAO Roles
CMMC Third-Party Assessment Organization (C3PAO): An authorized and independent
conformity-Assessment body that contracts with the OSC to conduct CMMC Assessments and
issues the CMMC certification.
CMMC Quality Assurance Professional (CQAP): Individuals who are responsible for ensuring
the completeness and accuracy of the Assessment documentation.
Certified CMMC Assessor (CCA): Individuals with background, training, and examination
requirements outlined by The Cyber AB who are trained to conduct assessments up to a
specific CMMC Level.
CC
A
Lead
Lead Assessor: Individual who oversees and manages the Assessment Team during a CMMC
Assessment for a particular OSC.
C3PAO
CQAP
Certified CMMC Professionals (CCP): Individuals whose purpose, requirements, benefits, and
timeline for certification and assessments are outlined by The Cyber AB.


Make final decisions if there is a dispute between
the OSC and the Assessment Team regarding
findings.

--- PAGE 461 ---
The CCP's Role in the Assessment Process
Working on a Level 2 Assessment, CCPs contribute to each phase of the Assessment Process.
•
In Phase 1: Plan and Prepare Assessments, a CCP must:
•
Assist in analyzing requirements.
•
Assist in developing an Assessment Plan.
•
Assist in verifying everyone’s readiness to conduct an Assessment.
•
In Phase 2: Conduct Assessment, a CCP must:
•
Assist in collecting and examining Evidence for Level 1 practices.
•
Assist in scoring Level 1 practices and validating the preliminary results.
•
Assist in generating the final Assessment results.
•
In Phase 3: Report Recommended Assessment Results, a CCP must:
•
Assist in delivering the recommended Assessment results.
•
In Phase 4: Remediate Outstanding Assessment Issues, a CCP must:
•
Assist in the validation and close-out of the OSC’s POA&M.
Figure 10-8: A CCP must be ready to assist the Lead Assessor at every phase, including assessing
Level 1 practices in a Level 2 Assessment.

CMMC eMASS
•
eMASS:
•
Provides situational awareness for Information Assurance activities throughout the DoD.
•
Serves as a centralized tool for Assessment and Authorization of DoD Information Systems.
•
CMMC eMASS is a fully customized and tailored version of the tool, providing for:
•
Assessment Records Management.
•
Data Ingest (JSON files or supported spreadsheets).
•
Assessment Workflow Management.
•
Reporting and Metrics.
•

•

Assessment process.
•
No sensitive or proprietary artifacts or data will be ingested or stored.
•
Access requires a favorable suitability determination for account activation and issuance of DoD hard token.
•
System compatible templates will be available from The Cyber AB through the CMMC eMASS application.
Enterprise Mission Assurance Support Service (CMMC eMASS): A web-based U.S. Department of
Defense off-the-shelf solution that automates a broad range of services for cybersecurity
management. CMMC eMASS serves as the system of record for CMMC Assessment data and
reporting.

Tenets for CCP and Assessment Team Members
•
Professionalism:
•
Be professional, courteous and meaningful in your actions.
•
Always remember, your role is that of a CMMC representative.
•
Overall success is contingent, in part, upon your actions.
•
Communication:
•
Ensure communications are clear and purposeful.
•
Requests for information and/or data should be supportive to Assessment needs.
•
Effective communication is the hallmark of high-performance teams.
•
Diligence:
•
Evaluate data and interview responses in their appropriate context.
•
Draw upon the expertise of the Lead Assessor, where necessary, for clarity and guidance.
•
Take good notes to preserve context and observations.
•
Reliability:
•
Comply with OSC visitation policies, internal team guidelines, and Assessment schedules.
•
Integrity:
•
Observe Assessment confidentiality.
•
Work to ensure fidelity in the Assessment process and supporting processes.
•
Employ proper use of methods as described by the Assessment Guides and the CAP.
•
Act in a manner consistent with the intent of the materials to preserve the integrity of the CMMC mission.

should be followed by a CCP and Assessment Team Members.

•

ACTIVITY 10-1
Identifying Assessment Roles and
Responsibilities
Scenario
As a CCP, you will work collaboratively with the rest of the Assessment Team under the leadership
of the Lead Assessor. In this activity, use the following scenario to answer the question about the
identifying roles in the Assessment.
1.
You are working as a CCP supporting the Lead Assessor in the selection of
team members for your organization's upcoming CMMC Level 2 Assessment.
The scope of this Assessment includes validation of the security controls
within the organization's Cloud Services Environment and, specifically, their
corporate infrastructure. The Lead Assessor is not familiar with the members
of the organization and asks your insight to help with the selection of
Assessment Team Members. One of the candidates, Tom Smith, is the son of
the business owner and has just graduated from college with a Bachelor of
Fine Arts.
Would you recommend Tom Smith as an Assessment Team Member? Yes or
no, and why?
A: No. The proposed candidate lacks the prerequisite experience and his relationship as the son of
the business owner may influence his decision making and diminish his objectivity.
2.
Who is responsible for ensuring the accuracy and completeness of the
Assessment Results Package?
○
Lead Assessor
○
CCP
○
CQAP
○
C3PAO
3.
Who oversees and manages the individuals involved and the phases of a
CMMC Assessment for a particular OSC?
○
CCP
○
Lead Assessor
○
CQAP
○
C3PAO

Request for CMMC Assessment (RFA)
•
OSCs can select any C3PAO in good standing and submit a request for a CMMC Assessment.
•
C3PAOs in good standing are found through the CMMC Marketplace.
•
The DoD and The Cyber AB will not serve in an introductory or facilitation role.
•
Requests can include a preference for a specific Lead Assessor.
•
C3PAO has final decision on Assessment Team selection.
•
C3PAOs have 5 business days to:
•
Respond in writing to the RFA.
•
Assign a Lead Assessor.
•
Factors for Lead Assessor selection:
•
Certified CMMC Level 2.
•
Assessment experience.
•
Knowledge of OSC context.
•
Type of DoD contract.
•
Industry.
•
Geographical location in relation to the OSC's location.

Assessment Framing
•
Contract discussions between the OSC and the C3PAO at the beginning of their engagement.
•
Recommended that C3PAOs sign an NDA as part of this initial contractual arrangement.
•
Elements to be discussed during framing include:
•
Corporate entity to be assessed.
•
OSC Assessment Official who has decision-making authority for the Assessment.
•
OSC’s target CMMC Level.
•
OSC’s Assessment Scope.
•
Location(s) and dates for conducting the Assessment.
•
Aspects of the Assessment that will be conducted virtually, if any.
•
Rough Order of Magnitude (ROM) to estimate duration and timing for the Assessment.
•
OSC organizational details, including roles and responsibilities of IT and information security staff.
•
Staff that will provide Evidence and context for the Assessment.
•
The OSC’s Self-Assessment Practice Deficiency tracker items that might result in a POA&M in Phase 2.
•
Assessment outputs to be provided to the OSC.
Assessment framing: The practice of identifying the size, scale, date, time, place, manner,
resources, and level of effort associated with the prospective conduct of a CMMC Assessment.
This high-level contract framing is performed jointly by the C3PAO and the OSC and is conducted at
the beginning of their engagement.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process, version 5.4
Figure 10-16: Assessment framing involves scoping out the logistics involved for a C3PAO to
conduct an Assessment for an OSC.

Non-Duplication Considerations
•
Lead Assessors may be asked to consider practices assessed under other industry standards.
•
ISO/IEC 27001
•
FedRAMP
•
DIBCAC High Assessments (DFARS 252.204-7012)
•
Until DoD releases guidance on non-duplication, other cybersecurity conformance regimes do not
bestow any status or credit towards an OSC’s CMMC certification.
•
Guidance will be included in the CAP once the DoD finalizes it.
•
Intent is that practices MET via non-duplication agreements:
•
May be subject to a reduction in Evidence review.
•
Will be described in the tailoring section of the Assessment Plan.
Non-Duplication: A planning step that allows consideration of commonalities between cyber
security certifications and/or relevant industry standards when planning a CMMC Assessment to
minimize duplication of effort.
Figure 10-19: Non-duplication is the act of reviewing other relevant cybersecurity certifications
in an attempt to reduce repetition of cybersecurity assessments.

ACTIVITY 10-2
Planning for an Assessment
Scenario
As a CCP on an Assessment Team, you need to be familiar with the logistics of an Assessment
beginning with the Planning phase. In this activity, you will consider some scenarios and determine
whether they follow the recommended Assessment Processes.
1.
What can an Assessment Team do to foster a positive Assessment culture?
A: To foster a positive Assessment culture, an Assessment Team should research the company
beforehand so that they can respect its unique context. They should be respectful, professional,
and kind at all times, remembering that they are guests of the OSC and that they have a
partnership with the OSC with the common cause of protecting the country's military supply chain.
They should be patient, asking questions in a different way if their first requests for information
aren't understood.
2.
An OSC contacts a C3PAO for a CMMC Assessment. The C3PAO assigns a
CCP who contacts the OSC and schedules a site visit for the Assessment.
Does this scenario include the proper initial steps in the Assessment
Process?
A: It is correct for the OSC to contact a C3PAO asking for an Assessment. However, all Assessments
must be led by a Lead Assessor who is a CCA, not a CCP. Another problem in the scenario is that
there should be a lot of planning before a site visit.
3.
An OSC contacts a C3PAO for a CMMC Assessment. They indicate they are
looking for a Level 2 Certification and provide a network diagram and other
details to document the scope of the Assessment. The C3PAO assigns a
Lead Assessor, who starts planning. First, the Lead Assessor confirms the
Assessment boundaries. Next, the Lead Assessor conducts a CA-RR. After
confirming that there is sufficient Evidence to proceed, the Lead Assessor
develops an Assessment Plan and schedules an on-site visit to the OSC.
Does this scenario include the proper initial steps in the Assessment
Process?
A: Yes. It is crucial to confirm that the OSC is truly ready for an Assessment, by validating the scope
boundary and doing a Certification Assessment Readiness Review (CA-RR).
468 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic B
Activity

--- PAGE 481 ---
4.
A C3PAO is conducting a CA-RR as part of the prep work for an Assessment
requested by an OSC. During the readiness review, a CCP points out that
there is no Evidence for SI.L1-3.14.4 and suggests that the OSC collect scan
results from their anti-virus and anti-malware software to fill the gap.
Is this an acceptable action during a Certification Assessment Readiness
Review?
A: No, it is not acceptable. During a CA-RR that is conducted as part of the preparation for an
Assessment, a CCP or CCA can point out that there is missing Evidence, but they can't make a
specific suggestion on how to fix the issue.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 469
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic B

--- PAGE 482 ---

In this phase of the Assessment, the CCP's role is determined by the Lead Assessor. As a member

in this phase, even though the roles' tasks might not be their responsibility.

--- PAGE 484 ---
On-Site Work for CCPs
•
Participate in the Assessment Kickoff known as the Opening Brief.
•
Follow the guidance and direction of the Lead Assessor.
•
Perform examination of OSC artifacts.
•
For each assigned CMMC Practice:
•
Participate in interview sessions.
•
Observe and analyze results of tests.
•
Examine and verify Evidence, noting gaps where identified.
•
Participate in team practice characterization and Final Findings.
•
Use good communication skills when interviewing or observing tests and demonstrations.
•
Ensure affirmations are provided by people who implement, perform, or support the procedures for the
practice being assessed.
•
Provide feedback.

Opening Briefing
•
Vital communication mechanism for the OSC, Lead Assessor, and the Assessment Team.
•
Participants include:
•
OSC Assessment Official and OSC PoC
•
All members of the OSC who will be providing Evidence and being interviewed.
•
Lead Assessor and Assessment Team Members
•
OSC provides high-level overview of their:
•
Company.
•
Cybersecurity program.
•
Lead Assessor provides information regarding:
•
Scope, purpose, and goals of the Assessment.
•
Overview of the Assessment process.
•
Assessment logistics and schedule.
•
Required participants and meeting location.
•
Opportunity for the OSC to ask questions of the Assessment Team.
•
Should be no surprises from work previously completed off site.

Daily Checkpoints
•
Required component of the Assessment process, helping to ensure:
•
Assessment is proceeding as planned.
•
Data collection needs are being met.
•
Issues impacting completion of the Assessment are identified, mitigated, and resolved.
•
Conducted at the end of each Assessment day and includes:
•
Lead Assessor.
•
Assessment Team Members.
•
OSC Assessment Participants (including, OSC Assessment Official, PoC, and other key personnel).
•
Helps to ensure that context is maintained during the review of Evidence.
•
A Daily Checkpoint Log will be used to record meeting decisions and disposition.

Guidelines for Working through an Assessment
•
Seek first to understand the nature of the OSC and the context of its processes.
•
What line of business is it in?
•
What does its ecosystem look like?
•
Adapt to the organization.
•
Be open minded, but consistent in your validation approach.
•
Ensure your actions as a CCP support effective Assessment characteristics.
•
Objective, fair, consistent, reliable.
•
Verify resource availability (Assessment participants, rooms, equipment, etc.).
•
Engage the right resources.
•
Conduct interviews with those responsible for performance of the process or development of the plan.
•
Verify the sufficiency of the collected Evidence.
•
Must address the full scope of the Assessment.
•
Must cover the Assessment scope (target level).
•
Must correspond to the Evidence collection approach.
•
Lead Assessor ensures that no proprietary data leaves the OSC’s environment.
•
Without the express written consent of the OSC Assessment Official.
•
At all times, comply with the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) and the CoPC.

476 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic C
Evidence Collection
Worksheet

--- PAGE 489 ---
Objective-Level Scoring of Practices
•
CCPs and CCAs will score practices:
•
Iteratively based on daily reviews.
•
At the Assessment Objective level based on Evidence received.
•
Evidence can be from any of the three Evidence types: Examine, Interview, and/or Test.
•
Evidence must meet the standards prescribed for “adequacy and sufficiency.”
•
As MET, NOT MET, or NA.
•
When validating preliminary results, for a practice to be scored as MET:
•
The CCP/CCA must examine and accept the Evidence.
•
The Evidence examined must be applicable to the practice.
•
The Evidence must fully reflect the performance of the practice Assessment Objective
defined in the CMMC Level 2 Assessment Guide.
•
CCPs and CCAs will document Evidence gaps.
•
Difference between what the Evidence shows, and what the Assessment Team requires.
•
For example:
•
Incomplete documents.
•
Incomplete tests or interview affirmations
•
Not from the person responsible for the practice.
•
Policies lacking endorsement from senior management.
•
No signature or digit endorsement via email or chat.
MET
NOT MET
NA
Figure 10-33: CCPs and CCAs score practices at the Assessment Objective level and Evidence
gaps must be recorded.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 477
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic C
Objective-Level Scoring
of Practices

--- PAGE 490 ---
The DoD Assessment Scoring Methodology
•
Provides an objective, quantitative assessment of the OSC’s CMMC L2 practice implementation.
•
Incorporates weighted values based on criticality and/or relative importance of the practice.
•
Awards 1 point for every practice fully implemented.
•
An OSC is awarded a score of 110 when all security requirements are fully implemented.
•
Subtracts points for practices not fully implemented.
•
Due to the different weights for each practice, an OSC could end up with a negative score.
•
Points will NOT be deducted for practices determined to be NA.
•
For example, companies may not permit remote access (AC.L2-3.1.12, AC.L2-3.1.16, and AC.L2-3.1.18) as
reflected in the OSC’s policies and procedures.
•
If the OSC disallows remote access, but this is NOT reflected in policies/procedures, 1 point will be deducted.
•
Implemented security measures adjudicated by the DoD CIO as equally effective, and security
requirements approved by the DoD CIO as ‘not applicable,’ in accordance with DFARS 252.204 -7008
or 7012 will be assessed as ‘implemented.’
Figure 10-34: CCPs and CCAs will use the DoD Assessment Scoring Methodology, which weights
practices based on their relative impact on the security of the network and its data.

Practices Weighted as 3 Points
A weight of 3 points is assigned for Basic and ‘Derived Security Requirements’ that, if left
unimplemented, would have a specific and confined effect on the security of the network and its data.
•
Basic Security Requirements with a value of 3 points include:
•
AU.L2-3.3.2, MA.L2-3.7.1, MP.L2-3.8.1, MP.L2-3.8.2, PS.L2-3.9.1, RA.L2-3.11.1, and CA.L2-3.12.2.
•
Derived Security Requirements with a value of 3 points include:
•
AC.L2-3.1.5, AC.L2-3.1.19, MA.L2-3.7.4, MP.L2-3.8.8, SC.L2-3.13.8, SI.L1-3.14.5, and SI.L1-3.14.7
*NOTE: There is one practice that can receive partial credit - SC.L2-3.13.11. It is
weighted 3 points if encryption is employed, but it is not FIPS-validated.

Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic C
Practices Weighted as 1

--- PAGE 494 ---
Limited Practice Deficiency Correction Program
What it is
•
A “Limited Practice Deficiency” includes practices that may be implemented, but not documented correctly.
•
Provides a mechanism by which OSC’s may remediate minor issues within a restricted timeframe.
Restrictions
•
Practices that could lead to significant exploitation of the network or exfiltration of CUI are excluded.
•
These practices are defined within the CAP.
•
Any practice(s) listed on the OSC’s Self-Assessment Practice Deficiency Tracker are excluded.
•
Practices that were not implemented prior to the CMMC Assessment are excluded.
•
Any practice that changes and/or limits the effectiveness of another practice scored “MET” are excluded.
Figure 10-38: The Limited Practice Deficiency Correction Program provides a mechanism that
enables an OSC to remediate minor issues within a restricted time frame.

Authorized Limited

--- PAGE 496 ---
Additional Considerations of Limited Practice Deficiencies
If on the allowed list, there are additional criteria for inclusion in the correction program:
•
The practice has been implemented for a period of time, but the Evidence shows:
•
It is missing minor updates.
•
For example:
•
Missing updates to policy signatures.
•
Procedural documentation inaccurate due to recent technology updates.
•
The practice does not change and/or limits the effectiveness of another practice scored “MET”.
•
Decision made in discussion with the:
•
OSC PoC.
•
OSC personnel that gave affirmations in Interviews and provided demonstrations in Tests.
•
Lead Assessor.
•
Assessment Team Member(s).

The Preliminary Recommended Findings
•
Generated and presented by the Lead Assessor.
•
Based on how the CCPs and CCAs of the Assessment Team rated the practices.
•
Occurs during Daily Checkpoints with Assessment participants.
•
Can be presented at a separate event to the participating OSC staff.
•
Build a shared understanding of the results of the Assessment.
•
Provides traceability of findings to the practices and identified noncompliance/nonconformance.
•
Enables the OSC to provide additional context or information where it is relevant and meaningful.
•
Validate preliminary findings with the OSC before the Final Recommended Findings are reported.
•
Review recommended CMMC practices MET/NOT MET scores.

Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic C
The Preliminary
Recommended Findings

--- PAGE 498 ---
OSC POA&M
•
Prioritizes efforts to correct security weaknesses.
•
Documents all proposed actions for remediation and the timeline for implementing changes.
•
Monitors the progress of remediation.
•
Should be updated regularly.
•
Qualifying OSC POA&M items:
•
Listed on the OSC Self-Assessment Practice Deficiency Tracker used in Assessment Framing.
Plan of Action and Milestones (POA&M): A document that identifies tasks needing to be
accomplished. It details resources required to accomplish the elements of the plan, any milestones
in meeting the tasks, and scheduled completion dates for the milestones.
CA.L2-3.12.2 Plan of Action
Develop and implement plans of action designed to correct deficiencies and reduce or eliminate vulnerabilities
in organizational systems.
[a] deficiencies and vulnerabilities to be addressed by the plan of action are identified;
[b] a plan of action is developed to correct identified deficiencies and reduce or eliminate identified
vulnerabilities; and
[c] the plan of action is implemented to correct identified deficiencies and reduce or eliminate identified
vulnerabilities.
Source: NIST Computer Security Resource Center
Figure 10-42: CA.L2-3.12.2 is the CMMC practice associated with OSC POA&Ms. This is a Level 2
practice; therefore, a CCP will not evaluate the Evidence for this practice.
Notes
486 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic C
OSC POA&M

--- PAGE 499 ---
POA&M Review and Validation
The Lead Assessor will:
•
Ask the Assessment Team to clarify their practice scores, if necessary.
•
CCPs provide clarification on Level 1 practices they’ve assessed.
•
CCAs provide clarification on any Level 1 or 2 practices they’ve assessed.
•
Review eligibility for a POA&M to be authorized as part of the Assessment:
•
DoD has established a minimum score requirement.
•
80% of the Levels 1 and 2 CMMC practices must be MET (88/110 practices).
•
Must meet the criteria of Appendix K.
•
The highest-weighted CMMC practices are not allowed on a POA&M.
•
Validate the OSC POA&M has the following:
•
The specific security weakness revealed in the Assessment and tied to a specific practice.
•
The severity of each weakness.
•
The scope of each weakness with the assessed environment.
•
The proposed mitigation approaches.
•
The estimated costs for remediation.
•
Documented records of mitigation status and delays.
•
A risk assessment of the deficiency.
•
Document the valid POA&M practices on the CMMC Assessment Results Template.
POA&M
Figure 10-43: The Lead Assessor reviews and validates whether the OSC's POA&M is authorized
by the DoD under CMMC Model 2.0.

POA&M Review and

--- PAGE 500 ---
The Final Recommended Findings
•
Lead Assessor generates and presents the Final Findings Briefing.
•
Based on the Assessment Findings Brief Template.
•
Occurs during the final Daily Checkpoint with:
•
Assessment participants.
•
OSC Assessment Official
•
Based on all the Evidence reviewed by the CCPs and CCAs on the Assessment Team.
•
Includes Assessment scores of practices from all CCPs and CCAs.
•
Use Assessment Results Template to detail the practice scores.
•
Aggregate scores from all CCPs and CCAs and review during the Daily Checkpoint.
•
Include characterization of findings for the OSC’s aggregated level and each in˗scope practice.
•
Describe noncompliance/nonconformance with sufficient detail to demonstrate how the finding was derived.
•
Check Authorized Deficiency Corrections list for allowable practices.
•
Review recommended CMMC Certification Level MET/NOT MET decision.
•
Review next steps for OSC after Final Findings Briefing:
•
Accept Final Findings.
•
Complete any Limited Practice Deficiency Correction items within 5 days.
•
Complete any POA&M items within 180 days.

--- PAGE 502 ---
ACTIVITY 10-3
Participating on an Assessment Team
Scenario
As a CCP on an Assessment Team, you will follow the instructions of the Lead Assessor. In this
activity, you will consider some scenarios and determine whether they follow the recommended
Assessment Processes.
1.
During the on-site portion of an Assessment, the Lead Assessor starts with an
Opening Briefing on the first day and concludes on the last day with a Final
Recommended Findings Briefing.
Does this scenario include the proper steps for an on-site visit?
A: No. In addition to these two sessions, it is recommended that the Lead Assessor conducts daily
checkpoints to keep the Assessment on track.
2.
During the course of the Assessment, you discover a critical flaw in the as-
implemented security controls, whereby team members who left the
organization were permitted to maintain their account credentials to support
"transition" activities. When seeking clarification, the owner of the company
states, "This is not something we need to worry about." You presume, as he
is the company owner, that his statement is correct.
Based on this scenario, what are your actions?
A: As an Assessment Team Member, you must ensure that your actions support effective
Assessment characteristics (objective, fair, consistent, reliable), that your review of the Evidence
is consistent with the CAP, and that you adhere to the CoPC at all times. In this scenario, the
information should be shared with the Assessment Team to support effective practice
characterization.
490 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)

Phase 3 Tasks and Outputs
In this phase of the Assessment, the CCP's role is determined by the Lead Assessor. As a member
of the Assessment Team, CCPs need to be aware of and understand the responsibilities of each role
in this phase, even though the roles' tasks might not be their responsibility.
Required Outputs
•
Final Recommended Findings
•
Assessment Results Package
•
Records of Evidence reviewed and examined
•
Practice Scores and Justification
•
OSC Artifacts Hash
•
Limited Practice Deficiency Correction
Evaluation
Tasks
•
Lead Assessor delivers recommended
Assessment Results Package to OSC
Assessment Official.
•
Lead Assessor submits Assessment Results
Package to C3PAO.
•
CQAP verifies the completeness and accuracy
of Assessment Results Package.
•
C3PAO uploads Assessment Results Package
into CMMC eMASS.
•
CCPs/CCAs archive or dispose of Assessment
artifacts.
•
Lead Assessor verifies that artifacts are
archived or disposed.
•
C3PAO issues CMMC L2 or L2 Limited
Certification.

Phase 3 Tasks and

--- PAGE 505 ---
Limited Practice Deficiency Correction Evaluation
Process
•
The Lead Assessor and CCPs/CCAs review new evidence for items
placed on the Limited Practice Deficiency Correction program.
•
5 days after the Final Recommended Findings Briefing.
Results
•
If all can be marked MET and closed out:
•
OSC will receive a CMMC L2 Final Certification.
•
If any still marked as NOT MET meet POA&M criteria:
•
Move them to the OSC POA&M.
•
OSC will receive a CMMC L2 Interim Certification.
•
Must close-out POA&M items within 180 days.
•
If the POA&M option can’t be utilized, the OSC will be required to
correct deficiencies and reapply.

Practice Deficiency Correction Program 5 days after the Final Recommended Findings Briefing.

Assessment Results Package
•

•

•

•
Final Report Part 2 – Final detailed, practice-level scores that are traceable to each finding/score result (e.g.,

•

•

•
C3PAO uploads the Assessment Results Package to CMMC eMASS.
•
Assessment Team (CCPs/CCAs) archives or destroys Assessment artifacts.

•

for each team member.

CMMC Artifact Hashing Tool User Guide
Purpose
•
Can check the integrity of the artifacts by verifying the hash generated during the Assessment.
•
If the artifact has not been modified, the hash will remain the same.
Process
•
The Assessment Team will store Evidence in a secure location with controlled access.
•
The OSC must hash the Evidence and retain for three (3) years.
•
The C3PAO will report the OSC’s hash into the CMMC eMASS.
Tool
•
The CMMC Artifact Hashing Tool is a PowerShell script that:
•
Generates a hash of each artifact using the SHA
-
256 algorithm.
•
Generates a list of file names and associated hashes.
•
Hashes the list.
•
The User Guide provides the script and directions for its use.
Hashing: The process of using a mathematical algorithm against data to produce a numeric value
that is representative of that data.
Source: NIST SP 800
-72
Published by the DoD
-
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
Figure 10-50: OSCs need to use the CMMC Artifact Hashing Tool to protect the integrity of the
Evidence collected and documented during their Assessment.

ACTIVITY 10-4
Reporting the Assessment Results
Scenario
As a CCP on an Assessment Team, you might be asked by the Lead Assessor to help prepare the
Assessment Results Package. In this activity, you will consider some scenarios and determine
whether they follow the recommended Assessment processes.
1.
After the Assessment is concluded, the Lead Assessor submits a Final
Assessment Plan and Schedule, summary-level findings, detailed practice-
and process-level findings, and the Daily Checkpoint Log to the C3PAO. The
Lead Assessor directs the CCPs on the Assessment Team to archive or
destroy any artifacts collected during the Assessment. The Lead Assessor
files the team notes and the Assessment Results Package with a destruction
date three years from the date of the Assessment.
Does this scenario include the proper final steps in the Assessment Process?
A: Yes. The scenario includes all the necessary components of the Assessment Results Package
and the destruction/archival of the artifacts. It also includes details about the fact that the
Assessment Team notes and Final Assessment Results Package must be maintained by the Lead
Assessor for a period of three years.
2.
Following the Assessment, another CCP asks if he/she may maintain a copy
of the findings as "they may be useful for future Assessments." Is this
permissible?
A: No; The Final Assessment Results Package is an artifact of the Assessment and is proprietary to
the OSC. As such, it can not be removed.

TOPIC E
Conduct the CMMC POA&M Close-Out
Assessment
Some OSCs will pass the CMMC Assessment and receive their desired CMMC Level Certification
after the on-site visit. However, there might be situations when an OSC comes close to passing the
desired CMMC Level Assessment but something prevents them from being recommended for
CMMC Certification. In this case, an OSC might be able to qualify for a grace period to address
these final issues. In this topic, you will define the POA&M Close-Out Assessment phase.

In this phase of the Assessment, the CCP's role is determined by the Lead Assessor. As a member
of the Assessment Team, CCPs need to be aware of and understand the responsibilities of each role
in this phase, even though the roles' tasks might not be their responsibility.

The wrong approach was used, or

Phase 4 Tasks and

--- PAGE 513 ---
POA&M Close-Out
Purpose
•
Allows OSCs that received a Limited CMMC L2 Certification during Phase 3 to close out all
practices validated on Plans of Action and Milestones (POA&M) during a secondary C3PAO
Assessment within 180 days of the Final Recommended Findings.
Responsibilities
•
OSC will:
•
Schedule the Close-Out Assessment.
•
Contract with either:
•
The C3PAO that conducted the original Assessment, or
•
A new C3PAO.
•
Lead Assessor will:
•
Review the updated POA&M.
•
Schedule Evidence collection via observations, interviews, or tests.
Figure 10-55: After the Final Findings, the OSC has 180 days to fix all the items on their POA&M
and contract with a C3PAO to do a Close-Out Assessment.
Notes
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 501
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic E
POA&M Close-Out

--- PAGE 514 ---
POA&M Close-Out Assessment and Results
POA&M Close-Out Criteria
•
The specific security weakness revealed by POA&M has been “Fully-Implemented” and scored “MET”
•
All POA&M items “Fully-Implemented” do not change and/or limit the effectiveness of another practice
scored “MET” during the CMMC L2 Assessment for which the CMMC L2 Interim Certification was issued.
•
An updated Risk Assessment shows the removal of the previous CMMC Practices listed on the POA&M.
•
An updated POA&M shows no CMMC practice deficiencies.
Results
In the event the OSC corrects all POA&M deficiencies:
•
The Lead Assessor will recommend CMMC L2 Final Certification.
In the event an OSC fails to correct the POA&M deficiencies:
•
The Lead Assessor will not recommend CMMC L2 Final Certification.
•
The OSC will still be required to correct deficiencies and reapply for CMMC L2 Certification.
•
The CMMC L2 Limited Certification will become null and void.
Figure 10-56: The Lead Assessor will collect and review new Evidence to assess the practices
listed on the POA&M.
Notes

ACTIVITY 10-5
Defining the POA&M Close-Out Process
Scenario
In very limited circumstances, CMMC will allow use of a Plan of Action and Milestones (POA&M)
to remediate practices determined to be not fully or successfully implemented, provided that the
eligibility POA&M requirements are met. As a CCP, you may be asked to provide input to this
process, either through preliminary characterization of Evidence or to provide support to the Lead
Assessor through validation of the POA&M.
In this scenario, you are supporting the Lead Assessor as an Assessment Team Member. During the
course of the Assessment, it was determined that the OSC has not yet fully implemented the
standardized DoD Warning Banners, including a "consent to monitoring" for connected
information systems and devices.
1.
Based on this scenario, which CMMC practice would apply?
A: AC.L2-3.1.9 Provide privacy and security notices consistent with applicable CUI rules.
2.
Would the practice be Met or Not Met?
A: Not Met.
3.
Provided that this is the only practice determined to be Not Met, could a
POA&M be employed? If so, what steps would be taken to validate:
A: Yes. Validation would be conducted by the Lead Assessor with input from the Assessment Team,
CCP, and/or OSC and should minimally include: a) The specific security weakness revealed in the
Assessment and tied to a specific practice, b) The severity of the weakness, c) The scope of each
weakness with the assessed environment. d) The proposed mitigation approaches. e) The
estimated costs for remediation. f) Documented records of mitigation status and delays, and g) A
risk assessment of the deficiency.
4.
As a CCP, what additional support might you provide?
A: As a CCP, you may be asked to support the review of new evidence and the Close-Out
Assessment of authorized POA&M items.
504 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Lesson 10: Working through an Assessment | Topic E
Activity

Summary
In this lesson, you learned how an Assessment Team will conduct a CMMC Assessment, from the
first planning stages through the on-site work and culminating in the final report submission. You
also learned about POA&Ms and how the POA&M Close-Out Assessment phase provides an
opportunity for OSCs to resolve minor issues in order to receive CMMC L2 Certification.
How do you think an Assessment Team can foster a positive culture instead of a
fearful audit mentality for the OSC?
A: Answers will vary.
As an Assessment Team Member, which stage of the Assessment process do
you expect to be the most rewarding or the most challenging?
A: Answers will vary.

other topics you are interested in. From the Course screen you can also access available
resources for a more continuous learning experience.

--- PAGE 519 ---
Course Follow-Up
Congratulations! You have completed the Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) course. You have gained
the information you need to participate on a CMMC Assessment Team and to provide certified
consulting services to organizations seeking CMMC certification.
You also covered the objectives that you will need to prepare for The Cyber AB Certified CMMC
Professional (CCP) exam. If you combine this class experience with review, private study, and hands-
on experience, you will be well prepared to demonstrate your advanced CMMC expertise both
through professional certification and solid technical competence on the job.
What's Next?
Your next step after completing this course will probably be to prepare for and obtain your CCP
certification. In addition to the CCP exam, all CCP candidates must pass the DoD Mandatory CUI
Training found at https://securityawareness.usalearning.gov/cui/index.html. You must pass
this training no earlier than three months prior to taking the exam.
If you are interested in continuing onwards to becoming a Certified CMMC Assessor, Certified
CMMC Assessor (CCA) is the next course in this series.
You are encouraged to explore CMMC further using the resources provided by The Cyber AB and
the OUSD (A&S).

Additional
Documentation for CCPs
This course provides an overview of CMMC, but to become knowledgeable professionals,
CCPs should study the CMMC documentation and the underlying regulations and
standards. In addition to this appendix, check https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/
Documentation/ and https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Resources/ for additional
resources and updates.
CMMC Documentation
CCPs should be very familiar with the official CMMC documentation.
Title
Description / URL
CMMC Model
Overview
Describes the overall framework for CMMC, which is a maturity
model with three levels, each of which have practices to protect
sensitive information.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
CMMC 2.0
Spreadsheet and
Mapping
Provides a matrix view of the model, a description of how the
CMMC Model maps to other sources and references for the model,
and also provides the previous CMMC 1.x practice numbering.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
CMMC Artifact
Hashing Tool User
Guide
Provides guidance on creating a cryptographic reference (or hash)
for assessment artifacts to ensure artifact integrity.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
CMMC Level 1 Self-
Assessment Guide
Provides the Assessment criteria and methodology for all CMMC
Level 1 practices.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
CMMC Level 2
Assessment Guide
Provides the Assessment criteria and methodology for all practices
at CMMC Levels 1 and 2.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms
Provides acronyms and definitions for CMMC terms.
https://dodcio.defense.gov/CMMC/Documentation/
B

--- PAGE 526 ---
Title
Description / URL
CMMC Assessment
Process (CAP)
Provides the process and guidance for performing a CMMC
Assessment. The final publication is currently in development by
the DoD and The Cyber AB. A draft version is available from The
Cyber AB.
https://cyberab.org/CMMC-Ecosystem/Member-Area-
Downloads-and-Forums
Standards and Regulations
CCPs should also familiarize themselves with the regulations and standards that provide support for
the CMMC Model. Follow these links to open the documents and become familiar with the
important document parts, and understand the documents' importance to CMMC Assessments.

Description / URL

Governs handling of CUI.

part-2002

Obtaining Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) certification requires candidates to pass the

and unzip the course files. The mapping reference will be in a subfolder named Mappings.

ACTIVITY 1-1: Identifying Threats to the Defense Supply
Chain
1.
Why is the Defense Supply Chain at risk?
A: Because the DIB is a complex system with Prime contractors and subcontractors dealing
with sensitive information. The more complex the system is, the more opportunities there are
for an adversary to hack into it to steal sensitive information.
2.
How does CMMC encourage a different way of thinking about
cybersecurity and protecting sensitive information within the Defense
Supply Chain?
A: CMMC shifts the focus to protecting the information and not just the IT systems. Additionally,
CMMC uses third-party verification to formalize evaluation of how thoroughly the contractors
supplying the DoD make cybersecurity an essential part of their organizational culture.
ACTIVITY 1-2: Identifying Regulatory Responses against
Threats
1.
What experience have you had with government contracts and the
regulations that govern them?
A: Answers will vary. For those with little to no experience of working with a government
contract, then they will need to become familiar with the legal, regulatory, and policy drivers
(i.e., FISMA, FAR 52, and 32 CFR Part 2002 that govern sensitive information). For those with
government contracting experience, the DFARS Clause 252.204-7021 that requires CMMC
Certification might be new to them.
2.
Which of the following is most clearly associated with a Department of
Defense contract?
○
NIST Cybersecurity Framework (CSF)
○
FAR
◉
DFARS
○
NIST SP 800-171

--- PAGE 532 ---

Where would you look for a primary regulation covering the basic
safeguarding of Federal Contract Information?
◉
48 CFR 52.204-21 (also known as FAR 52)
○
NARA
○

○
NIST SP 800-53

○

○
DFARS Clause 252.204-7020
◉
DFARS Clause 252.204-7021

and focuses on protecting the confidentiality of Controlled

○
NIST SP 800-171A

NIST SP 800-171
○
NIST SP 800-53
○
NIST SP 800-172
ACTIVITY 2-1: Identifying What Is and What Is Not
Sensitive Information
1.
Is a RFP sensitive information?
A: It depends. Most RFPs are entirely public, so they are not FCI. However, some
aspects of an RFP may only be available to companies with credentials for certain
systems. In such cases, those portions of the RFP are likely to be at least FCI and may
even rise to the level of CUI.
2.
When an RFP is released, in many cases a Prime contractor will
be able to perform most of the requirements themselves. However,
they frequently need assistance from one or more subcontractors
to meet the RFP's requirements. So, the Prime contractor and
subcontractor(s) enter into teaming agreements that describe their
respective roles and responsibilities should they win a contract. Is a
teaming agreement sensitive information?
A: Typically, no. Teaming agreements are not created for the government, so although
they are (typically) nonpublic documents, they are not FCI. However, as discussed in the
previous example, some RFPs include FCI or CUI. If the teaming agreement includes
portions of the RFP with FCI or CUI, then the teaming agreement "inherits" the FCI or CUI
designation.
520 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 533 ---
3.
Are government contracts sensitive information?
A: Many contract terms are publicly available. These include the contractor's name and address, the
amount of the contract, the contracting and funding agencies, the types of goods or services purchased,
and more. But specific contract terms are typically not public information. So, should the contract be
treated as FCI? Probably. Is the contract still FCI if it is made public because of a Freedom of
Information Act (FOIA) request? It depends on the information that is disclosed by the FOIA request, but
there is a good chance that the contract terms will now be public information. Does that mean we must
stop treating the contract information as FCI? No. There is no requirement that you stop protecting
information; the only difference is that you may not need to protect it. As a general rule, it is better to
assume that contract terms are FCI until you are confident they aren’t.
4.
When a company creates product documentation when under contract with
the DoD, are those materials sensitive information?
A: Yes, a manual for a product created for the government is CUI. In fact, the manual is likely an
example of CTI, a specified defense subcategory of CUI.
5.
Are commercial product specification sheets produced by a defense
contractor sensitive information?
A: Product specifications for a government contractor's products are not likely to be information created
under a contract for the government. Therefore, they are not typically going to be FCI.
6.
A contractor documents design specifications and submits them to the
government. Are design specifications created under contract sensitive
information?
A: Design specifications and other information created under a contract with the government is likely to
be CUI, unless the contract explicitly states that the information is public or to be made public. For
example, if the government hires your customer to create a new software application that will be
released as open-source software, that might cause the software's design specifications and source
code to not need to be treated as CUI. Short of outlier situations such as this, though, the software, its
documentation and design information, and its source code should be treated as CUI.
7.
Under contract with the DoD, a contractor creates various drafts of marketing
materials. Are drafts of marketing materials sensitive information?
A: Marketing materials are public. But drafts of the materials are not typically public. Therefore, the
drafts should be treated as FCI.
8.
The defense contractor receives emails from the government communicating
information about the contract. Are emails communicating contract
information sensitive information?
A: If the defense contractor receives emails from specific individuals in the government (as opposed to
public announcements like the CISA weekly new cybersecurity vulnerability alerts), those emails are
likely to contain nonpublic information and should be treated as FCI or even CUI, depending on whether
the information conveyed in the email is subject to safeguarding or dissemination controls.
9.
The defense contractor creates encryption technologies as part of a drone
operating system. Are the software and encryption source code sensitive
information?
A: The drone operating system is CUI and probably CTI. In addition, the encryption technology
embedded in the operating system might also be ECI.
10.
Is a road map for a military base sensitive information?
A: No, the road map is public information. However, civil engineering maps for a military base would be
considered CUI because it details the infrastructure, which is not public information.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 521
Solutions

--- PAGE 534 ---
ACTIVITY 2-2: Managing Sensitive Information
1.
You are providing consulting services for a small business that is
planning to obtain CMMC Certification. The OSC has previously
only worked in the private sector, but wants to start bidding on
government contracts. How do you even start to identify the
sensitive information when they don't have any yet?
A: They might identify RFPs that represent the kind of contracts they will seek. That will
help them consider requirements they must be able to meet and to plan the systems they
would use in doing so. If they are looking to subcontract, they might talk to the Prime
contractor. They might also confer with the contracting officer with the government. They
might also confer with a PTAC (Procurement Technical Assistance Center) for guidance.
There are many of these around the country, and they can provide free advice to
organizations looking to do business with the government.
2.
While you are doing some consulting work for Consolidated Flange
Company, in the Engineering office, you notice that one of the
packages awaiting pickup by the U.S. Postal Service is marked
with a CUI cover sheet. You inquire about this and are told that the
package contains some technical drawings that are being sent to
one of the company's subcontractors. Are there any potential
problems with this scenario?
A: If the package is awaiting pickup by the U.S. Postal Service, then presumably what
you have seen is the actual package that will be used to ship the drawings. When the
package is moving through the postal system, no CUI markings should be visible from the
outside, as that would provide a clue to a bad actor that the package contains something
they might be interested in intercepting. An inner package with the proper CUI markings
should be completely hidden by outer packaging, so that no CUI marking is visible on the
outer package.
3.
When the OSC asks for guidance on proper management of FCI
and CUI, what are the key things you would advise them to do?
A: Answers will vary, but might include: create a physically-controlled environment with
locked doors and file cabinets; segment the network and restrict access to data using
role-based or rule-based access control; implement encryption technologies in your email
system; and sanitize media before disposal.
ACTIVITY 3-1: Looking Up Model Information in the
CMMC Documentation
4.
Why does the CMMC Model have multiple levels?
A: The levels take into account the type of information being protected and the complexity
of potential threats, from foundational to expert.
5.
What is the definition of "periodically"?
A: The definition is adapted from the Oxford Dictionary. To paraphrase, periodically
simply means regular intervals. Each organization is allowed to define the period that is
best for their business, but for CMMC practices that period can't be longer than a year.
522 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 535 ---
7.
Practice number AC.L2-3.13 specifies that the organization should "Control
the flow of CUI in accordance with approved authorizations." At what CMMC
Level would an organization become responsible to demonstrate they follow
this practice?
A: CMMC Level 2 or higher. While you
could
answer this question by looking it up in the CMMC Model
document's List of Practices, that effort really isn't necessary. The second part of the practice number
contains "L2", which tells you that the practice is introduced into the requirements at Level 2.

Given all the legal, policy, and cybersecurity regulations that are established,
what unique benefits does the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification
offer?
A: Third-party verification of cybersecurity compliance.

5.
Which of the following best describes the authorized roles of a
CCP and a CCA? (Choose two.)
☐

☐

☑

☑
A CCP can be a consultant or serve on the Assessment Team for an OSC; while a
CCA leads the Assessment Team for multiple OSCs.
6.
What are the consequences of non-compliance with CMMC?
A: Organizations must be in compliance with CMMC before they will be awarded a
contract. With the phased implementation, organizations already under contract will need
to seek certification or risk losing their contracts when due for renewal. Other legal
liabilities, including the False Claims Act, might also be a consequence of being out of
compliance with CMMC.
ACTIVITY 3-3: Identifying the Risks of Inaccurate Self-
Assessments
2.
What is the charge against Aerojet Rocketdyne?
A: Brian Markus, the former senior director of Cybersecurity, Compliance, and Controls at
Aerojet Rocketdyne, charged the company with violating the False Claims Act. Markus
claimed that the company fraudulently entered into contracts with the federal government
knowing that they were not in compliance with the regulations in 48 CFR 252.204-7012
and 48 CFR 1852.204-76.
3.
Which of the arguments that Aerojet Rocketdyne attempted to use
in an effort to dismiss the case surprised you?
A: Answers may vary. The claim that cybersecurity regulations were not the core of the
contract was a surprise. And the claim that the government's constant changes to the
regulations meant they didn't really expect full compliance was a shock. Both indicate a
wholesale rejection of the importance of the contractor's responsibility to protect the
sensitive government information that is provided and created under a defense contract.
4.
Explain the court's ruling and the ramifications it has for self-
attestation.
A: The Court has not issued a final ruling. They have rejected the motion to dismiss,
which indicates that organizations that claim self-attestation to cybersecurity regulations
and requirements are legally obligated to be in compliance and can be held accountable
under the False Claims Act.
524 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 537 ---
5.
What are the consequences with the court case still being unsettled six years
after the noncompliance was reported?
A: Unless Aerojet Rocketdyne has changed their business practices, the sensitive data is still potentially
unprotected.
Note: On July 8, 2022, the Department of Justice announced in a press release
that the Aerojet Rocketdyne lawsuit had been settled (https://
www.justice.gov/opa/pr/aerojet-rocketdyne-agrees-pay-9-million-resolve-false-
claims-act-allegations-cybersecurity).
6.
Which of the following statements about self-assessment are accurate?
(Choose two.)
☑
Self-assessment enables organizations to officially declare their compliance with cybersecurity
regulations without third-party verification.
☐
Mandatory procurement clauses and cybersecurity regulations must be explicit in a contract as
governed by the Christian Doctrine.
☐
Before CMMC, the government was unable to prosecute those who were less than honest about
their self-assessment scores.
☑
The False Claims Act provides some protection against those who knowingly defraud the
government; but it is not a sufficient nor an efficient method of protection.
ACTIVITY 4-1: Identifying the Responsibilities of a CCP
1.
When you were preparing to become a CCP, you were working for Better
Industries Inc., helping them identify gaps in their effort to obtain CMMC Level
2 Certification. After obtaining your CCP certification, you left Better Industries
and took a position with the Best Group, a CMMC Third-Party Assessment
Organization (C3PAO). Now, Better Industries is undergoing the Assessment
process, and the Best Group that will be managing Better Industries' CMMC
Level 2 Assessment. Since you are no longer employed by Better Industries,
you reason that it is acceptable for you to serve on the Assessment Team. Is
your reasoning correct?
A: No, it isn't proper for you to participate on the Assessment Team. This would present a conflict of
interest.
2.
Narrow Valley Industries (NVI) obtains more than half of its business through
government contracts. Since compliance with government regulations and
policies is so important to their business, they have decided it would be
helpful to have someone in their organization who is thoroughly familiar with
CMMC. They have offered a position in their company to your colleague,
Dave Farragut, who is a CCP. Will Dave be able to retain his CCP certification
if he accepts the position at NVI?
A: Yes, he may continue to retain his certification and serve as a cybersecurity team member for NVI.
Of course, he would not be able to serve in any way that would cause a conflict of interest. For example,
he would not be able to serve on an Assessment Team evaluating NVI. Furthermore, NVI might not
want Dave acting as a consultant to other companies, but that's a matter between NVI and Dave. Dave
will still have to be mindful of any such requests, though, as any arrangements that appear to be in
violation of the Code of Professional Conduct could put his CCP certification in jeopardy.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 525
Solutions

--- PAGE 538 ---
3.
As a CCP and external consultant, you have been helping the
Capstan Company perform a gap analysis as they prepare for
CMMC Level 2 Certification. Because you are also knowledgeable
in computer networking and cybersecurity, they would like you to
help them redesign their network to create a "secure enclave" in
one wing of their building where all government projects will be
worked on, isolated from people and systems in other parts of the
building. As a CCP functioning as an external consultant, are you
permitted to provide this type of technical consulting?
A: Yes. As a CCP working as a consultant, it is assumed that you may have considerable
influence over technical aspects of your clients' network, as well as on operation and
business decisions.
ACTIVITY 4-2: Looking Up Ethical Guidelines in the
CoPC
4.
If you find yourself in an unavoidable situation that leads to the
appearance of a conflict of interest, how should you proceed?
A: You should always consider how any situation might lead to a real or perceived conflict
of interest. Section 2.2 Objectivity states that, "In the case where a perceived or
management conflict may be present, document and describe the conflict to all affected
parties and secure agreement to continue."
6.
In the past, you have performed services for Develetech
Corporation. They have requested the organization you work for (a
C3PAO) to conduct their CMMC Assessment. You are not clear on
whether the work you have done for them would constitute a
conflict of interest. Identify where you can find any statements in
the Code of Professional Conduct that might help you determine
what would constitute a conflict of interest.
A: Section 3.1 Professionalism, Item 9 states, "Under no circumstances are credentialed
or registered individuals permitted to conduct a certified assessment, or participate on a
certified assessment team, if they have also served as a consultant to prepare the
organization for that assessment. Consulting is defined as 'providing direct assistance to
the creation of processes, training, and technology required to meet the intent of CMMC
controls and processes.'" If the work you performed for Develetech previously had
nothing to do with the processes, training, and technology that would now help them to
meet CMMC requirements, there might not be a conflict of interest. However, even when
you know there isn't any conflict of interest, it is always wise to consider when there might
be an
appearance
of such. If you identify potential for so much as a
perceived
conflict of
interest, you may opt to address it beforehand (see Step 4 of this activity) or simply
choose not to take on the assignment.
526 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 539 ---
8.
You suspect that one of your colleagues might be in violation of the CoPC.
How does the CoPC recommend that you deal with such a situation?
A: You should first take this matter up directly with your colleague. If your colleague does not resolve
the problem, then you should report the problem to The Cyber AB. Section 4.1 Response for Potential
Violations, Item 1 recommends, "When observing colleagues making choices that are in violation of the
CoPC, you should privately request clarification or offer help rectify the violation. However, if clarification
or help is not effective to resolve the issue, or if you believe corrective action is required to resolve the
situation, then it is expected that you will submit a report."
9.
How would you handle this suspected violation with your co-worker? You
notice that your co-worker, Todd, has been meeting with the OSC, after
business hours each day of the assessment. Todd is in charge of reviewing
the policies, processes, and procedures being used as evidence for some of
the Assessment Objectives, and you noticed that all have been updated in the
evenings. You have a feeling Todd has been working with the OSC to update
their policies, processes, and procedures.
A: The first step you should take is to casually approach Todd and say "Hey, Todd, I noticed the OSC's
documentation has been updated every night, and they look way different than what I saw during the
day. What do you think about that?" Now, if you have witnessed Todd being cut a check by the OSC
that was most decidedly not for the assessment that is taking place, and Todd has a temper, it is not
recommended that you reach out to Todd regarding the check or his activities after work.
ACTIVITY 5-1: Using the CMMC Scoping Guidance
2.
Based on the scenario above, which Scoping Guidance document (Level 1 or
Level 2) would be applicable?
A: Level 2, because CUI (biometric data and payment information) is being processed and stored for the
U.S. Government.
3.
Which CMMC Asset Categories would likely apply?
A: CUI and Specialized Assets
4.
Would the cloud-based environment be considered in/out of scope? Why?
A: This is a tricky question and is intended to encourage students to follow the information to determine
the answer. We know that biometric and payment information is hosted on GovCloud *and* that this
data are likely CUI, which means that the environment would be "in scope". Access to the GovCloud is
managed by the USG, however, tenants are responsible for assuring security controls are implemented
in all systems and access points from the device TO the cloud. There are implications in this: the
devices and access to the devices are controlled, that accounts accessing the data are in fact
authorized, and that they still have a business need for such data. Some Assessment Objectives (e.g.,
"AC.L2-3.1.3 – CONTROL CUI FLOW") may be a shared responsibility. OSCs are encouraged to
develop a shared responsibility matrix delineating roles and responsibilities related to the Assessment
Objectives.
5.
What assets are in scope for a Level 1 self-assessment?
A: Assets that process, store, or transmit FCI are included in the self-assessment scope.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 527
Solutions

--- PAGE 540 ---
6.
Based on the broad definition of FCI Assets, which of the following
statements best describes the self-assessment scope?
○
The self-assessment scope must include everything in the OSC's environment.
○
The self-assessment scope must include all government property.
◉
The self-assessment scope will probably include everything in the OSC's
environment.
○
The self-assessment scope will include only Specialized Assets.
7.
Describe the four types of assets and provide examples of each.
A: People refers to personnel, such as employees, contractors, vendors, and external
service providers. Technology refers to the systems involved in processing, storing, and
transmitting FCI and CUI, such as servers, network appliances, database systems, and
mobile devices. Facilities refers to the physical locations, such as office buildings, server
rooms, datacenters, and secured rooms. External Service Providers (ESPs) refers to the
people, technology, or facilities provided by an organization that is outside of the OSC.
8.
Comparing the two scoping documents, what are some differences
between the self-assessment scope and the Level 2 Assessment
Scope?
A: The Level 2 Assessment Scope document has more detailed information because
there are more assets included at the higher level. You will find descriptions of the five
Asset Categories and the requirements that contractors must satisfy for each Asset
Category. Also included at Level 2 is guidance on defining the CMMC Assessment
Scope, separation techniques, and External Service Provider considerations.
9.
Similar to FCI Assets, CUI Assets are defined as assets that
process, store, or transmit CUI. What are the additional
requirements that an OSC must satisfy when dealing with CUI
Assets?
A: The contractor, or OSC, must document CUI Assets in an asset inventory, in the SSP,
and provide a network diagram of the Assessment Scope. This additional CUI Asset
information will be used during scoping discussions in the pre-assessment process.

Contractor Risk Managed Assets
☐
CUI Assets
☑
Specialized Assets

Which Level 2 assets are assessed against all applicable CMMC
practices? (Choose two.)
☑
CUI Assets
☑
Security Protection Assets
☐
Contractor Risk Managed Assets
☐
Specialized Assets

ACTIVITY 5-2: Taking Inventory of the OSC Environment
4.
According to the inventory, is the Gusto HR System accessible to the public?
◉
Yes
○
No
5.
In cell E3, why do you suppose Dave Farragut wrote "N/A" regarding Gusto's
virtual machine capabilities and simply "Gusto" regarding the Datacenter,
Subnet/VLAN, and Responsible Technical Point of Contact?
A: Since Gusto is a Software-as-a-Service (SaaS) offering, the OSC probably does not know that level
of detail, and probably doesn't need to know it, since they don't directly control it. For now, it will suffice
to leave “N/A” in the Virtual Machine(s) field and "Gusto" for the other columns, since they are Gusto's
responsibility.
6.
In regard to the question in column I, the system owner is the individual or
role within the organization that asked for the system to be stood up and is
also typically the primary user of the system. Who should you list as the
System Owner for the HR System?
A: You don’t have enough information. It seems from the information you've been given that it is
probably Chris, but you need to go back to the OSC to gather that information. You should also
encourage the OSC to assign different technical and system owners. While not required, and
sometimes unavoidable, this gives better visibility and accountability across the organization.
ACTIVITY 5-3: Taking Inventory of IaaS Assets
3.
When it comes to scoping, how is the development environment significantly
different from Gusto, Office 365, and QuickBooks?
A: The difference is the (expected) presence/absence of FCI and CUI. We know from the fact pattern
that there is FCI and CUI in the dev environment. Gusto "just" has HR information in it, which shouldn't
have FCI or CUI in it. In the fact pattern, we looked at the information in QuickBooks and determined
that there wasn't any FCI or CUI in there, either. That's why they are out of scope. Office 365 and Dev,
though, have FCI and CUI, so they are in scope. If you want to wade into the waters of inheritance, you
could contrast the controls that Office 365 will inherit versus those the dev environment will inherit. The
Office 365 environment is likely to inherit more of the certification than the AWS environment due to the
OSC's ability to deviate from/change those controls.
4.
The datacenter is physically located in the U.S. Is this relevant to the security
of government-owned information?
A: To some extent, the location of the datacenter is relevant. However, the issue isn't just the physical
location of the resources, it also is who has administrative control over the resources. In this case,
although it is in the US-East region, the administrators could be in the U.S., Canada, China, India, etc.
Ironically, this isn't really an issue under CMMC except when it comes to export-controlled information.
Systems containing export-controlled information must only be administered by U.S. citizens (no non-
U.S. citizens, including green card holders).
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 529
Solutions

--- PAGE 542 ---
5.
AWS and Office 365 are both cloud-based external service
providers that are in scope because they process, store, or
transmit FCI. What does that mean for Rudison as they move
forward in their level 1 self-assessment?
A: Rudison needs to build shared responsibility matrices for both companies that include
all the assessment objectives for the Level 1 practices so that they are clear on what the
ESPs are responsible for and what they are responsible for.
ACTIVITY 5-5: Identifying Where FCI Is Likely to Be
Found
1.
Is FCI likely to be on the WordPress site?
A: Our analysis did not determine that the WordPress site would be used to create or
receive any FCI. Let’s now validate this by looking at it from a systems perspective. The
WordPress site is a marketing site. It talks about Rudison and its capabilities. If it isn’t
used to send or receive files or other information, is it likely to contain FCI? Probably not.
So, we can feel pretty confident that the WordPress site can be excluded from the
Assessment scope boundary.
2.
What about QuickBooks?
A: Our analysis did not identify any FCI that would be stored in QuickBooks. Let’s look at
it from a systems perspective to confirm. In some cases, shipping information, employee
names and contact information, or other information that would go into an accounting
system might be nonpublic and, therefore, would need to be treated as FCI. So, did we
miss something? It depends on the nature of the data that will be received. It is good to
flag issues such as this as needing additional discussions with the OSC. For our
purposes, we will assume that Rudison has been told that the contract information will
only be public information (e.g., only the information in Beta.SAM.gov) and, therefore, is
not FCI. That means that QuickBooks is not in scope of an eventual CMMC Assessment.
3.
What about Gusto?
A: Our analysis did not identify any FCI that would be stored in Gusto. Let’s look at it from
a systems perspective to confirm. Gusto provides HR and payroll services. Is HR or
payroll information likely to contain FCI? Probably not. Payroll and HR information is
proprietary to Rudison, but it probably won’t contain FCI. In some cases, such as where a
services company is working for the government on a time and materials basis, the notes
that accompany each employee’s billing records may contain FCI. Again, this is
something that you will need to confirm on a case-by-case basis with the OSC. For our
purposes, we will assume that Gusto does not contain any FCI.
4.
How about the AWS environment?
A: Our analysis indicated that the AWS development environment would be used to
create FCI. Looking at this from a systems perspective, we know that the FCI software
will be developed in that environment. So yes, it will be in the Assessment scope.
5.
Will the Office 365 environment contain FCI?
A: Our analysis showed that, at a minimum, the SharePoint site, emails, and OneDrive
would contain FCI. Looking at this from a systems perspective, we know that the Office
365 environment is used to communicate with the government. Therefore, we can be
pretty confident that the Office 365 environment will contain FCI. That means the Office
365 environment will be within the Assessment scope boundary.
