Solutions

--- PAGE 543 ---
6.
Are there any other locations where you might find FCI?
A: Yes. You also need to look at end-user devices.
ACTIVITY 5-6: Evaluating FCI and End-User Devices
1.
Could these end-user devices potentially be removed from the scope of the
Assessment if the Rudison users worked in a fully VDI (virtual desktop
infrastructure) environment?
A: Potentially, yes. You would need to investigate how the VDI worked, but if the FCI is only accessed
via the end-user device and never actually exists on the device, then the end-user devices could be
moved outside the Assessment scope boundary. If Rudison did employ a fully VDI environment, it would
be up to them to demonstrate to an assessor that the end-user devices should be out of scope. If you
were a CCP helping Rudison to prepare their environment for Assessment, you should help them
understand the advantages and disadvantages these different architecture options pose for the
company.
2.
Like most people today, the Rudison employees all have mobile phones, and
they access their Rudison emails from those phones. Will these mobile
devices be within scope for Certification and/or Assessment?
A: You have already established that the emails need to be treated as FCI. Therefore, the mobile
devices will also be in both the Certification and Assessment scope.
3.
The Rudison employees all work from home, and their home networks are
very simple. They all use a Wi-Fi router supplied by their Internet providers to
access the Internet and the Rudison shared resources. Are these routers and
home networks in scope?
A: The routers, and the networks they create, are also in scope since they are used to create, store, or
transfer FCI.
4.
What about the other devices on their home networks, such as smart phones
owned by other family members?
A: It depends. If the network uses an appropriate separation technique, such as VLANS or separate
subnetworks with routing controls, then the other devices are likely to be out scope for both the
Assessment and Certification, assuming they do not have access to the FCI. If separation techniques
are not used, those devices are likely to still be considered in the Assessment scope but outside the
Certification scope, even though they do not themselves contain any FCI.
ACTIVITY 5-7: Confirming the Systems in Scope
1.
Why is the website out of scope?
A: There is no FCI on the website, so we can assume it is out of scope for a CMMC Assessment. You
should document the separation techniques that ensure FCI will not end up on the site. For example: a)
Limit ability to publish content on the site to a single individual; b) Require review and approval of all
posted content required by another individual.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 531
Solutions

--- PAGE 544 ---
2.
Why is QuickBooks out of scope?
A: QuickBooks is out of scope because it should never contain FCI. You need to
document the information that will be contained in QuickBooks, so you can demonstrate
to the Assessment Team why QuickBooks should not be in scope for Assessment. This
can be tricky because we don’t need to make changes to QuickBooks or other overt
actions for us to suddenly wind up storing FCI. Instead, it can happen simply by using the
software as intended. Rudison should be able to demonstrate that they have a practice in
place for keeping FCI out of QuickBooks. Rudison should also periodically review the
information it stores in QuickBooks to ensure FCI is not stored in it. Alternatively, Rudison
could assume QuickBooks is within scope and include it in its CMMC-related efforts.
3.
Why is Gusto out of scope?
A: HR and benefits systems typically do not include FCI. Of course, you should
investigate to be certain that this Gusto instance does not contain FCI. Document the
data contained in Gusto so Rudison can later demonstrate why Gusto should be out of
both the Assessment and Certification scopes.
4.
Gusto and QuickBooks are integrated, enabling them to share
data. How does this affect scope?
A: Integration is something you need to pay careful attention to when you determine
scope, as it provides a way for sensitive information to move quickly and possibly without
anyone noticing. In this example, since QuickBooks does not contain any FCI and no FCI
is placed directly into Gusto, Gusto inherently cannot contain or have access to FCI.
ACTIVITY 5-8: Using Separation Techniques to Limit
Scope
1.
It is intended that no CUI will be stored or processed in the
systems located on the manufacturing floor. Is the manufacturing
floor, therefore, out of scope?
A: No. The NAS being in a separate, access-controlled room helps to limit the physical
scope of the Assessment. However, the fact that the NAS, which contains CUI, is on a
network that is not physically or logically isolated from the manufacturing floor’s network
means the manufacturing floor’s network must also be considered in scope. Furthermore,
while the physical barrier provided by the door with a cipher lock is a good step, CUI will
not be completely protected from unauthorized viewing if there are windows throughout
the office's outer walls.
2.
How can you increase the likelihood that the manufacturing floor
will be considered out of scope by the assessor?
A: To prevent access of the CUI from outside the engineering office, you should segment
the network so the engineering office and manufacturing floor are on separate networks,
and limit network traffic so the NAS can only be accessed from within the engineering
office. By isolating the NAS from access over the machines on the manufacturing floor,
you can take the manufacturing floor out of scope. Any possibility of viewing CUI from
outside the office (through windows, for example) must be eliminated.
532 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 545 ---
ACTIVITY 6-1: Fostering a Mature Cybersecurity Culture
1.
What is the relationship between a policy and a procedure?
A: A policy describes the overall characteristics of a required activity. (For example, the purpose, who
needs to follow the policy, and the roles involved in the implementation and the responsibilities of each.)
A policy is focused on the "why" of an organizational action. Procedures explain the "how," providing the
detailed steps necessary to implement a policy on a day-to-day basis.
2.
Why do you need a plan as part of governance?
A: Plans are necessary to ensure the ongoing monitoring of the activities mandated in policies and
procedures. A plan provides the details of the implementation timeline, the budget for resource
management, and required training.
3.
Why is implementing cultural change a critical step in building cybersecurity
maturity?
A: To achieve real institutionalization of cybersecurity in an organization where all employees
understand they play a role in protecting sensitive information, you have to consciously build such a
culture by obtaining executive support, identifying employees views, and implementing regular
cybersecurity training. Without that institutionalization, policies and procedures are just documents.
4.
How does having a mature cybersecurity culture help with a CMMC
Assessment?
A: A CMMC Assessment is more likely to go smoothly when everyone in the OSC understands the
importance of protecting sensitive information and their specific cybersecurity responsibilities. When
cybersecurity is institutionalized and mature, the artifacts an assessor examines will be comprehensive
and clear, the people an assessor interviews will speak knowledgeably about their responsibilities, and
the tests an assessor conducts will demonstrate that the technical controls are correctly and
consistently implemented.
ACTIVITY 6-2: Evaluating Readiness
1.
When you return to your company, how might you start helping them
prepare?
A: Answers will vary, depending on the organization, what they do, the CMMC Level they are seeking,
how mature their practices and processes already are, and how far into the process of preparing for
CMMC Assessment they already are. In general, any organization must start by determining what
CMMC Level they are seeking, as this is necessary to determine the practices and processes that will
be evaluated. Also, the organization needs to determine where sensitive information will exist within
their environment, as this will help them identify the Assessment scope. You can inventory the
company's systems to help determine where sensitive information exists and how it flows through the
organization's business processes. Once you have identified what should be considered "in scope", you
can perform a gap analysis against the Level the organization is targeting.
2.
You've completed a gap analysis for your company, and you believe you have
helped them close all the gaps. At that point, is the OSC ready for
Assessment, or is there something else that must also be done?
A: It depends on what you did as you closed the gaps. If you updated the Evidence as you closed the
gaps, then the OSC may be ready for Assessment. With all the changes you've made, however, you
may need to circle back and verify that all of the Evidence is indeed in place and up to date.

◉

○
Test
○

4.
You are trying to find Evidence to prove that a particular practice
has been put into place at the OSC. How might you come up with
ideas for the type of Evidence you might use?
A: A good place to begin is by interviewing the people at the OSC who have implemented
the practice in question. Also, you can turn to the CMMC documentation for guidance. For
example, the CMMC Assessment Guide not only identifies the practices that must be
evaluated, it also provides tips regarding the security controls and types of Evidence you
might look for. You can refer to the CMMC Assessment Process (CAP) document for
guidelines on evaluating the quality or suitability of the Evidence you find. Of course, your
own experience working with information systems can also be quite helpful in informing
what type of Evidence you should look for.
ACTIVITY 7-2: Using the CMMC Assessment Guides
2.
What is the first CMMC Level 1 practice in the list?
A: AC.L1-3.1.1 is the first practice listed for CMMC Level 1.

3.
Read the description of AC.L1-3.1.1. Does this give you enough information
to determine if this is being performed in a client’s environment?
A: The description is "Limit information system access to authorized users, processes acting on behalf
of authorized users, or devices (including other information systems)." This does not really provide
enough information to determine if this is being performed in the client's environment. The first place to
turn for more guidance is the CMMC Assessment Guides.
6.
Why do you think these terms were identified as being important to Level 1
self-assessment?
A: Asset, component, and information systems are terms critical to defining the scope of the self-
assessment. Monitor and organizationally defined are terms used frequently in the practice descriptions
so it is important to know how they are defined in the context of CMMC.

800-171 Rev 2 requirement 3.1.1 to find additional information to further understand the concepts.

2.
How many domains are involved in a Level 1 Assessment? Where did you
find the answer?
A: There are 6 domains involved in a Level 1 Assessment. You can find this by counting number of
times "Level 1" appears in the List of Practices starting on page 9 of the CMMC Model.
3.
Against how many practices is an OSC evaluated in a Level 1 Assessment?
Where did you find the answer?

CMMC Model.
4.
CMMC Level 1's practice requirements are the equivalent of what other
regulation's safeguarding requirements? Where did you find the answer?
A: Level 1 is equivalent to FAR 52.204-21. This can be found on several pages, including pages 6, 7,
and 9.

ACTIVITY 8-3: Analyzing Technical Cybersecurity
Controls
3.
Based on the ideas listed in this section of the Assessment Guide,
what methods and objects might you use to prove that access to
the development and collaboration environments is limited to only
authorized users?
A: Looking specifically at Rudison, you could demonstrate that the objective is met by
supplying a list of all employees and their roles in the organization. This is illustrated in
Example 1 a bit further down in the Assessment Guide. It is important to remember that
the assessment method and objects listed are suggested, not mandatory, and that
Rudison doesn’t need to demonstrate that they have every assessment method and
object covered. For example, the assessment methods and objects listed under
AC.L1-3.1.1 in the CMMC Level 1 Self-Assessment Guide come directly from NIST SP
800-171A, and they include looking at procedures addressing account management. In
Level 1, procedures can be performed on an undocumented basis, so assessors should
not expect Rudison to have written processes. In your gap analysis and its associated
POA&M, you do not need to worry about creating written practice documentation.
7.
The processes referred to in this objective include, for example,
any processes handling automatic updates of software in the
system. Each such process should be associated with the user
who authorized the process to run and should be tracked. So, what
does this mean?
A: As a practical matter, this is a situation in which a user enters credentials into some
software (i.e., a process) and that software does things on the user's behalf. One easy
example of this is a cloud-based backup system. The user has an account in the cloud-
based backup system, and the agent running on the user's computer (or, to use the
language from the practice, the "process acting on behalf of the user") uses that account
to back up the data from the user's computer to the cloud storage. To meet this objective,
we need to be able to demonstrate that we know all of those processes running in the
Rudison systems.
8.
Based on the information provided, do we know all of the
processes running in the Rudison systems?
A: Not from the information we have been given.
11.
Has Rudison identified the devices and other systems authorized
to connect to the system?
A: Rudison knows that only the workstations and mobile devices belonging to Jen, Chris,
and Mark are authorized to connect to the development or collaboration systems. From
the information we've been given, we can't really tell if they have this information stored
somewhere so that an Assessor can see it. So, for now, Objective C will have to be
marked as"Not Yet Addressed". For the purposes of the gap analysis, we might be done
with the analysis of that objective at this point. However, as a practical matter since we
determined that their home computers and networks are in scope, we will also need to
analyze each user's home network to determine if those networks meet the objective.
Based on the information we have been given, we would again have to go with an
implementation status of "Not Yet Addressed" for this objective.
536 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 549 ---
15.
How would you demonstrate that Rudison's systems limit access to
authorized users?
A: You can take a screen capture of the accounts in the development and collaboration systems, and
show that only users who are defined in the employee list have accounts in those systems. You can
also show that the end-users' mobile devices are locked by a password or other identifier (e.g.,
biometrics) and that the users' computers are set up to require the user to log in (i.e., they do not
automatically log in without a password).
17.
Are either of the underlying objectives for AC.L1-3.1.2 met? If not, what would
be a good plan of action for meeting them?
A: Rudison has only 3 employees and, given the work they do for the company, they all need access to
the FCI that is in the environment. The "Further Discussion" section of this practice states "Limit users
to only the information systems, roles, or applications they are permitted to use and are needed for their
roles and responsibilities [a]. Limit access to applications and data based on the authorized users' role
and responsibilities [b]. Common types of functions a user can be assigned are create, read, update,
and delete." In Rudison's case, the company limits access to the development and collaboration
environments to only those whose roles and responsibilities require access to such environments. On
the home computers, we know that the users must log in to each device, which limits access to any
applications on those devices to only the logged-in user. Rudison, therefore, limits users to only the
information systems, roles, and applications they are permitted to use and are needed for their roles
and responsibilities, so they have successfully implemented the first objective. We also know that Chris
has access to QuickBooks while the others do not. Therefore, we can demonstrate that although all of
the employees have access to both the development and collaboration environments, it is because they
need access, and that Rudison does limit access to applications and data based on the users' roles and
responsibilities, as evidenced by their QuickBooks implementation. We would collect Evidence,
including screen captures of the QuickBooks access controls, as well as the screen captures and other
information used in AC.L1-3.1.1, to demonstrate the successful adoption.
ACTIVITY 8-4: Analyzing Non-Technical Cybersecurity
Controls
3.
For Rudison, do you know which individuals are allowed physical access to
each environment?
A: For their individual homes, that is likely to be each individual. At this point, you may not yet know if
anyone else has access to their home. So, for now we can't mark any of these objectives as “Fully
Implemented” because we need a list of the individuals who are allowed access, and we need to be
able to demonstrate that, at least for the environments containing the FCI, access to those
environments is limited to only the authorized individuals. Remember, too, that we are looking not only
at electronic equipment, such as computers and printers, but also paper copies of documents. Access
to all FCI must be limited to only those with appropriate permissions. For example, we would need to
know who else has keys to their homes. If others, such as a landlord, have keys and can access the
home without their knowledge, we will need to ensure that the environment in which the FCI is stored
(e.g., an office or closet in the home) has additional access controls, such as additional locks, that keep
those other people from accessing the FCI. Once we identify this as a gap, we then turn to closing the
gaps. In this case, we would help Rudison create lists of the different environments, who has access to
them, and how they are protected.
4.
Once we've analyzed their home environments, are we finished with
PE.L1-3.10.1?
A: No, we aren't. Rudison has data in the collaboration and development environments, too. Those are
maintained by Microsoft and Amazon, respectively.
Certified CMMC Professional (CCP) | 537
Solutions

--- PAGE 550 ---
5.
So, how do we demonstrate that those environments have
appropriate physical access controls in place?
A: In some cases, service providers like Amazon and Microsoft will have their
environments CMMC-certified as well. An assessor may accept their certification as
evidence that they are meeting certain practices, like PE.L1-3.10.1. It should be noted,
however, that assessors may not accept certifications, on their own, as evidence for
some practices which are more implementation-specific. In some cases, Rudison will
need to work with the service provider to obtain the necessary Evidence to demonstrate
that the control is met. For our purposes, since neither Amazon nor Microsoft have
environments that are CMMC-certified at this time, and since Rudison does not have
other evidence that the environments meet the physical access control requirements, this
practice would have to remain in a state other than "Fully Implemented" until this
information is obtained.
7.
Does Rudison have the information we need to mark any of these
practices as "Fully Implemented"?
A: We do not have enough information yet. We will have to work with the Rudison team to
find out additional details and write plans of action and milestones to fill any identified
gaps. It is evident that Rudison already has some homework to do before they will be
ready for an Assessment.

ACTIVITY 10-2: Planning for an Assessment
1.
What can an Assessment Team do to foster a positive Assessment
culture?
A: To foster a positive Assessment culture, an Assessment Team should research the
company beforehand so that they can respect its unique context. They should be
respectful, professional, and kind at all times, remembering that they are guests of the
OSC and that they have a partnership with the OSC with the common cause of protecting
the country's military supply chain. They should be patient, asking questions in a different
way if their first requests for information aren't understood.
2.
Does this scenario include the proper initial steps in the
Assessment Process?
A: It is correct for the OSC to contact a C3PAO asking for an Assessment. However, all
Assessments must be led by a Lead Assessor who is a CCA, not a CCP. Another
problem in the scenario is that there should be a lot of planning before a site visit.
3.
Does this scenario include the proper initial steps in the
Assessment Process?
A: Yes. It is crucial to confirm that the OSC is truly ready for an Assessment, by validating
the scope boundary and doing a Certification Assessment Readiness Review (CA-RR).
4.
Is this an acceptable action during a Certification Assessment
Readiness Review?
A: No, it is not acceptable. During a CA-RR that is conducted as part of the preparation
for an Assessment, a CCP or CCA can point out that there is missing Evidence, but they
can't make a specific suggestion on how to fix the issue.
540 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Solutions

--- PAGE 553 ---
ACTIVITY 10-3: Participating on an Assessment Team
1.
Does this scenario include the proper steps for an on-site visit?
A: No. In addition to these two sessions, it is recommended that the Lead Assessor conducts daily
checkpoints to keep the Assessment on track.
2.
Based on this scenario, what are your actions?
A: As an Assessment Team Member, you must ensure that your actions support effective Assessment
characteristics (objective, fair, consistent, reliable), that your review of the Evidence is consistent with
the CAP, and that you adhere to the CoPC at all times. In this scenario, the information should be
shared with the Assessment Team to support effective practice characterization.
ACTIVITY 10-4: Reporting the Assessment Results
1.
Does this scenario include the proper final steps in the Assessment Process?
A: Yes. The scenario includes all the necessary components of the Assessment Results Package and
the destruction/archival of the artifacts. It also includes details about the fact that the Assessment Team
notes and Final Assessment Results Package must be maintained by the Lead Assessor for a period of
three years.
2.
Following the Assessment, another CCP asks if he/she may maintain a copy
of the findings as "they may be useful for future Assessments." Is this
permissible?
A: No; The Final Assessment Results Package is an artifact of the Assessment and is proprietary to the
OSC. As such, it can not be removed.
ACTIVITY 10-5: Defining the POA&M Close-Out Process
1.
Based on this scenario, which CMMC practice would apply?
A: AC.L2-3.1.9 Provide privacy and security notices consistent with applicable CUI rules.
2.
Would the practice be Met or Not Met?
A: Not Met.
3.
Provided that this is the only practice determined to be Not Met, could a
POA&M be employed? If so, what steps would be taken to validate:
A: Yes. Validation would be conducted by the Lead Assessor with input from the Assessment Team,
CCP, and/or OSC and should minimally include: a) The specific security weakness revealed in the
Assessment and tied to a specific practice, b) The severity of the weakness, c) The scope of each
weakness with the assessed environment. d) The proposed mitigation approaches. e) The estimated
costs for remediation. f) Documented records of mitigation status and delays, and g) A risk assessment
of the deficiency.
4.
As a CCP, what additional support might you provide?
A: As a CCP, you may be asked to support the review of new evidence and the Close-Out Assessment
of authorized POA&M items.

Glossary
acquisitions
The acquiring by contract with
appropriated funds of supplies or services
(including construction) by and for the use
of the Federal Government through
purchase or lease, whether the supplies or
services are already in existence or must be
created, developed, demonstrated, and
evaluated. Source: 48 CFR § 2.101.
artifacts
Tangible and reviewable records that are
the direct outcome of a practice or process
being performed by a system, person or
persons performing a role in that practice,
control, or process. Artifacts can be a
printed hard copy, soft- or electronic copy
of a document or file or embedded in a
system or software but must be a result or
output from performing a process in the
OSC. Source: CMMC Assessment Process.
assessment boundary
Identifies all assets in the contractor's
environment for the assessment
engagement. Assets within the assessment
boundary can be part of the CMMC
Certification Boundary or Enabling Assets.
Assessment Objective
(AO). Identifies the specific set of
objectives that must be met to receive
MET for the practice as defined in NIST
SP 800-171A. Source: CMMC Self-
Assessment Guide Level 1.
Assessment Team Member
Individuals performing the Assessment
and working under the leadership of a
CCA. The team members can include
CCPs, CCAs, as well as other non-certified
individuals such as technical writers and
project managers.
asset
Anything that has value to an organization,
including, but not limited to, another
organization, person, computing device,
information technology (IT) system, IT
network, IT circuit, software (both an
installed instance and a physical instance),
virtual computing platform (common in
cloud and virtualized computing), and
related hardware (e.g., locks, cabinets,
keyboards). Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms (NISTIR 7693, NISTIR 7694).
awareness
A learning process that sets the stage for
training by changing individual and
organizational attitudes to realize the
importance of security and the adverse
consequences of its failure. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-16).
awareness and training program
Explains proper rules of behavior for the
use of agency information systems and
information. The program communicates
information technology (IT) security
policies and procedures that need to be
followed. (i.e., NSTISSD 501, NIST SP
800-50). Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms (CNSSI 4009).

--- PAGE 556 ---
C3PAO
(CMMC Third-Party Assessment
Organization). An authorized and
independent conformity-Assessment body
that contracts with the OSC to conduct
CMMC Assessments and issues the
CMMC Certification.
CA-RR
(Certification Assessment Readiness
Review). An evaluation that determines the
OSC's readiness and reasonableness to
process with the on-site Assessment.
CAP
(CMMC Assessment Process).
CCA
(Certified CMMC Assessor). Individuals
with background, training, and
examination requirements outlined by The
Cyber AB trained to conduct Assessments
up to a specific CMMC Level.
CCI
(Certified CMMC Instructor). Individuals
who are authorized to train CCPs and
CCAs.
CCMI
(Certified CMMC Master Instructor).
Individuals who train the instructors who
want to become CCIs.
CCP
(Certified CMMC Professional).
Individuals credentialed as understanding
the requirements of CMMC for a DoD
supplier.
CDI
(Covered Defense Information). Term
used to identify information that requires
protection under DFARS Clause
252.204-7012. Unclassified CTI or other
information, as described in the CUI
Registry, that requires safeguarding or
dissemination controls pursuant to and
consistent with the law, regulations, and
government-wide policies. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (DFARS Clause
252.204-7012).
CMMC
(Cybersecurity Maturity Model
Certification).
CMMC Certification Boundary
Defines the assets to which an assessor will
evaluate conformity with applicable
CMMC practices. This is the boundary to
which a CMMC certificate will be applied.
CMMC Marketplace
Centralized access point for Organizations
Seeking Certification (OSCs) or anyone
else who wants to find organizations and
individuals in the CMMC ecosystem.
CMMC Standard
A framework that combines various
cybersecurity standards and best practices
and maps those controls and processes
across several maturity levels that range
from basic to advanced cyber hygiene, and
that, when implemented, will reduce risk
against a specific set of cyber threats.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms.
confidentiality
Preserving authorized restrictions on
information access and disclosure,
including means for protecting personal
privacy and proprietary information.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms
(44 U.S. Code Sec 3542).
configuration management
A collection of activities focused on
establishing and maintaining the integrity
of information technology products and
systems, through control of processes for
initializing, changing, and monitoring the
configurations of those products and
systems throughout the system
development life cycle. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-53
Rev 5).
conflict of interest
A situation where an individual has
competing obligations that might lead to
compromises in decision-making and
damage to the integrity of the outcome.
544 | Certified CMMC Professional (CCP)
Glossary

--- PAGE 557 ---
contractor
Any individual, firm, corporation, partnership,
association, or other legal non-Federal entity
that enters into a contract directly with the
DoD to furnish services, supplies, or
construction. Also referred to as a Defense
contractor or Prime contractor. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (32 CFR
158.3).
CoPC
(Code of Professional Conduct). A document
signed by all individuals in the CMMC
ecosystem that defines the expectations,
responsibilities, and guidelines for performing
work on behalf of the DoD and The Cyber
AB.
COTS
(Commercial Off-the-Shelf). Packaged
software and hardware solutions that already
exist and are available for purchase by the
public.
CQAP
(CMMC Quality Assurance Professional).
Individuals who are responsible for ensuring
the completeness and accuracy of the
Assessment documentation.
CTI
(Controlled Technical Information). Technical
information with military or space application
that is subject to controls on the access, use,
reproduction, modification, performance,
display, release, disclosure, or dissemination.
Source: DFARS Clause 252.204-7012.
CUI
(Controlled Unclassified Information).
Information that requires safeguarding or
dissemination controls pursuant to and
consistent with law, regulations, and
government-wide policies, excluding
information that is classified under Executive
Order 13526, Classified National Security
Information, December 29, 2009, or any
predecessor or successor order, or the Atomic
Energy Act of 1954, as amended. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (E.O. 13556
(adapted)).
cybersecurity
Prevention of damage to, protection of, and
restoration of computers, electronic
communications systems, electronic
communications services, wire communication,
and electronic communication, including
information contained therein, to ensure its
availability, integrity, authentication,
confidentiality, and nonrepudiation. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NSPD-54/
HSPD-23).
data integrity
Property that data has not been altered in an
unauthorized manner. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-33).
decontrolling
What occurs when an authorized holder,
consistent with CUI regulations and the CUI
Registry, removes safeguarding and
dissemination controls from CUI that no
longer requires such controls. Sources: 32 CFR
2002.18 and NARA CUI Registry.
DFARS
(Defense Federal Acquisition Regulation
Supplement). A supplement of the FAR that
includes policies and procedures that apply
only to acquisition activities by the Department
of Defense.
DFARS 252.204-7012
Safeguarding Covered Defense Information
and Cyber Incident Reporting.
DIB
(Defense Industrial Base). The worldwide
industrial complex that enables research and
development, as well as design, production,
delivery, and maintenance of military weapons
systems, subsystems, and components or parts,
to meet U.S. military requirements. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (DIB Sector-
Specific Plan, DHS CISA).
dissemination control
Method of managing sensitive information
distribution so that it doesn't spread more
widely than allowed by law, regulation, or
government-wide policy.

Glossary

--- PAGE 558 ---
DMZ
(demilitarized zone). A small section of a
private network that is located between two
firewalls and made available for public access.
DoD
(Department of Defense). Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms.
Domain
A grouping of like practices based on the 14
control families set forth in NIST SP 800-171.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms.
ECI
(Export-Controlled Information). Any
information or material that cannot be released
to foreign nationals or representatives of a
foreign entity, without first obtaining approval
or license from the Dept. of State for items
controlled by ITAR or the Dept. of Commerce
for items controlled by the Export
Administration Regulations (EAR).
eMASS
(Enterprise Mission Assurance Support
Service). A web-based U.S. Department of
Defense off-the-shelf solution that automates a
broad range of services for cybersecurity
management. CMMC eMASS serves as the
system of record for CMMC Assessment data
and reporting.
Enabling Asset
An asset that provides security functions or
capabilities to the contractor's CMMC
Certification Boundary to support the
implementation of CMMC practices. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms.
enclave
A segmentation of an organization's network
or data that is intended to "wall off" that
network or database from all other networks or
systems. An Assessment can be conducted
within the boundaries of an enclave.
encryption
The process of changing plaintext into cipher
text. Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms
(NISTIR 7621 Rev 1, CNSSI 4009).
Evidence
The body of data, or "proof," that an
organization either meets or does not meet the
objectives of the domain, control, or processes.
Evidence Validation
An evaluation that examines sufficiency of
Evidence presented by the OSC, ensuring it
meets the intent and objectives of the control
or practice.
examine
The process of reviewing, inspecting,
observing, studying, or analyzing assessment
objects (i.e., specifications, mechanisms,
activities). Source: CMMC Assessment Guide -
Level 2.
FAR
(Federal Acquisition Regulations). Chapter 1 of
Title 48 of the Code of Federal Regulations.
Provides uniform policies and procedures that
apply to all executive agencies regarding
acquisitions.
FAR 52.204-21
48 CFR § 52.204-21 - Basic Safeguarding of
Covered Contractor Information Systems. Also
called FAR 52.
FCI
(Federal Contract Information). Federal
contract information means information, not
intended for public release, that is provided by
or generated for the Government under a
contract to develop or deliver a product or
service to the Government, but not including
information provided by the Government to
the public (such as on public websites) or
simple transactional information, such as
necessary to process payments. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (48 CFR § 52.204-21).
FedRAMP
(Federal Risk and Authorization Management
Program).
FISMA
(Federal Information Security Management
Act).

Gap Analysis
An evaluation that examines the organization's
processes "as performed" to identify issues,
impediments, and potential risks to sustained
implementation.
governance
The policies, processes, and controls that are
utilized by the OSC to assure sustainment and
continual improvement with respect to
detection, prevention, and response to cyber
incidents.
headquarters (HQ) organization
The legal entity that will be delivering services
or products under the terms of a DoD
contract. Source: CMMC Assessment Process,
version 5.4.
host unit
The specific people, procedures, and
technology within an HQ Organization that
would be applied to the DoD contract and that
are to be considered as the OSC for CMMC

Assessment Process, version 5.4.
information flow
The flow of information or connectivity from
one location to another. This can be related to
data as well as connectivity from one system to
another, or from one security domain to
another. The authorization granting permission
for information flow comes from a control
authority granting permission to an entity,
asset, role, or group. Source: CMMC Glossary
and Acronyms.
institutionalization
The action of establishing something as a
convention or norm in an organization or
culture. Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms (Oxford Dictionary).
interview
The process of holding discussions with
individuals or groups of individuals to facilitate
understanding, achieve clarification, or obtain
evidence. Source: CMMC Assessment Guide -
Level 2.
IP
(Intellectual Property). A work or invention,
such as a design, process, computer program,
or trade secret developed through work of the
mind or intellect, which may be protected
through legal mechanisms such as patent,
copyright, and trademark, or by taking steps to
keep the information secret.
ISO/IEC
The International Organization for
Standardization (ISO) is an independent, non-
governmental international organization of
national standards bodies. The International
Electrotechnical Commission (IEC) is a global
organization for the preparation and
publication of international standards for all
electrical, electronic, and related technologies.
Together the ISO/IEC publish international
standards.
ISOO
(Information Security Oversight Office). Part
of NARA responsible to the President for
policy and oversight of the Government-wide
security classification system and the National
Industrial Security Program. Authority on
protection of CUI.
lawful government purpose
Any activity, mission, function, operation, or
endeavor that the U.S. Government authorizes
or recognizes as within the scope of its legal
authorities or the legal authorities of non-
executive branch entities (such as state and
local law enforcement). Source: 32 CFR
2002.4.
Lead Assessor
The CCA who overseas and manages the
Assessment Team during a CMMC
Assessment for a particular OSC.
LPP
(Licensed Partner Publisher). Organization
authorized to publish CMMC training
curricula.
LTP
(Licensed Training Provider). Organization
authorized to train registered or certified
CMMC individuals.

Maturity Model
A model that assesses how institutionalized
critical practices and processes are in an
organization and helps determine what
capabilities they need in order to continue to
improve their performance.
media sanitization
The actions taken to render data written on
media unrecoverable by both ordinary and
extraordinary means. Source: CMMC Glossary
and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-88 Rev 1).
MFA
(multifactor authentication). A mechanism that
provides for added protection of data through
electronic methods.
NARA
(National Archives and Records
Administration).
NDA
Non-disclosure agreement. A contract by
which one or more parties agree not to disclose
confidential information that they have shared
with each other as a necessary part of doing
business together.
network segmentation
The use of physical devices such as firewalls or
logical separation such as subnetting to create
distinct segments in your internal network.
NIST
(National Institutes of Standards and
Technology).
NIST SP 800-171
Protecting Controlled Unclassified Information
in Nonfederal Systems and Organizations.

Assessing Security Requirements For
Controlled Unclassified Information.

Enhanced Security Requirements for
Protecting Controlled Unclassified
Information: A Supplement to NIST SP
800-171.
NIST SP 800-53
Security and Privacy Controls for Information
Systems and Organizations.
non-duplication
A planning step that allows consideration of
commonalities between cyber security
certifications and/or relevant industry
standards when planning a CMMC Assessment
to minimize duplication of effort.
observation
A real-time demonstration or review of a test,
system, tool, software, hardware, practice,
control, or process being performed and
witnessed first-hand by the CCA and, if
applicable, the Assessment Team. Source:
CMMC Assessment Process.
OSC
(Organization Seeking Certification).
Organization going through the CMMC
Assessment process to receive a level of
certification for a given environment.
OSC Assessment Official
The most senior representative of an
Organization Seeking Certification who is
directly and actively responsible for leading and
managing the OSC's engagement in the
Assessment and who possesses decision-
making authority for the OSC with regard to
the CMMC Assessment. Source: CMMC
Assessment Process (CAP). The Assessment
Official must be an employee of the
organization that is being assessed.
OUSD (A&S)
(Office of the Under Secretary of Defense for
Acquisition and Sustainment). DoD office that
procures what is necessary for national
defense. OUSD (A&S) is also the owner of the
CMMC Model and Assessment Guides.
PA
(Provisional Assessor). Individuals who
possess the background, training, and
examination requirements as outlined by the
CMMC-AB (before it was branded The Cyber
AB). PAs were provisionally trained to conduct
Level 2 Assessments during the interim period.

PAs are required to pass the CCP and CCA
exams when they are officially available.
PI
(Provisional Instructor). Individuals who
possess the background, training, and

Cyber AB and were provisionally trained to be
instructors. PIs are required to pass the CCI
exam when it's officially available.
PII
(Personally Identifiable Information).
Information that can be used to distinguish or
trace an individual's identity, either alone or
when combined with other information that is
linked or linkable to a specific individual.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NIST
SP 800-33).
plan
An artifact or collection of artifacts that
provides oversight for implementing defined
CMMC policies. Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms.
POA&M
(Plan of Action and Milestones). A document
that identifies tasks that need to be done that
details resources to accomplish the plan and
the timeline for completion.
PoC
(Point-of-Contact). The individual who
provides daily coordination and liaison support
between the OSC and the Assessment Team.
The PoC does not necessarily have to be an
employee of the organization that is being
assessed. They could be a contractor,
consultant, or advisor like a CMMC Registered
Practitioner (RP).
policy
An artifact or collection of artifacts that
establishes governance over the
implementation of CMMC practices and
activities. Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms.
Practice
An activity or set of activities that are
performed to meet the defined CMMC
objectives. Source: CMMC Glossary and
Acronyms.
procedure
The documented details for how an activity is
implemented to achieve a desired outcome. A
procedure should provide enough detail for a
trained individual to perform the activity.
Source: CMMC Glossary and Acronyms.
regulated information
A generic term for CUI, FCI, ECI, and CTI.
Also referred to as protected information.
remediation
Intended to address situations where the initial
Assessment's target Maturity Level score was
not achieved, but only by a narrow margin.
Source: CMMC Assessment Process.
risk
A measure of the extent to which an entity is
threatened by a potential circumstance or
event, and typically a function of: 1) the
adverse impacts that would arise if the
circumstance or event occurs, and 2) the
likelihood of occurrence. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (FIPS 200 (adapted)).
RMF
(Risk Management Framework).
RP
(Registered Practitioner). Professionals who
provide consultative services that include non-
certified CMMC advice. RPs are not involved
in the formal CMMC Assessment process.
RPO
(Registered Practitioner Organization).
Organizations familiar with the basic
constructs of the CMMC Standard.
scope
The scale or extent of what will be evaluated
for conformity, which includes those assets
(people, facilities, technology) within the OSC's
environment that are targeted for CMMC
Assessment because they interact with sensitive
information—for example, by containing it,
touching it in transit, or operating on the same
network as it.

scoping
The process of setting or determining the
scope.
Security Control Inheritance
A situation in which an information system or
application receives protection from security
controls (or portions of security controls) that
are developed, implemented, assessed,
authorized, and monitored by entities other
than those responsible for the system or
application; entities either internal or external
to the organization where the system or
application resides. Source: Security and
Privacy Controls for Federal Information
Systems and Organizations (NIST SP 800-53
Rev. 4).
self-assessment
Assessing your organization's compliance to
the practice requirements.
self-attestation
Making an official declaration that something
complies with regulations without independent
substantiating evidence.
sensitive information
Information where the loss, misuse, or
unauthorized access or modification could
adversely affect the national interest or the
conduct of federal programs, or the privacy to
which individuals are entitled under 5 U.S.C.
Section 552a (the Privacy Act). Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (NIST SP 800-53 Rev
4 (adapted)).
shared responsibility matrix
A mechanism that identifies the person(s) or
team(s) in the OSC or the ESP responsible for
the implementation and sustainment of the
technical controls, as reflected in the terms of
service between the ESP as provider and the
OSC as customer.
SPRS
(Supplier Performance Risk System). The
database operated by the Defense Information
Systems Agency (DISA) for organizations to
report their self-assessment cybersecurity
scores. SPRS provides an interim solution as
the DoD phases in the CMMC Assessment
system.
SSP
(System Security Plan). The formal document
prepared by the information system owner (or
common security controls owner for inherited
controls) that provides an overview of the
security requirements for the system and
describes the security controls in place or
planned for meeting those requirements. The
plan can also contain as supporting appendices
or as references, other key security-related
documents such as a risk assessment, privacy
impact assessment, system interconnection
agreements, contingency plan, security
configurations, configuration management
plan, and incident response plan. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (CNSSI
4009).
stakeholder
A person with an interest or concern in the
success of a business. Interviews with them
provide an excellent opportunity to gain insight
and establish context.
standards
A document, established by consensus and
approved by a recognized body, that provides
for common and repeated use, rules, guidelines
or characteristics for activities or their results,
aimed at the achievement of the optimum
degree of order in a given context. Source:
CMMC Glossary and Acronyms (NISTIR 8074
Vol. 2).
subcontractor
A person or company hired by the Prime
contractor to perform a specific task as part of
an overall project.
supplier
A person or organization that provides
something needed such as a product or service.
supply chain
A system of organizations, people, activities,
information, and resources, possibly
international in scope, that provides products
or services to consumers. Source: CMMC
Glossary and Acronyms (CNSSI 4009).

--- PAGE 563 ---
supporting organization/unit
The people, procedures, and technology
external to the HQ Organization that support

system
A discrete set of information resources

test
The process of exercising Assessment objects

behavior. Source: CMMC Assessment Guide -
Level 2.
The Cyber AB
Non-profit organization that oversees the

threat
Any circumstance or event with the potential
to adversely impact organizational operations
(including mission, functions, image, or
reputation), organizational assets, individuals,
other organizations, or the Nation through an
information system via unauthorized access,
destruction, disclosure, modification of
information, and/or denial of service. Source:

800-30 Rev 1).
